Mishneh Torah, Sacrifices Rendered Unfit



Chapter 1

All persons disqualified from performing sacrificial service<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">A non-priest or a priest who was disqualified for various reasons. See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 9:15 for a detailed list of such individuals.</i> may slaughter sacrificial animals, even sacrifices of the most sacred order, as an initial preference,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The wording of <i>Zevachim</i> 3:1 (the source for this ruling) could be interpreted as implying that the slaughter is acceptable only after the fact. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (based on <i>Zevachim</i> 31b), the Rambam explains that this restriction applies only to a person who is ritually impure.</i> with the exception of a person who is ritually impure, who may not slaughter as an initial preference. Even though he stands outside the Temple Courtyard<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">And thus does not violate the prohibition against entering the Temple Courtyard while ritually impure (see <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 3:6).</i> and inserts his hands and slaughters in the Courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Where the sacrificial animals must be slaughtered. As stated in <i>ibid.</i> 3:18, it is forbidden for an impure person to insert his hand into the Temple Courtyard according to Rabbinic Law. Nevertheless, this person was willing to transgress. Significantly, in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>) the Rambam offers an interpretation that does not require that the person transgress: he slaughtered with a long knife.</i> [he was restricted]. This a decree, lest he touch the [sacrificial] meat.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance he would make the sacrifice impure and disqualify it. That is forbidden.</i>
If [an impure person] transgressed and slaughtered [a sacrificial animal], the sacrifice is acceptable. Similarly, with regard to the bull [brought by] the High Priest on Yom Kippur even though [Leviticus 16:11] states: "And Aaron... shall slaughter [the bull],"<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Seemingly, implying that the slaughter must be performed by a High Priest. Nevertheless, Aaron's name is explicitly associated with the verb <i>vihikriv,</i> "and he shall offer." According to the Rambam, the verse should be interpreted as meaning that the offering of the bull must be performed by the High Priest, not necessarily its slaughter.</i> if a non-priest slaughtered it, it is acceptable. Even a red heifer that was slaughtered by a non-priest is acceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Nevertheless, as an initial preference, the slaughter should be performed by a priest (<i>Hilchot Parah Adumah</i> 3:2; 4:17).</i> for there is no slaughter by a non-priest that invalidates [a sacrifice].
When a person slaughters sacrificial animals, but does not have the intent to slaughter them, but instead, is merely busying himself [thoughtlessly], they are disqualified. [He must] have the intent to slaughter them.
One should not slaughter the heads of two sacrificial animals at the same time.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., using a long knife so that the two are slaughtered with the same movements of the knife.</i> If one slaughtered [in this manner], the sacrifices are acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., after the fact. This applies only with regard to sacrificial animals. Ordinary animals may be sacrificed in this manner as an initial preference. See <i>Chullin</i> 29a.</i>
Two people may slaughter a sacrificial animal together, just as they may slaughter an ordinary animal.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 2:10.</i>
A minor may not slaughter sacrificial animals even if an adult is standing over him.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Such slaughter is acceptable for ordinary animals after the fact (<i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 4:5).</i> [The rationale is that the slaughter of] sacrificial animals requires concentrated intent and a minor does not have such a potential. Even when [a minor's] deeds indicate that he is acting with intention, [he is] not considered [to have acted] with intention if that will produce a leniency,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This is a principle applicable in many different contexts of Jewish Law, e.g., <i>Hilchot Tuma'at Ochalin</i> 3:10, 14:2; <i>Hilchot Keilim</i> 2:1.</i> only if it will produce a stringency.<br>What is implied? If [an animal to be sacrificed as] a burnt-offering was standing in the southern [portion of the Temple Courtyard] and a minor led it and brought it to the north where he slaughtered it - thus his actions indicate that he intended to slaughter a sacred animal<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">For burnt-offerings may only be slaughtered in the northern portion of the Temple Courtyard (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:2-3). Thus he obviously had the intent to slaughter the animal as a burnt offering.</i> - [the sacrifice] is still disqualified.
When sacrifices of the most sacred order were slaughtered in the southern [portion of the Temple Courtyard] or their blood was received there, they are disqualified.
If one was standing in the southern [portion of the Temple Courtyard], but he extended his hands into the northern portion and slaughtered [a sacrifice of the most sacred order], his slaughter is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 48b interprets Leviticus 1:11 as implying that what is significant is the place where the animal is standing and not where the slaughterer is standing.</i>
If, [by contrast, a priest] was standing in the southern [portion of the Temple Courtyard], but he extended his hands into the northern portion and received the blood [of such a sacrificial animal], his receiving of the blood is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim, op. cit.</i>, interprets the above verse as implying that with regard to the receiving of the blood, what is important is where the person performing that act is standing.</i>
If he brings his head and the majority of his body into the northern [portion of the Temple Courtyard], it is considered as if he was standing there.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This reflects a general principle of Torah Law: the majority of a person's body is considered as his entire body (Rav Yosef Corcus).</i>
If one slaughtered [such an animal] in the northern portion [of the Temple Courtyard] and then in the convulsive movements that accompanied its death, it moved to the southern portion or even if [a priest] took it to the southern portion, it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">For the slaughter was performed in the appropriate place.</i> If after these convulsive movements took it to the southern portion and then it returned to the northern portion and its blood was received there, it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The fact that between the slaughter and the receiving of the blood, it entered the southern portion of the courtyard does not disqualify it.</i><br>Similarly, if [animals to be slaughtered as] sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity were inside [the Temple Courtyard]<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Where it is required that they be slaughtered (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:4).</i> and one was standing outside the Temple Courtyard and inserted his hand inside and slaughtered it, his slaughter is acceptable.
If, [while standing outside the Temple Courtyard, a priest] inserted his hand inside and received the blood, the receiving of the blood is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">For the blood must be received in the Temple Courtyard.</i> Even if [the entire body of the priest] performing the service was inside [the Temple Courtyard], and his locks of hair<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This translation is necessary, because we are speaking of a portion of the person's body and not his garments. See Ezekiel 8:3 for a similar usage.</i> were outside, his service is unacceptable, for [when describing the priests' service in the Temple, Leviticus 10:9] states: "When you come to the Tent of Meeting."<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Significantly, <i>Zevachim</i> 26a, the source for this law, uses a different prooftext. Some commentaries have suggested that perhaps a printing error crept into the text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>.</i> Implied is that one must enter in his entirety.
If in the convulsive movements that accompanied its death, [such an] animal moved out of [the Courtyard] after its blood was received,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Implied is that if an animal moved out of the Temple Courtyard before its blood was received, it is disqualified.</i> it is acceptable. For even if the organs and fats to be offered on the altar, and the meat of sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity, were taken outside [the Courtyard] before [the blood] was presented [on the altar], the sacrifice is acceptable, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 32-33 of this chapter.</i>
If the entire body of [such an] animal was inside the Temple Courtyard and its foot was outside and it was slaughtered, the sacrifice is unacceptable. For [when speaking of bringing the sacrifices, Leviticus 17:5] states: "And they shall be brought to God." Implied is that they should be entirely within [the Courtyard].<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 18:15.</i>
If one slaughtered [a sacrificial animal]<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">From the sacrifices of the most sacred order.</i> while it was located entirely in [the Temple Courtyard], and afterwards, it moved one of its feet outside, he should cut off the meat until he reaches the bone,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">He should not, however, cut off the bone, because that would render the animal as blemished before the reception of the blood and thus disqualify it (see <i>Zevachim</i> 26a and commentaries).</i> and afterwards, the blood should be received. If he received the blood and afterwards, cut off the meat, it is disqualified because of the fat of the meat that is outside [the Temple Courtyard].<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">As indicated by <i>Zevachim, op. cit.</i>, the problem is not because of the blood from the meat that was outside the Temple Courtyard, because our Sages made a distinction between the blood that flows from the animal at the time of ritual slaughter and the blood that remains within its body (see <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 6:4). Nevertheless, the fat from the portion of the animal that is outside the Temple Courtyard becomes mixed with its blood. This blood could also be part of the blood which is received, causing that blood to be disqualified (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).<br>The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling, maintaining that sacrificial animals of the highest degree of sanctity become disqualified when they are removed from the Temple Courtyard whether before the blood was presented on the altar or afterwards. Moreover, even if the meat is cut off as the Rambam suggests, the animal will become ritually impure, because there is an unresolved doubt whether our Sages decreed that any animal that is removed from the Temple Courtyard becomes ritually impure. Hence, because of the doubt, we should rule stringently (see <i>Pesachim</i> 85a). The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and Rav Yosef Corcus resolve the Rambam's ruling.</i><br>With regard to sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity, there is no need to cut off [the meat]. Instead, he should bring its foot back inside [the Temple Courtyard], and receive the blood. For [even] if meat from sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity were taken out [of the Temple Courtyard] before their blood was cast [on the altar], [the sacrifice] is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Provided of course that the animal was returned to the Temple Courtyard, and the blood received there. Even if a portion of the animal was outside the Courtyard, as long as the blood was received inside the Temple Courtyard, the sacrifice is not disqualified. Nevertheless, the portion that was outside the Temple Courtyard is forbidden to be eaten (see Halachah 32).<br>The Ra'avad states that after the blood was cast on the altar, the meat may be taken out of the Temple Courtyard. Rav Yosef Corcus states that this is obvious, because the meat of sacrifices of a less degree of sanctity may be eaten throughout the city of Jerusalem (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 11:5-6).</i>
If one hung the animal [above the earth] and slaughtered it in the free space of the Temple Courtyard, it is unacceptable, for [Leviticus 1:11] speaks [of slaughtering animals] "on the flank of the altar," implying that one must slaughter on the ground.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Zevachim</i> 26a, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> interprets this halachah as referring only to sacrifices of the most sacred order. (This is reflected also in the prooftext which refers to such a sacrifice.) Sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity, by contrast, may be slaughtered if they are hoisted in the air, as long as they are within the space above the Temple Courtyard.</i>
If the [sacrificial] animal was on the ground, but [the slaughterer] was hanging in the air and he slaughtered the animal while hanging, this disqualifies sacrifices of the most sacred order. Sacrifices of lesser sanctity, by contrast, are acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Based on <i>Zevachim, op. cit.</i>, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> - and his objections are also seconded by Rav Yosef Corcus - suggests emending the text of this halachah. As stated in Halachah 19, there is a difficulty in receiving the blood of a sacrifice while hanging in the air, because this is not befitting to the Temple service. Nevertheless, slaughtering an animal is not a formal part of the Temple service (and hence can be performed by a non-priest). Therefore there is no difficulty in performing it while hoisted in the air. And as stated in Halachah 20, the open space of the Temple Courtyard is considered as the Temple Courtyard, so it is as if the slaughterer is standing in the Temple Courtyard.</i>
If one slit the lesser portion of the organs that must be slit for ritual slaughter<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The windpipe and the gullet.</i> outside [the Temple Courtyard],<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For an animal to be sacrificed as a sacrifice of a lesser degree of sanctity.</i> and one completed the slaughter inside or one slit the lesser portion of the organs<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Of an animal to be slaughtered as a sacrifice of the most sacred order.</i> in the southern portion of [the Temple Courtyard], and completed the slaughter in the north, they are unacceptable. For ritual slaughter is considered as one continuous, integral act from the beginning to its completion.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 4:13 for another application of this principle.</i>
If one was hung and received the blood from the neck of a [sacrificial] animal<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">This applies both with regard to sacrifices of the most sacred order and those of a lesser degree of sanctity.</i> that is located on the ground, [the act] is unacceptable, because this is not the manner of Temple service.
If one was standing in the Temple Courtyard and hung a receptacle over his arm and received the blood in the air, or lifted the animal and thus received the blood in the air, [the act] is acceptable, for the open space above the place is considered as the space itself.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Thus the animal's blood is considered to have been received in the Temple Courtyard.</i>
If one placed one receptacle within a second receptacle and received the blood, [the act] is acceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">It is considered as if one was holding the receptacle in which the blood was received in one's hands.</i> one substance is not considered as an interposing substance for another substance of the same type.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general principle, applying in several areas of Torah Law (e.g., <i>Hilchot Shofar Sukkah ViLulav</i> 1:5; 7:12).</i> If one placed fibers inside the receptacle and received the blood, [the act] is acceptable, because the fibers are porous and thus the blood descends into the receptacle and there is no interference. If, however, one does this while taking a handful of flour from a meal offering and took the handful with the fibers, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The difference is that the blood will flow through the fibers, but the flour will not.</i>
Receiving the blood [of a sacrificial animal], bringing it to the altar, casting it on the altar and bringing the limbs [of a sacrificial animal] to the ramp, are all tasks<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are considered integral parts of the process of offering a sacrifice and therefore require a priest's involvement.</i> that are only acceptable if performed by a priest who is fit to perform service, as we explained with regard to taking the handful of flour from a meal offering<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 12:23; 13:12.</i> or snipping of the head of a fowl.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that this refer to <i>ibid.</i>, ch. 6. The <i>Lechem Mishneh</i> states that he does not understand where in that chapter there is an allusion to the need for a priest to perform that service.</i>
Bringing [blood or limbs] to the altar in a way other than walking is not considered as bringing them. Therefore if a priest<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on authoritative manuscripts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard published text states <i>kohen gadol</i>. Translating that term as "the High Priest" would not be appropriate in the present context. Some have suggested that the intent is a large priest, but most consider it a printing error. However, see Likkutei Sichos, Vol. 7, p. 205, where an explanation is given that justifies the wording <i>kohen gadol</i>.</i> receives the blood [and while] standing in that place, casts it on the altar, the sacrifice is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the blood was cast on the appropriate place.</i>
If [the priest] received [the blood] with his right hand and then transferred [the receptacle in which he received] it to his left hand, he should return it to his right hand.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">And continue the service with it. If he received the blood while holding the receptacle with his left hand, the sacrifice is disqualified.</i> If he received [the blood] with an ordinary utensil, the sacrifice is disqualified. If he received it in a sacred receptacle and transferred it to an ordinary receptacle, he should return it to a sacred receptacle.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">He may then continue the service; the sacrifice is not disqualified.</i>
If [the blood] spilled out of the receptacle on to the floor [of the Temple Courtyard, the sacrifice] is acceptable if one gathers up [the blood].<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since initially it was received in the proper manner, the fact that it spilled is not considered significant.</i> If, however, it spilled out from the neck of the [sacrificial] animal to the floor, and then was collected and placed in a sacred receptacle, the sacrifice is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since initially, it was not received in the proper manner.</i>
If some of the blood from the neck of the [sacrificial] animal was spilled on the ground and not collected, but [a priest] received the remainder from the neck of the animal, [the sacrifice] is acceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">For, after the fact, it is not necessary to receive all of the animal's blood (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). This is, however, the initial preference (see <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 4:8).</i> provided the blood that was received is [the animal's] lifeblood<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 6:3, the Rambam defines this term as "blood that flows out [from the animal] when it is slaughtered, killed, or decapitated as long as it is tinted red." See also the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Keritot</i> 5:1).</i> and not blood concentrated [in the limbs]<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Blood that flows slowly after the majority of the animal's blood has already been discharged. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> or the blood of the skin.
If any of the individuals who are unacceptable to perform Temple service<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">A non-priest or a priest who was disqualified. See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 9:15.</i> receive the blood [of a sacrificial animal], bring the blood to the altar, or cast it on the altar as required by law,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Once sacrificial blood has been cast on the altar as required by law, the blood remaining in the receptacle is considered merely as remnants and it is no longer able to be used to fulfill the service associated with this sacrifice.<br>One might object because, from Halachah 28, it appears that a person who is unfit to carry out Temple service does not cause the remainder of the blood to be considered as remnants. Hence, in the situation at hand, it would appear that if there is sufficient blood left in the receptacle, the sacrifice should not be disqualified. A distinction can however be made between Halachah 28 which speaks about blood that remains in the body of the sacrificial animal and this situation where the blood is remaining in the receptacle from which other blood was taken (<i>Tosafot, Zevachim</i> 92a). If the blood was not cast on the altar as required by law, the sacrifice is not disqualified and it is acceptable if that service is performed properly by an acceptable priest (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> the sacrifice is disqualified. If [a priest] who is acceptable for such service receives the blood and gives it to one who is unacceptable, but the latter does not walk with it, but instead stands in his place, he should return it to the acceptable [priest].<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Who should then bring it to the altar. The fact that the person who was unacceptable held it does not disqualify the sacrifice.</i> If, however, the unacceptable person carried it [toward the altar] and then returned it to the acceptable [priest] who carried it [to the altar] or the acceptable priest carried it [to the altar] and then gave it to the unacceptable one who carried it, since it was carried by the unacceptable person, whether at the beginning or the end, the sacrifice is disqualified, because this matter cannot be corrected.
[The following laws apply if] the blood was received by an unacceptable person. If any of [the animal's] lifeblood remains, an acceptable [priest] should receive the blood, bring it [to the altar], and cast [it upon it]. [The rationale is that] individuals who are unacceptable for Temple service do not cause the remainder of the blood to be considered as remnants.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">With which the service may not be performed.</i><br>An exception is one who is impure. Since he is fit to carry out Temple service when a sacrifice is brought in a state of impurity as explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 4:10.</i> he causes [the blood to be considered as] remnants. What is implied? If an impure [priest] received<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> maintains that the intent is that the impure priest received the blood and cast it on the altar. Receiving the blood alone does not disqualify the animal. He bases his interpretation on <i>Hilchot Me'ilah</i>3:2-3 where this concept is stated explicitly. In this manner, he resolves the Ra'avad's objections to the Rambam's ruling.</i> the blood [of a sacrificial animal] even if an acceptable [priest] received [the animal's] lifeblood afterwards and cast it [upon the altar], the sacrifice is disqualified. For [the blood] received by the acceptable [priest] afterwards is considered as remnants and is of no consequence.
When even the slightest substance is taken from one of the [sacrificial] animal's limbs<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in a manner which would cause the animal to be disqualified as physically blemished. See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i>, ch. 7, for a list of such blemishes.</i> after it was slaughtered, but before its blood was cast upon the altar, it is disqualified. Even if one [merely] mutilated the ear of an animal before [its blood] was received, it is as if [its blood] was not received. [This is derived from Leviticus 16:14 which] states: "And he shall take from the blood of the bull." [Implied is that] it must be entirely whole at the time [the blood] is received. If one received [the blood] of an imperfect [animal] and cast it upon the altar, [the sacrifice] is unacceptable.
If, however, its substance was reduced after [its blood] was received,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Note the gloss of Rabbi Akiva Eiger who maintains that it is possible that it be necessary also to bring the blood to the altar while the animal is whole.</i> before it was cast [on the altar], even if the meat was lost before the blood was cast [on the altar] or it was consumed by fire, he should cast the blood [on the altar] as long as an olive-sized portion of the meat or the organs and fats to be offered on the altar's pyre remain.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">An olive-sized portion is considered significant. If even that small a portion of the meat can be eaten or the organs and fats can be offered on the altar, the purpose of the sacrifice will be consummated. Hence, it is appropriate to cast the blood on the altar.</i> If not even that remains,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">The remnants are not considered as significant.</i> he should not cast the blood. With regard to a burnt-offering, even if half an olive-sized portion from the meat and half an olive-sized portion of the organs and fats [remain, he should cast the blood], because the entire [animal] is [offered on the altar's] pyre.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Hence the fat and the meat can be combined.</i>
If less than an olive-sized portion [of a burnt-offering remains], [the blood] should not be cast [on the altar]. If it is cast [upon it], the sacrifice is not received with favor [Above].<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifice is disqualified and if the person was bringing it to fulfill an obligation, he must bring another one.</i> If the meat<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">And the organs and fat to be offered on the altar.</i> is disqualified before [the blood] is cast on the altar or it was taken out of the Temple Courtyard, the blood should not be cast. If, however, it was cast, the sacrifice is received with favor [Above].<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance, casting the blood on the altar is sufficient to cause the sacrifice to be considered acceptable. See also the following halachah.</i>
When the meat of sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity was taken out of the Temple Courtyard before the blood was cast [on the altar], even though the blood was cast [upon the altar] while the meat was outside, the sacrifice is acceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the organs and the fats should be offered on the altar and the person bringing the sacrifice is considered to have fulfilled his obligation.</i> because ultimately, the meat will be taken outside [the Temple Courtyard].<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">For sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity may be eaten throughout the city of Jerusalem.</i><br>Casting [the blood on the altar] is effective with regard to [the obligation to have the sacrificial meat] which was taken out [of the Temple Courtyard] burnt,<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, when a sacrifice was disqualified, it would have to be burnt immediately. If, however, it was disqualified because of a difficulty with the casting of the blood or because the owners were disqualified, it should be kept until its form decomposes and then it is burnt (Rashi, <i>Me'ilah</i> 7b-8a).</i> but not to have it permitted to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., casting the blood of sacrifices of a lesser degree enables the meat of the animal to be eaten. This applies, however, only when the animal was in the Temple Courtyard at the time the blood was cast on the altar. If not, the sacrifice is acceptable, but the meat may not be eaten (Rav Yosef Corcus). The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> (see also Ra'avad) offers a different interpretation, saying the intent could be sacrificial meat taken out of the city of Jerusalem.</i>
Similarly, when the organs and fats to be offered on the altar from sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity were taken out [of the Temple Courtyard] before their blood [was cast on the altar] and the blood was cast [on the altar] when they were outside, the sacrifice was not disqualified. If they were returned [to the Temple Courtyard], they should be offered on the altar's pyre.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling, questioning why these organs and fats should be offered on the altar's pyre. The Rambam's maintains that since the prohibition of <i>me'ilah</i> and the prohibitions mentioned in this halachah apply, the sacrifice is not disqualified. Hence, there is no reason why these organs and fats should not be offered (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).<br>Rav Yosef Corcus avoids this difference of opinion by explaining that this is referring to an instance where the organs and the fats were returned to the Temple Courtyard before the blood was cast upon the altar. According to all authorities, the fats and the organs should be offered in this instance.</i> Even if they were not returned [to the Temple Courtyard], one is liable for violating the transgressions<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">These transgressions apply when the blood is cast on the altar in the proper manner. The Rambam is emphasizing that even in this instance when the fats and organs are outside the Temple Courtyard at the time the blood is cast on the altar - and therefore disqualified - these prohibitions still apply.</i> [against partaking of] <i>piggul,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><i>As will be explained in chs. 14-16, when a person slaughters an animal with the intent of partaking of its meat at times other than those which are permitted, the sacrifice is considered as piggul and it is forbidden to partake of its meat.</i></i> notar</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in Chapter 18, Halachot 9-10, when sacrificial meat is left beyond the time when it should be eaten, it is called <i>notar</i> and it is forbidden to partake of it.</i> and impure [sacrificial] meat<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>ibid.</i>:12, when sacrificial meat becomes impure, it is forbidden to partake of it.</i> if he partakes of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">Even if these organs and fats were outside the Temple Courtyard at the time the blood was cast upon the altar, the casting of the blood caused them to be considered as sacrificial meat.</i>
With regard to any sacrifices [brought by] a private individual, whether the meat became impure, but the fats are intact or the fats became impure, but the meat remains intact, the blood should be cast on the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if either the meat could be eaten (or offered on the altar in the instance of a burnt-offering) or the fats could be offered on the altar, there will be some positive value to the sacrifice.</i> If they both became impure, the blood should not be cast. If, however, it was cast on the altar, the sacrifice is received with favor [Above], for the High Priest's forehead plate arouses [God's] favor.<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i> 4:2, the Rambam writes that if a priest cast the blood on the altar when he knows that the Paschal sacrifice is impure, the forehead plate does not cause it to be considered acceptable, while in this instance, he does not make such a distinction. Nevertheless, the reason for this distinction is evident from the Rambam's statements in <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i>: the Paschal sacrifice is offered solely that it be eaten, while with regard to other sacrifices there is a positive dimension to the offering of the fats and organs on the altar even if the sacrifice is not eaten.</i> Similarly, when fats and organs to be offered on the altar's pyre or the limbs of a burnt-offering became impure and they were [nonetheless]<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">The initial preference is that they should not be offered on the altar.</i> offered on the altar, the High Priest's forehead plate arouses [God's] favor, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 4:7.</i> With regard to any of the communal sacrifices, [even if] all of the meat and fats became impure, the blood should be cast [upon the altar].<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance as well, it is the High Priest's forehead plate that arouses the positive spiritual influences that cause the sacrifice to be accepted. In contrast to individual sacrifices, however, with regard to communal sacrifices, one should cast the blood on the altar as an initial preference even though both the fats and the meat have been disqualified, for the restrictions against ritual impurity are superseded by the obligation to offer communal sacrifices (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>; Rav Yosef Corcus).</i>
When the blood of sacrificial animals was taken outside the Temple Courtyard, the sacrifice is disqualified. Even though it was brought back inside and cast on the altar, the sacrifice is not received with favor [Above].<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">And another sacrifice must be brought in its place. The forehead plate does not cause such sacrifices to be acceptable (<i>Zevachim</i> 8:12).</i>
No blood from sacrificial animals is susceptible to ritual impurity at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">Even if it comes in direct contact with a source of impurity, it does not become impure. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Ediot</i> 8:4), the Rambam states that there are some Talmudic references to the blood of sacrificial animals becoming impure, but all of those statements were made before the testimony given by Rabbi Yossi ben Yoezar which stated that they never become impure. Once that statement was made, the principle was accepted without argument.</i> For [Deuteronomy 12:16] states with regard to blood: "You shall pour it on the earth like water." [Implied is that] blood which is poured out like water is considered as water and is susceptible to ritual impurity. The blood of sacrificial animals, by contrast, is not poured out like water<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Instead, it is poured on the altar.</i> and hence is not susceptible to ritual impurity.
When the sun sets and the blood from a sacrificial animal [slaughtered that day] has not been cast [on the altar], the sacrifice is disqualified. If [the blood] is cast [on the altar afterwards],<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Whether at night or on the next day.</i> [the sacrifice] is not received with favor [Above].

Chapter 2

[With regard to the presentation of] the blood from any of the sacrifices on the outer altar: as long as one makes one presentation of blood, atonement is generated.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Although there is a desired manner in which the blood from every sacrifice should be offered on the altar, that is merely the desired manner of fulfilling the mitzvah. After the fact, even one presentation of blood is sufficient.</i> Even with regard to a sin-offering, one presentation is of fundamental importance.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yosef Corcus explains that the Rambam's intent is that even if the priest did not present the blood of the sin-offering on the corners of the altar at all as required, but rather poured it on the wall of the altar, it is sufficient to bring atonement.</i> Making the remaining [three of] the four [required] presentations is [merely] the optimum manner of fulfilling the mitzvah, as [implied by Deuteronomy 12:27]: "The blood of your sacrifices shall you pour on the altar."<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that rather than use the method of exegesis stated in <i>Zevachim</i> 37b, the Rambam quotes a different prooftext. This follows a pattern demonstrated in several other places in the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> where the Rambam derives a concept from the apparent meaning of Biblical verses even though the traditional Rabbinic approach is to derive the idea from other sources.</i> [One can infer that one] pouring of blood on the altar is of fundamental importance.
Whenever [a priest] poured [blood] over the altar when he should have cast it upon it,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Casting blood on the altar refers to a situation where a priest stands slightly removed from the altar and casts the blood upon it powerfully. The blood of the burnt offerings, peace offerings, and guilt offerings are presented on the altar in this manner. Pouring the blood on the altar refers to a situation where the priest stands next to the altar and pours the blood gently upon it. The blood of firstborn offerings, tithe offerings, and Paschal sacrifices are presented in this manner.</i> the obligation is fulfilled, as [implied by the verse:] "The blood of your sacrifices shall you pour."
With regard to all of the blood presented on the inner altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the bull and the goat offered on Yom Kippur and the other sin-offerings which are burnt rather than eaten that are mentioned in <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 1:5. See <i>ibid.</i> 5:11 for a description of the manner in which the sacrifice was offered.</i> if one of the presentations is lacking, the sacrifice does not bring atonement. Instead, all of them are integral for the atonement, for Scripture was precise with regard to their number, as [Leviticus 4:7, 17; 16:14] states: "seven times."
If the owner died after one of the presentations of the blood of a sin-offering was made, the remainder of the presentations should be made after his death.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">If the owner of a sin-offering dies before even one presentation of the blood was made, the blood should not be presented (see Chapter 4, Halachah 1). If, however, one presentation was made, the sacrifice is fundamentally acceptable, as stated in Halachah 1. Hence the remainder of the presentations should also be made.</i>
If, however, he made one presentation during the day, he should not make the [remaining] three at night.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For the blood is disqualified at sunset (<i>Zevachim</i> 56a) and hence should not be presented upon the altar. Hence, even though the sacrifice is acceptable, the remaining presentations should not be made.</i>
If one made one [of the presentations of blood required to be made on] the inner altar on [that altar] and made the remainder on the outer [altar], he is liable for [<i>karet</i>] for offering [a sacrifice] outside its appropriate place.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 19:13.</i>
If [a priest] was sprinkling [the blood of a sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">The Torah uses the term <i>haza'ah</i>, "sprinkling," with regard to the sin-offering of a fowl and the sin-offerings whose blood is offered on the inner altar.</i> on the altar] and his hand was cut off before the blood reached the space above the altar, the sprinkling of the blood is not acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">For at the time the sprinkling is completed, the priest who performed it was no longer acceptable for Temple service, because of his physical blemish. Even though the blemish did not occur until after the priest completed his activity, the time when the blood reached the altar is most significant. See <i>Zevachim</i> 15a.</i>
If one changed [the order] of corners<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on authoritative manuscripts and early printings. The standard published text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> uses a different version.</i> [of the altar on which the blood was presented when bringing] a sin-offering - whether for a sin-offering [whose blood] is offered on the inner altar<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:12, 14, for a description of the presentation of the blood for these sacrifices.</i> or a sin-offering [whose blood] is offered on the outer altar<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>ibid.</i>:10 for a description of the presentation of the blood for these sacrifices.</i> - [the sacrifice] is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Based on Halachah 10, it appears that the intent in this and the following halachah is that the meat of the sacrifice is unacceptable and may not be eaten. Nevertheless, the sacrifice itself is acceptable, since its blood has reached the altar.</i> If, however, [the order of the presentation of the blood] for other sacrifices is changed, [the sacrifices] are acceptable.
If [the priest] presented the blood beyond the corner of the altar - whether for a sin-offering or for another offering and whether for the inner altar or for the outer altar - [the sacrifice] is unacceptable.
When blood that according to [the Torah's] command should have been presented above the midpoint of the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 2:9, there was a scarlet band dividing the upper half of the altar from the lower half. Sin-offerings of animals (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:7) and burnt-offerings of fowl (<i>ibid.</i> 6:20) should be offered above the midpoint of the altar.</i> was presented below it;<br>if according to [the Torah's] command, it should have been presented below the midpoint of the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the blood of all other sacrifices.</i> but it was presented above it;<br>if [blood that] according to [the Torah's] command should have been presented inside [the Temple]<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 3:6), the Rambam states that this refers to blood presented on the inner altar, on the <i>Parochet</i> (the curtain separating between the Sanctuary and the Holy of Holies), and within the Holy of Holies itself.</i> and was presented on the outer altar;<br>if [blood that] should have been presented on the outer altar was presented in [the Temple]; or<br>if blood that should have been presented on the outer [altar] was presented on the ramp in a place that is not opposite the foundation [of the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The foundation of the altar did not surround the altar on its southern side, the place where the ramp was positioned. Rav Yosef Corcus explains that since the ramp is considered as equivalent to the altar in several contexts (see <i>Menachot</i> 57b; <i>Zevachim</i> 87a), after the fact, presenting the blood on it is considered equivalent to presenting it on the altar itself.</i><br>[in all these instances,] the meat of the sacrifice is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Since the blood was not offered in its proper place, in this context, it is as if the sacrifice was disqualified and the meat cannot be eaten. Nevertheless, even if "the blood of life" remains, it may not be offered upon the altar again. The rationale is that since the blood reached the altar, atonement is granted and another sacrifice is not required.</i> Nevertheless, the owners of the sacrifices receive atonement because of it, for its blood has reached the altar. Although it did not reach the proper place, it is as if it reached its [proper] place with regard to atonement.<br>When does the above apply? When the person casting [the blood] is acceptable for Temple service. If, however, a person fit for Temple service received [the blood] and gave it to a person who is unacceptable and that unacceptable person presented [blood] that should have been presented above [the midpoint] of the altar below its midpoint, [blood] that should have been presented [on the] outer [altar] was presented inside [the Temple Sanctuary], [blood] that should have been presented inside [the Temple Sanctuary] was presented[on the] outer [altar], or one presented [the blood] on the ramp in a place that is not opposite the foundation [of the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Although the Rambam does not mention all the instances that were mentioned in the first clause, they are all included in this ruling.</i> the meat of the sacrifice is not disqualified if [any of the sacrificial animal's] blood of life remains.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since the casting of the blood is disqualified entirely because the person sprinkling it was unacceptable, it is as if it was not performed at all. Hence, if more "blood of life" remains, the sacrifice can be offered as if nothing had happened.</i> Instead, an acceptable person should receive the remainder of the blood of life and cast it [on the altar] in its appropriate place.
[The following rules apply when] the blood of sacrificial animals becomes mixed between two types of blood<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Blood from two sacrificial animals were mixed into the same cup.</i> or between two cups of blood.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The blood from two sacrificial animals was collected in separate cups, but it was forgotten in which cup the blood of each sacrificial animal was contained.</i> If [the blood of sacrifices that require] one presentation [of blood] was mixed with [the blood of others that require] one presentation [of blood],<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., the blood of a firstborn offering with the blood of a tithe offering or of a Paschal sacrifice. See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:17.</i> one presentation should be made of the entire [mixture]. Similarly, if [the blood of sacrifices that require] four presentations [of blood was mixed] with [the blood of others that require] four presentations,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Sin-offerings are the only sacrifices that require four presentations of blood on the altar. The Rambam is speaking about a situation in which the blood from one sin-offering was mixed with the blood from another.</i> four presentations should be made of the entire [mixture]. If, [however, the blood of sacrifices that require] one presentation [of blood] was mixed with [the blood of others that require] two presentations that are four,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., burnt-offerings, guilt-offerings, and peace-offerings whose blood is dashed on the northeast and southwest corners of the altar so that it will have been presented on all four sides as described in <i>ibid.</i> 5:6.</i> one presentation should be made of the entire [mixture].<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale for this ruling is that it is forbidden to make more than one presentation of the blood that requires only one presentation, because doing so would be a violation of the prohibition of adding to the Torah's commandments (see <i>Zevachim</i> 8:10). And after the fact, it is sufficient to make one presentation of the blood of sacrifices that require more as stated in Halachah 1. Although in failing to make the four presentations required for a sin-offering, the priest is detracting from the Torah's commandments and that is also forbidden, he is not performing an act when doing so.</i><br>If [blood that was] to be presented on the upper [half of the altar] became mixed with [blood that was] to be presented on the lower [half of the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See the notes to Halachah 10.</i> all [the blood] should be poured into the [waste] channel<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 2:11.</i> and the sacrifices are disqualified. Even if the remainder of [the blood from] a sin-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., this refers to the blood that remains after the presentations on the corners of the altar were completed. This blood should be poured out at the base of the altar.</i> is mixed with the blood of a burnt-offering in which instance, all of the blood should be presented on the bottom of the altar, the entire [mixture] should be poured into the [waste] channel.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For the blood of the burnt-offering should be dashed on the wall of the altar, while the remnants of the blood of the sin-offering should be poured directly on the altar's base. It should not be poured on the altar's wall, for that would be considered as an addition to the required number of presentations.</i>
If [blood from a sin-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Which must be presented on the upper half of the altar.</i> became mixed with the remnants of the blood of a burnt-offering]<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Which should be poured on the altar's base. The definition of the halachah as speaking about such a situation is taken from the gloss of Rav Yosef Corcus, based on <i>Zevachim</i> 81b.</i> and [the priest] did not inquire [concerning the law], but instead presented some of the mixture on the upper portion [of the altar] and some on the lower portion, it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">As an initial preference, he should not have presented the blood on the upper portion of the altar as stated in the previous halachah. Once he did, however, we assume that some of the blood from the sin-offering was presented there, thus the entire mixture is considered as the remainder of the blood of both a sin-offering and burnt-offering. In both instances, the remainder should be poured on the base of the altar.</i> If he presented a portion on the upper [portion of the altar] and then inquired, he should present [the remainder] on the lower portion.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., on the altar's base. Once some of the mixture was presented on the upper half of the altar, the preferred course of action is to pour the entire mixture on the altar's base.</i> He is considered to have fulfilled his obligation for both [sacrifices].
If blood that was required to be presented in the Temple building<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 10.</i> became mixed with blood to be presented on the outer [altar], the entire [mixture] should be poured into the [waste] channel.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Because, as an initial preference, there is no satisfactory manner of presenting this blood. For the blood from the sacrifices that is required to be offered in the Sanctuary should not be offered on the outer altar. Conversely, the blood that is required to be offered on the outer altar should not be offered in the Sanctuary.</i> If he did not inquire and took the mixture of the blood and presented some in the Temple [building] and some outside - whether first he presented it inside and then he presented it outside or first he presented it outside and then he presented it inside - everything is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">For we assume that some of the blood for each sacrifice was presented in the appropriate manner.</i><br>When does the above apply? With regard to the blood [of the sacrifices that must] be presented on the outer [altar] with the exception of a sin-offering. If, however, the blood of a sin-offering that should be presented outside becomes mixed with the blood of a sin-offering that should be presented inside, it is acceptable [only] if one made the presentation outside and then made the presentation inside.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even after the fact, it is acceptable only in this manner.</i> If, however, one made the presentation inside and then made the presentation outside, [the sacrifice whose blood was to be presented outside] is unacceptable. [The rationale is that] the blood of a sin-offering that was brought into the Temple building - even the blood of a sin-offering brought by an individual [whose meat] should be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that from the standard published text of <i>Zevachim</i> 82a, it would appear that the concept is more obvious with regard to an individual sin-offering than a communal sin-offering. They assume that the Rambam had a different version of the text.</i> - becomes forbidden, as [implied by Leviticus 6:23]: "Any sin-offering whose blood has been brought [into the Tent of Meeting... shall not be eaten]."<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Just as the sacrifice is forbidden to be eaten, the blood is forbidden to be presented on the altar. If, however, the blood was presented outside, the sacrifice is acceptable after the fact. The meat, however, is forbidden to be eaten.</i><br>[The above applies] provided [the blood] is brought in through the gate to the Temple Building, for the prooftext speaks of it being "brought," implying an ordinary manner of entrance.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">The term <i>huvah</i> has as its root the word <i>ba</i> which means "come," leading to the inference the Rambam draws. See parallels in <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 3:19; <i>Hilchot Tuma'at Tzara'at</i> 16:5.</i> If, however, it was brought in through a wicket<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 4:6 with regard to the wickets leading to the Sanctuary.</i> or through a window or the roof, it is not disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">For these are not the normal manner through which blood is brought into the Sanctuary.</i>
When the blood from a bull brought [because of the violation of a law] forgotten [by the High Court] or from a goat brought [because of the violation of the prohibition against] idol worship<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 1:16 and notes for a description of these sacrifices.</i> which should be brought into the Sanctuary was brought to the Holy of Holies, it is disqualified. For this place is inward with regard to the appropriate place for this blood.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., just as blood that is required to be presented on the outer altar becomes disqualified if it is brought into the Sanctuary, blood that is to be presented on the inner altar, becomes disqualified when it is brought further inward, to the Holy of Holies.</i><br>Similarly, with regard to the bull and the goat brought on Yom Kippur<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 1:1; 4:1.</i> whose blood is brought into the Holy of Holies, if the blood [of these offerings] was brought into the Holy of Holies and sprinkled there,<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">But the required number of sprinklings were not completed.</i> was then taken to the Sanctuary and afterwards, returned to the Holy of Holies, it is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Just as the blood of an ordinary sin-offering is disqualified when taken out of the Temple Courtyard, so, too, the blood of these offerings is disqualified when taken out of the Holy of Holies before the sprinklings are completed.</i> [The High Priest] should not complete the sprinkling [of the blood] in the Holy of Holies. Once he departs, he has departed.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the blood of this sacrifice will later be sprinkled in the Sanctuary as well, at the present time, the sprinklings should have been completed in the Holy of Holies. Since that was not done, taking the blood out disqualifies it.<br>The Ra'avad (and similarly, Rashi in his commentary to <i>Zevachim</i> 82b-83a) has a different understanding of the passage on which the Rambam's ruling was based and hence, objects. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> offers grounds to justify the Rambam's understanding.</i><br>Similarly, if he completed the sprinklings in the Holy of Holies, then brought the blood into the Sanctuary and made some of the sprinklings [required there], then took [the blood] out of the Sanctuary and afterwards returned it, he should not complete the sprinklings in the Sanctuary. [The rationale is that] since the blood was taken out of its place, it became disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The same rationale applies here as in the previous clause. Rav Yosef Corcus questions why the Rambam rules that the blood has been disqualified. Seemingly, since <i>Zevachim</i> 83a leaves this as an unresolved question, the Rambam should not rule that it is definitely disqualified. He explains that although one of the Sages considered it an unresolved issue, when the entire passage is considered, it would appear that it is not acceptable.</i>
If the blood of an [ordinary] sin-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">That should be offered on the outer altar.</i> was received in two cups and one of them was taken outside, the one that remained inside is acceptable and the sprinklings may be performed.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For as long as a portion of the blood of a sin-offering is offered in the appropriate manner, it is acceptable. Although the blood that was taken out is disqualified, it does not disqualify the blood that remains.</i> If, [by contrast,] one [of the cups] was taken into the Sanctuary and sprinkled there, even the one left outside is disqualified, as [implied by the prooftext] : "whose blood has been brought in," i.e., even if only a portion of its blood was brought in to the Sanctuary to bring about atonement, it is disqualified.
[The following rules apply when] the blood of an [ordinary] sin-offering was taken into [the Sanctuary] to achieve atonement, but nothing was done and instead, he took it out without sprinkling it inside. If he brought it in unknowingly, [the blood] remains acceptable and should be sprinkled outside, for nothing to achieve atonement was done in the Sanctuary.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">Implied is that if it was sprinkled inside, even unknowingly, it is disqualified.</i> If he brought it in intentionally, it is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Even if it was not sprinkled inside. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and Rav Yosef Corcus note that this ruling appears to contradict <i>Zevachim</i> 36a. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> concludes that although the Rambam's ruling can be reconciled with the passage, the resolution still leaves certain points that require explanation.</i>
If the sin-offering itself was brought into the Sanctuary,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Even after the animal was slaughtered and before its blood was received (<i>Zevachim</i> 92b).</i> it is acceptable, because [the prooftext] mentions "whose blood was brought in," [i.e., it is the blood] and not the meat [that disqualifies it].
When a sin-offering of a fowl moved in its death throes and entered the Sanctuary, it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">This law is mentioned because of the contrast to the law that follows. The prooftext above speaks of a sacrifice being disqualified because its blood was "brought into" the Temple Sanctuary. In this instance, the fowl was not brought in, but rather entered on its own.</i>
If the blood of a sin-offering of a fowl was taken into the Sanctuary in its neck,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the fowl was slaughtered, but the neck was held upright instead of allowing the blood to flow out into a receptacle.</i> there is an unresolved doubt whether its neck is considered a receptacle [which would disqualify the sacrifice].<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">For when the blood of a sin-offering is brought into the Temple sanctuary in a utensil, it is disqualified, as stated in Halachah 13.</i>
If the blood of a sin-offering of a fowl<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">With regard to a parallel situation concerning a sin-offering of an animal, see Chapter 1, Halachah 25.</i> was spilled<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Unto the floor of the Temple Courtyard.</i> and then collected,<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Into a receptacle. The blood of a fowl should be squeezed from the neck of the animal unto the altar directly as stated in <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 7:6. The question is whether collecting the blood in a receptacle disqualifies the sacrifice or not, i.e., when the Torah stated that the blood of a sin-offering of a fowl should be presented directly on the altar was that granting permission (but not negating, presenting it from a receptacle) or stating that it must be presented in this manner (see <i>Zevachim</i> 92b).</i> there is an unresolved question: Does the receptacle disqualify the blood or not? Therefore the fowl should be burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that this also applies to the situation mentioned in Halachah 19.</i> like all the sin-offerings of fowl concerning which there are unresolved doubts. <sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 7, Halachah 10.</i>
When [a priest] received the blood of a sin-offering in four cups and made one presentation [on the altar] from each cup, the remainder of all four cups is poured on the altar's base, as [Leviticus 4:30] states: "And all of its blood shall be poured [on the base of the altar]." If he made all four presentations from one cup, the remnants of that cup should be poured on the altar's base and the other cups poured in the drainage canal.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Since they were set aside as separate entities, but blood was not presented on the altar from them, they are not considered as the remnants of the blood presented and hence should not be poured on the altar's base. <i>Yoma</i> 57b derives this from the fact that Leviticus 4:30 states: "Its blood should be poured on the altar's base," implying that there are times when all of its blood is not poured there.</i>
When blood falls into water or into ordinary blood,<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., blood from an animal that was not offered as a sacrifice.</i> it should not be sprinkled on the altar. If it was sprinkled, it is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if it has the appearance of blood. For every drop of blood that falls into the mixture is nullified as it falls in. Thus it is considered as if there is never a majority of blood (<i>Zevachim</i> 77b).</i> When water fell into blood in a receptacle, if it has the appearance of blood, it is acceptable. If wine or ordinary blood fell into it, we make an assessment [as follows]: Were it to have been water would there have been enough to nullify the blood in the receptacle to the extent that it would no longer have the appearance of blood, he should not sprinkle from [the mixture]. If there would not have been enough to nullify its appearance, he should sprinkle from it.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">See parallels to the above in <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 14:6; <i>Hilchot Metamei Mishkav UMoshav</i> 2:6.</i>
When the blood of sacrificial animals becomes mixed with the blood of animals that are disqualified from the altar or with the blood of sacrificial animals that were disqualified because of unsatisfactory ritual slaughter, the entire mixture should be poured into the drainage canal.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the instances mentioned in the previous halachah, in this instance even if the amount of the unacceptable blood is not sufficient to nullify the acceptable blood, the sacrifice is disqualified. Among the explanations given for the distinction is that the previous halachah describes mixtures that were made with ordinary blood and it is uncommon for ordinary blood to be found in the Temple Courtyard. Hence there was no need for a Rabbinic decree to serve as a safeguard. This halachah, by contrast, speaks of mixtures that could frequently occur in the Temple. Hence lest the mixture also be permitted even when the unacceptable blood could nullify the ordinary blood, our Sages were strict and disqualified all mixtures (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> Even if all the cups [of blood] were offered aside from one, it should be poured into the drainage canal and all of those offerings are unacceptable.<br>If the [life-]blood [of a sacrificial animal] became mixed with blood concentrated [in the limbs],<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Blood that flows slowly after the majority of the animal's blood has already been discharged. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>).</i> [the mixture] should be poured into the drainage canal.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Here also, our Rabbis saw the need for a safeguard, because this is a common situation (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> If, [however,] one did not ask and presented [the blood on the altar], [the sacrifice] is acceptable.
If one cast the organs and fats offered on the altar, the limbs of burnt-offerings, the handfuls [of meal offered on the altar], the frankincense, or the meal-offerings that are to be burnt on the [altar's] pyre after they were consecrated in a sacred utensil, whether by hand<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yosef Corcus notes that the Rambam's ruling here directly contradicts his ruling in Chapter 11, Halachah 6, which states that a handful of meal that is presented on the altar by hand is disqualified. He states that although the Rambam's ruling can be resolved with difficulty, the explanations appear forced.</i> or with a utensil, whether with one's right hand or with one's left hand, they are acceptable.
When wine or water was poured [on the altar as a libation] with a bowl, the <i>hin</i> measure,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">This was one of the measures that were used in the Temple, as stated in <i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash</i> 1:17-18.</i> or another sacred utensil, it is acceptable. If they were poured with an ordinary vessel or by hand, they are unacceptable.
When one arranged the limbs [of a burnt-offering] or the handful [of meal from a meal-offering] and then arranged the logs for the altar's pyre above them<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of placing them above the logs.</i> or arranged them at the side of the limbs, there is an unresolved doubt whether this is considered as the typical way of having them consumed by fire or not.<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">More precisely, it is obviously not the ordinary way of offering these substances. The question is whether the departure from the norm is great enough to disqualify them or not.</i> Therefore as an initial preference, one should not offer them in this manner, but if one did, it is acceptable.

Chapter 3

When sacrifices of the most sacred order were slaughtered on the top of the altar, it is as if they were slaughtered in the northern [portion of the Temple Courtyard],<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:2, sacrifices of the most sacred order must be sacrificed in the northern portion of the Temple Courtyard. Now, the altar is located in the southern portion. Nevertheless, based on the prooftext the Rambam cites, <i>Zevachim</i> 85a derives that it is acceptable to slaughter these sacrificial animals on the top of the altar.</i> as [Exodus 20:21] states: "You shall slaughter upon it<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The altar.</i> your burnt-offerings and your peace-offerings." This teaches that the entire altar is an appropriate place for the slaughter of burnt-offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is acceptable for burnt-offerings, our Sages assumed that it was also acceptable for other sacrifices of the most-sacred order.</i> and peace-offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Peace-offerings are sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity which may be sacrificed anywhere in the Temple Courtyard (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:4). Nevertheless, it is necessary to state that they may be sacrificed on the top of the altar, because one might think that since there is ample space to sacrifice them, they would have to be sacrificed on the ground (<i>Zevachim, loc. cit.; Gittin</i> 67a).</i>
When a burnt-offering was slaughtered on the top of the altar or it was slaughtered below [on the ground of the Temple Courtyard] and then brought up to the top of the altar, it should be skinned and cut into portions in that place. The inner organs should be taken down and washed below<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For it is not respectful to clean out the wastes on the top of the altar.</i> and then brought back [to the top of the altar]. The skin should be taken down and given to the priests.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:19.</i>
Similarly, when [other] sacrifices that had been slaughtered were brought up to the altar, they should be skinned and cut into portions in that place. The inner organs should be taken down and washed below and then brought back [to the top of the altar]. The skin and the meat should be taken down and given to the owners. The remainder [of the sacrifice] should be offered on the altar's pyre.<br>Why shouldn't he bring down the entire [carcass] instead of skinning it and cutting it up on the top of the altar? Because anything that is fit to be offered on the pyre<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to an entity like the carcass of a sacrifice which in its present state is not fit to be offered on the altar, but is not lacking any great tasks like slaughter.</i> if it was brought up to the top of the altar should not be taken down,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">This general principle (stated in <i>Zevachim</i> 9:1) is the subject of discussion in the following halachot.</i> as [indicated by <i>ibid.</i> 29:36]: "Everything that touches the altar shall be sanctified."<br>Does [the above principle apply] even when [the entity brought to the top of the altar] is not fit [to be offered on the altar's pyre]? It is taught [Leviticus 6:2] "It is the burnt-offering on the pyre." Just as the burnt-offering is fit to be consumed by the altar's pyre should not be taken down once it was taken up [to the altar], so too, any entity that is fit for the altar's pyre<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">But only an article that is fit to be consumed by the fires.</i> if it is brought up, it should not be brought down.
When [an animal to be sacrificed as] a burnt-offering is brought up to the top of the altar while alive, it should be brought down, because it is not yet fit [to be consumed by the altar's pyre].
Similarly, a handful of meal from a meal-offering that was not consecrated in a sacred vessel<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Unless a meal offering is consecrated in a sacred vessel, it is unacceptable.</i> and all entities that are forbidden to be offered on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Animals forbidden to be sacrificed, e.g., one that was worshipped, one that is <i>treifah</i>, or one which killed a person or the like. See <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 3:11.<br>The commentaries question why the Rambam does not mention animals with disqualifying physical blemishes. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 9:3) he rules that even if they were brought to the top of the altar, they should be brought down. And if the Rambam changed his mind, it would have been appropriate to say so explicitly. Nevertheless, it is possible to explain that such animals are also included in the general category of "entities forbidden to be offered on the altar," as stated in <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach, loc. cit.</i>.</i>should be taken down from the altar even if they were brought up, because from the outset, they were not fit [to be offered on the pyre].<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The leniency that every entity brought up to the altar should be offered upon it applies only to entities that were disqualified after having been fit to be offered upon it. As our Sages taught (<i>Zevachim</i> 84a; see Halachah 8) whenever an entity was disqualified in the Temple, if it was brought to the top of the altar, it should be offered. In this instance, these substances were never fit to be offered on the altar's pyre.</i>
Similarly, when a consecrated animal was slaughtered at night, its blood was spilled, or its [blood]<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">This addition is obvious from a comparison to the following halachah.</i> was taken out of the Temple Courtyard, if it was taken up [to the altar's top], it should be taken down.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it was disqualified in the Temple, nevertheless, it was disqualified before the time its meat and/or fats and organs were to be offered on the altar's pyre (see <i>Zevachim</i> 84a,b).</i>
[Different rules apply] if, by contrast, sacrificial animals were left overnight - whether their blood, their meat, or their fats and organs were left overnight;<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Once blood is left past sunset, it is disqualified (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 4:1) and if the meat of a burnt-offering is left overnight, it is disqualified (<i>ibid.</i>:2). Nevertheless, since the meat of a peace-offering is acceptable if left overnight, this is not a serious enough disqualifying factor to prevent these entities from being offered on the altar's pyre (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i><br>a sacrificial animal was taken out of the Temple Courtyard;<br>it became impure or disqualified because of a disqualifying thought concerning the time [it was to be eaten] or the place [it was to be eaten];<br>it was sacrificed for the sake of a different offering;<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapters 13-18 with regard to these factors.</i><br>impure [priests] received its blood and cast it on the altar; [this is significant] since they are fit to perform Temple service when a sacrifice is brought in a state of impurity;<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 4:10-12.</i><br>when the blood was presented in an improper place;<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, after the fact, in many instances, the sacrifice is acceptable, as stated in Chapter 2.</i><br>or sacrifices of the most sacred order were slaughtered in the southern portion of the Temple Courtyard or their blood was received there.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Rather than in the north as required (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:2).</i><br>Although in all of these situations, [the sacrifices] are disqualified, if [the meat, fats, and/or organs] were brought to the top of the altar, they should not be taken down.
[This is the general principle:] Whenever an entity is disqualified in the Temple Courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on Rashi's commentary, <i>Zevachim</i> 84a.</i> the holiness [of the altar] accepts it.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">After the fact, as stated above.</i> Just as when these entities were brought up, they should not be taken down, so too, if they were taken down, they should not be brought up a second time. For they have been disqualified.
