If the [altar's] fire took hold of them<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 18, Halachah 21, where the Rambam speaks of the fire taking hold of the majority of the entity. Seemingly, that concept would apply here as well.</i> [before they were brought down from the altar], they should be brought up again [so that] they will be consumed by its fire.
When the handful of meal [from the meal-offering] becomes disqualified as <i>piggul</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13, Halachah 1, for a definition of this term.</i> and a portion of it is on the ground and a portion took fire, the entire [handful] should be brought up [to the altar to be consumed].<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">For the handful of meal is considered as an integral entity.</i>
When limbs, fats, and handfuls of meal were left overnight on the top of the altar, it is as if they were left overnight in the Temple Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are disqualified. Nevertheless, they should be offered on the altar's pyre, because they are on the top of the altar.</i> If they were brought down from the altar, they should not be brought up again. If, however, they were not brought down, they should be offered on the altar's pyre in all situations.
The open space above the altar is considered as the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Thus even if the disqualified sacrificial entities were not placed down on the altar, but held by a person standing on the altar, the above concepts apply (see <i>Zevachim</i> 88a). Rav Yosef Corcus notes that the Talmud mentions also a situation where a person is standing in the Temple Courtyard and holds a disqualified sacrificial entity over the altar with a pole. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that since the Talmud does not reach a final decision whether such an entity should be offered on the altar's pyre or not, because of the doubt, they should not be taken down from above the altar.</i> When one brought the fats and the organs of sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Certainly, this applies to the fats and the organs of sacrifices of the most sacred order (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> up [to the altar] before their blood was poured on the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of afterwards, as required.</i> they should not be taken down, because they have become "the food of the altar."
[The following law applies when a person] sets aside two [animals for] a guilt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Or two sin-offerings (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> one intended to serve as surety for the other.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if one was lost, the other would be sacrificed (Rashi, <i>Meilah</i> 7a).</i> If he slaughtered both of them and brought the fats and organs of one up [to the altar] before [the blood] was cast [upon it], they should be brought down.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The blood of the second animal should be cast on the altar and then its fats and organs offered. Since two animals were slaughtered and one offering can be carried out in a perfectly desirable manner, that is preferable to performing the offering in a manner that is effective only after the fact. Since the second animal will be offered in an effective manner, the blood of the first should not be cast on the altar. And since its blood should not be cast on the altar, the fat and the organs should not be offered on the altar's pyre (<i>ibid.</i>).</i>
When an unacceptable sacrifice and unacceptable wine libations<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">This clause is speaking about wine libations that were brought as accompanying offerings for a sacrifice.</i> were brought up to the altar, the sacrifice should not be taken down as we explained. The wine libations, [by contrast,] should be taken down.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the opinion of Rabbi Yehoshua in <i>Zevachim</i> 9:1, who maintains that for a disqualified entity to be offered on the altar, it must be fit for the altar's pyre and wine libations are poured over the altar and not on its pyre.</i> Similarly, when wine libations are brought independently and they were disqualified and brought to the top of the altar, they should be taken down.
When <i>melikah</i> was performed on a fowl by a non-priest and then it was brought to the top of the altar, it should not be taken down.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that in the era when sacrifices could be brought on individual altars (see the notes to <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 1:1), it was possible for a non-priest to perform <i>melikah</i> on a fowl that was offered as a sacrifice. Hence, even after the Temple was built, when a non-priest performs <i>melikah</i> on a fowl, that act is significant enough to endow with holiness to the extent that if the fowl is brought to the top of the altar, it should be offered on the pyre (<i>Zevachim</i> 69a).</i> If, [by contrast,] a handful of meal was taken by a non-priest and brought to the top of the altar, it should be brought down. Even though both of these acts disqualify [the offerings], [the handful from the meal-offering] is considered as if it was never consecrated at all.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">One might ask: Why isn't the handful of meal acceptable? When offerings were brought on an individual altar, a handful of meal could also be separated by a non-priest. In resolution, however, it is explained that in the Temple, the handful of meal was afterwards placed in a sacred utensil (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 13:12) and then offered on the altar and such service was not performed by a non-priest on an individual altar (<i>Zevachim, loc. cit.</i>).</i> [These laws apply to] a non-priest as well as to anyone else who is disqualified [from performing sacrificial service].<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., a priest with a disqualifying physical deformity; one who is intoxicated; one in the state of severe <i>onein</i> mourning (see <i>Hilchot Issurei Bi'ah</i>).</i>
The following, however, should be taken down [even though] they were brought up to the top of the altar, anything that is not fit<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., halachically not fit to be offered, but either eaten, offered on the inner altar, or discarded.</i> for the altar's pyre. [This includes:] the meat of sacrifices of the most sacred order, the meat of sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The meat of these sacrifices should not be offered on the altar, but rather eaten by the priests and, with regard to sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity, the owners.</i> the remainder of the <i>omer</i>, the remainder of the meal-offerings,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">After a handful of meal is taken from the <i>omer</i> and the meal-offerings, the remainder should not be offered on the altar, but eaten by the priests.</i> the two breads [offered on Shavuot], the showbread,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">These breads are eaten by the priests.</i> the incense offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This is not fit to be offered on the pyre of the outer altar, but instead, on coals on the inner altar.</i> the wool that is on the heads of sheep, the hair on the beards of goats, the bones, the sinews, the horns, and the hoofs if they are not attached to [the animal's body].<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Once these entities are separated from an animal's body, they should be discarded rather than offered on the altar's pyre. See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 6:2.</i> [In all these instances,] if [these entities] were brought to the top [of the altar], they should be brought down.
If some of the oil from the handful of meal was squeezed out on a bone<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's ruling is derived from the version of <i>Menachot</i> 23a in his possession. Ra'avad, Rashi, as well as the standard published text of that passage follow a different version which reads <i>eitzim</i>, wood, rather than <i>etzem,</i>, bone.</i> [that had been laying on the altar] and then the bone was taken down, it should be returned [to the altar]. [The rationale is that] there is an unresolved doubt whether entities attached to entities that should be brought up [to the altar]<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The oil is the entity that should be returned to the altar and the bone is the entity attached to it (Rav Yosef Corcus). Although on its own accord, the bone should not be returned to the altar as stated in the previous halachah, since it is attached to the oil, it should be returned, lest this be considered as treating sacred articles with disdain (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> are considered as if they should be brought up as well.
The inner altar sanctifies unacceptable entities whether they are fit for it or not fit for it,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">The inner altar has an added measure of holiness, because it was anointed and thus is comparable to a sacred vessel (Rashi, <i>Zevachim</i> 23b).</i> but the outer altar only sanctifies unacceptable entities that are fit for it, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 16.</i><br>What is implied? When sacrifices that were disqualified are brought up to the outer altar, they should not be brought down. If an unfitting incense offering<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">This includes all incense offerings, because no incense offerings are ever offered on the outer altar.</i> was offered upon it, it should be brought down, because an incense offering is not fit for the outer altar. If, by contrast, a handful of meal from the meal-offering was placed on the inner altar, it should not be taken off, whether it was fit or not fit. Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.<br>Just as the altar sanctifies any entity that is fit for it, so too, the ramp, and other sacred utensils, sanctify what is fit for them. For with regard to the sacred utensils, [Exodus 30:29] states: "Any entity that touches them will be sanctified." Thus when an entity that is fit for it reaches the ramp, it should not be brought down even though it is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Instead, it should be offered on the altar's pyre.</i> Similarly, if any entity that is fit for a sacred vessel reaches a sacred vessel, it should never be redeemed even though it was disqualified, as stated in <i>Hilchot Issurei HaMizbeiach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 6:4-5.</i>
Vessels made for liquids do not consecrate solids and vessels made for solids do not consecrate liquids.<br>To what does the above apply? To the liquid and dry measures that existed in the Temple which we mentioned in <i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot K'lei Hamikdash</i> 1:16-17. The rationale is that these vessels were anointed only for the sake of measuring and only for measuring the particular types of substances - liquids or solids - intended for them.</i> Receptacles,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The receptacles used to receive the blood from the sacrificial animal and then cast it on the altar.</i> by contrast, consecrate both liquids and solids.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Since they were anointed to serve as receptacles, they consecrate anything placed inside of them.</i> Sacred utensils sanctify blood that was disqualified and cause it to be offered [on the altar].
Sacred vessels only consecrate [entities] in the Temple.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">If, by contrast, an entity is placed within a sacred vessel outside the Temple Courtyard, it is not consecrated.</i> Also, they only consecrate [entities placed in them] willfully,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">And not something that fell in accidentally.</i> from their insides,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, solids are piled up over the edges of a sacred container, they are not consecrated. Note the apparent contrast to <i>Hilchot K'lei HaMikdash</i> 1:19 which states that liquid measures consecrate the overflow that drips down their sides.</i> and when intact. [The following rules apply if] a hole was made within them. If they could still be used to perform the original task for which they were used when intact, they consecrate what is placed within. If not,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the hole prevents them from being used as a container.</i> they do not consecrate [their contents].<br>They consecrate [their contents] only when they are full. The measures do not consecrate their contents when they are lacking unless one intends to fill them. If one does not intend to fill them, they consecrate their contents only in that [the contents] could [later] be disqualified,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if they remained overnight or were taken out of the Temple Courtyard.</i> but not to have them offered.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to offerings of flour and the like. As mentioned in the previous halachah, blood that is placed in such utensils is sanctified to be offered on the altar.</i>
[When an entity is placed] in a sacred vessel at an inappropriate time, it is consecrated only to the extent that it is disqualified, not that it should be offered.<br>What is implied? When an entity whose mitzvah is performed during the day is placed into a sacred vessel at night, it is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., placing it in the sacred utensil is significant - for if it was not significant, it would not have been disqualified, and would have been able to be used on the following day.</i> It should be burnt,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">As are sacred entities which became disqualified.</i> but not sacrificed. For example, if a handful is taken from a meal-offering at night and that handful is placed in a sacred vessel, it should be burnt.
When an altar became damaged,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The square of the altar must be totally intact, even a slight chip disqualifies it, as stated in <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 1:14-16; 2:18.</i> all of the sacrificial animals that were in the Temple and had been slaughtered, but whose blood had not been cast on the altar, are disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Even if the altar is repaired before sunset, the blood of these sacrifices should not be cast upon it. Since at the outset, the altar was fit to have their blood cast upon it and then there was a time when that service could not be performed, the blood is disqualified forever.</i> For there is no altar on which to cast the blood and [Exodus 20:21] states: "And you shall slaughter upon it your burnt-offerings and your peace-offerings." Implied is that when you slaughter [your offerings,] it shall be intact and not blemished.
Sacrificial animals that were alive and located in the Temple Courtyard at the time [the altar] became damaged are not disqualified. Instead, when the altar is repaired, they should be sacrificed. [The rationale is that] living animals are not deemed unacceptable forever.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">This is a concept that applies in many different contexts of the laws concerning the consecration of animals. See Chapter 4, Halachah 24; Chapter 6, Halachah 1; <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 15:4; <i>et al</i>.<br>As mentioned in the notes to <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 4:15, this represents a reversal of the Rambam's initial position on the matter.</i>
If animals were consecrated before the altar was built, they should be sacrificed after the altar was built, for the fact that they could not [be sacrificed] originally is not a disqualifying factor.
Similarly, sacrificial meat should not be eaten while the altar is damaged, as [Leviticus 10:12] states: "You shall eat it<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">The meal-offering brought as part of the dedication of the altar. As the verse states, it was considered as a sacrifice of the most holy order.</i> as unleavened bread near the altar."<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 60a explains that there is no obligation to eat sacrificial food near the altar. Rather the intent is as explained here.</i> This also applies to sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity; they should not be eaten in Jerusalem while the altar is damaged until it is repaired.

Chapter 4

[All of the following:]<br>a) the offspring of [an animal designated as] a sin-offering,<br>b) an animal exchanged for [an animal designated as] a sin-offering,<br>c)[an animal designated as] a sin-offering whose owner dies, and<br>d) such animal that was lost and then found only after the owner secured atonement,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Through offering a different animal as a sin-offering. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Temurah</i> 4:1), the Rambam mentions several conditions when an animal is consigned to death in such a situation. They are listed in Halachot 9-13.</i> should be consigned to die.<br>[In the latter instance, if the first animal designated as a sin-offering] was found after the second that was set aside was slaughtered, but before its blood was presented on the altar, there is an unresolved doubt<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The doubt arises because the person does not secure atonement until the blood is presented.</i> if it should be consigned to death or left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">When an animal has been consecrated, but is unfit to be sacrificed for various reasons, it must be redeemed before being used for ordinary purposes. Nevertheless, it may not be redeemed until it becomes disqualified as a sacrifice through contracting a physical blemish. Therefore it is left to pasture until it contracts such a blemish. During that time, it is still consecrated and it is forbidden to benefit from it.</i> Therefore<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For if it is to be consigned to death, it would be forbidden to benefit from it. Since there is a possibility that it is forbidden in this manner, it is not redeemed.</i> it should be consigned to death.<br>What is meant by being consigned to death? Not that one should kill them with a utensil or by hand. Instead, they should be brought into a room, locked inside, [and left] until they die. All of these matters were conveyed by Moses our teacher.<br>All of the above applies only to a sin-offering designated by an individual. [An animal designated as] a communal sin-offering, by contrast, which was lost and then discovered after atonement was secured - whether it is fit [to be offered as a sacrifice] or unfit<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">It became blemished or its age increased beyond that which is appropriate.</i>- should be left to pasture until it contracts a blemish and then sold.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">See footnote 3.</i> The proceeds should be used for freewill offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the money is used to buy animals that are offered as burnt-offerings at a time when the altar is free [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Shekalim</i> 4:4)].</i><br>With regard to communal sin-offerings, it is impossible to speak of offspring, exchanges, or the owners dying, because all of the communal offerings are male.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 1:15. Since they are male, there are no offspring.</i> The community cannot exchange one sacrificial animal for another as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Temurah</i> 1:1.</i> And the entire Jewish people will not die.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Temurah</i> 2:2), the Rambam cites <i>Bava Batra</i> 115b which states that an entire tribe will not die and states that how much more so does this apply to the entire Jewish people.</i>
When the bull<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrificed by the High Priest as atonement for his household and for the entire priestly family. <i>Hilchot Avodat Yom HaKippurim</i> 1:1; 4:1</i> and/or the goat<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The pair of the goat sent to Azazel. This goat is offered as a sin-offering, for the entire Jewish people (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> of Yom Kippur were lost, other [animals] were set aside instead of them [and sacrificed, and then the original animals were found], they should be left to pasture until they contract a disqualifying blemish. [Then] they should be sold and the proceeds used for freewill offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The wording used by the Rambam literally means "the proceeds should fall to a freewill offering." In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Temurah</i> 3:3, Rav Kapach's edition), the Rambam explains the meaning of that phrase. There were thirteen chests shaped like <i>shofarot</i> in the Temple. Six of them were for money to be used for freewill offerings (see <i>Hilchot Shekalim</i> 2:2). The money from such a sale would be deposited in one of these chests.</i> [The rationale is that] a communal sin-offering is never consigned to death. [This same law applies when] the goats sacrificed as [atonement for the worship of] false divinities<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 12:1.</i> are lost other [animals] were set aside instead of them [and sacrificed, and then the original animals were found].<br>Why are [the animals that were lost and then discovered] themselves not offered as freewill offerings, for they are male? This is a decree, forbidding [offering them] after atonement was achieved, [lest they be offered as freewill offerings] before atonement [was achieved].<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">For as stated in the following halachah, it is preferable that the animals originally set aside as sin-offerings be offered for that purpose instead of their replacements.</i>
[The following rules apply when a person] designated [an animal as] a sin-offering and it was lost, he designated another one instead of it, then the first one was found, and they both stood [before him]. If he took one of them and attained atonement through its [sacrifice], the other should be consigned to death.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Since he took one without questioning what should be done with the second, it is obvious that he consciously rejected the second one and is not concerned with its future. Hence it should be consigned to death (Rashi, <i>Temurah</i> 23a).<br>This ruling reflects a reversal in the Rambam's thinking. Originally [i.e., in the first version of his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Temurah</i> 4:3)], he accepted the opinion of Rav Abba who made his statements in the name of Rav. According to that view, if he sacrificed the animal that was set aside originally, the second animal that was set aside need not be consigned to death. He later changed his mind (see Rav Kapach's version of the Commentary to the Mishnah; see also the gloss of <i>Tosafot Yom Tov</i> to <i>Temurah, loc. cit.</i>) and amended his text to read as above.</i> If he asks the advice [of the court],<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">And thus shows that he is concerned about the fate of the other animal. Accordingly, it is not consigned to death.</i> he is told to gain atonement through the one set aside first. The second should be left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish. [Then] it should be sold and the proceeds used for a freewill offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 9.</i><br>If one of them was unblemished and the other had a disqualifying physical blemish, the unblemished one should be sacrificed and the blemished one should be redeemed.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">And the proceeds used for freewill offerings as above.</i> If, [after it was redeemed,] the blemished one was slaughtered before the blood of the unblemished one was cast [upon the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The act that brings about atonement.</i> it is forbidden to benefit from [the blemished one].<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This follows the opinion of Rabbi Eliezar ben Shimon (<i>Temurah</i> 24a) who maintains that once the owner receives atonement, it becomes forbidden to benefit from the second animal even if the second animal was already slaughtered. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that it is unlikely the Rambam accepted this view when it is opposed by the majority of the Sages. Hence he suggests amending the text to read: "If, [after it was redeemed,] the blemished one was slaughtered before the blood of the unblemished one was cast [upon the altar], it is permitted. [If it was slaughtered] after [the blood of the first] was cast upon the altar, it is forbidden to benefit from [the blemished one]."</i> If they were both blemished, they should both be sold, a sin-offering should be purchased from the proceeds, and the remainder used for freewill offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 5, Halachah 3.</i>
[The following rules apply when a person] designated [an animal as] a sin-offering and it was lost, he designated another one instead of it and it was [also] lost, and he designated a third. Afterwards, the lost ones were found, and all three stood [before him]. If he received atonement through the first animal [that was set aside], the second should be consigned to death and the third left to pasture [until it contracts a disqualifying blemish].<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Since the third animal does not have a direct connection with the first, the fact that the owner received atonement through the sacrifice of the first does not cause the third to be consigned to death.</i> If he received atonement through the third animal [that was set aside], the second should be consigned to death and the first left to pasture.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">For the same reason as stated in the previous note.</i> If he received atonement through the second animal [that was set aside], the first and the third should be consigned to death.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For both of these share a direct connection with the second.</i>
When a person sets aside two [animals for] a sin-offering for surety,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">So that if one is lost or becomes unacceptable, he will be able to offer the other one. Rav Yosef Corcus states that this is speaking about a situation when the person says: "One of these two should be consecrated as a sin-offering."</i> he may gain atonement through which one he desires, the second should be left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish. [Then it should be sold and] the proceeds used for a freewill offering.
When a person sets aside [an animal that] is pregnant as a sin-offering and it gives birth, it and its offspring are considered as two animals set aside for a sin-offering and as surety for it.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">And he can offer either as a sacrifice.</i>
If one set aside a sin-offering and then its year passed,<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">A lamb is not fit to be brought as a sin-offering if it is more than one year old.</i> it should be left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish. [Then] it should be sold and the proceeds used to bring another animal in its place. Similarly, if [an animal] was set aside as a sin-offering and it contracted a disqualifying blemish, [it should be sold and] the proceeds used to bring another animal in its place.
Whenever [an animal designated as] a sin-offering was lost and then discovered before [the owner] achieved atonement,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Through the offering of another sacrifice.</i> - even though when it was discovered it was blemished or its year had passed<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">And thus when it was discovered, it was no longer fit to be offered as a sacrifice. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggests that this clause is a printing error, because according to the Rambam's logic, the term "even though" is inappropriate.</i> - it is not consigned to death.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Rambam (see his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Temurah</i> 4:1-2), the determining factor is whether the animal was discovered before atonement is achieved or not. Only when it is lost at the time of atonement is it consigned to death.</i> Instead, it should be left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish. [Then it should be sold and] the proceeds used for a freewill offering.<br>If it was found after [the owner] achieved atonement, even if it was discovered when it was blemished or its year had passed,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, there is room to say that it should not be consigned to death, because perhaps it was already disqualified as a sacrifice at the time the other animal was offered. Even in such a situation, however, it is consigned to death.</i> since it was lost at the time atonement was achieved, it is consigned to death.
If [the animal] was stolen or robbed at the time atonement was achieved and afterwards returned, it is not consigned to death. Instead, it is allowed to pasture [until it becomes blemished], for all we heard from Moses our teacher was that [an animal] that was lost [should be consigned to death].<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Halachah 1, the laws applying to the consignment of an animal designated as a sin-offering to death are part of the Oral Tradition conveyed to Moses at Sinai. And all that was mentioned in that tradition was an animal that was lost.</i><br>If it first<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Temurah</i> 22b explains that this is the meaning of <i>ikar</i> in this instance.</i> was lost at night, even though it remained lost at the time atonement was achieved, it is not consigned to death.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Because at the time it was lost, it was not fit to be sacrificed, since sacrifices are not offered at night.</i> Instead, it is allowed to pasture [until it becomes blemished].
If [the owner] considered [an animal designated as a sin-offering] lost, but the shepherd did not or the shepherd considered it lost, but the owner did not, it is not consigned to death [if it is discovered after another animal was offered in place of it].<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">As long as one - the owner or the shepherd - knows of the animal's existence, it cannot truly be considered as lost.</i> Instead, it is allowed to pasture [until it becomes blemished].
All of those [animals mentioned in the above laws] that are allowed to pasture, may pasture until they contract a blemish. [Then they are sold and] the proceeds used to purchase a freewill offering.
If [the animal] was considered lost by both [the owner] and the shepherd, but another person - even in a far corner of the world - knows of its existence, there is an unresolved doubt whether it should be consigned to death.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Temurah</i> 22b leaves this question unresolved. Significantly, in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Temurah</i> 4:1), the Rambam writes that as long as one person knows of the animal's existence, it is not consigned to death.</i> Therefore it should be consigned to death.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 1.</i>
[If the animal designated as a sin-offering] was hiding behind a door or behind a stairwell, it is considered as lost, for no one will see it at the time atonement is gained. If it is in a field or in a swamp, there is a doubt whether it is lost, for perhaps there is a person who saw it at the time atonement is achieved. Therefore it is consigned to death because of the doubt.
When a person sends [an animal designated as] a sin-offering from a distant country, we sacrifice it under the assumption that he is alive.<br>When does the above apply? With regard to a sin-offering of a fowl or a sin-offering of an animal for a woman who does not perform <i>semichah</i>, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 3:5. A sin-offering for a man, by contrast, should not be brought unless he is present to perform <i>semichah</i> upon it. Although, after the fact, the sacrifice is acceptable if <i>semichah</i> is not performed, as an initial preference, one should not offer it unless that rite could be performed.</i> [Different rules apply with regard to an animal set aside as] an unconditional guilt-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The situations under which a person is required to bring such a sacrifice are described in <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 1:6.</i> [If] its owner died or received atonement,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Through the sacrifice of another animal.</i> it should be left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish. [Then it should be sold and] the proceeds used for a freewill offering. Whenever it is deemed that [an animal designated as] a sin-offering should be consigned to death, [one designated as] a guilt-offering should be left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish. [Then it should be sold and] the proceeds used for a freewill offering.
[If] any [animal set aside as] a guilt-offering that is consigned to pasture [until it contracts a disqualifying blemish] is sacrificed as a burnt offering itself,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if instead of being left to pasture, the animal was itself offered as a burnt-offering.</i> it is acceptable. Why is it not the initial preference to offer it as a burnt-offering? [This is] a decree, [using such an animal for a burnt-offering] after [its owner] gained atonement [is forbidden] lest [such an animal be used for a burnt-offering] before [the owner] gained atonement.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">For if it was discovered before the owner gained atonement through the sacrifice of another animal, the initial preference would be to sacrifice it.</i>
When a person sets aside a female [animal] for a guilt-offering in which he [is obligated], it should be left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">All guilt-offerings are male (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 1:10). Hence the animal cannot be used for the purpose for which it was consecrated.</i> [Then] it should be sold and the proceeds used for a guilt-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">For it was consecrated for that purpose.</i> If his guilt-offering was offered, the proceeds from the sale should be used to purchase a freewill offering. This also applies to her offspring.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">If the female set aside as a guilt-offering became pregnant, its offspring (even if male) should be allowed to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish and then sold and the proceeds used to purchase a freewill offering. The rationale is that the consecrated status of the offspring stems from the mother. Since the mother was not fit to be offered as a guilt-offering, the offspring also should not be used for that purpose.</i>
When a person sets aside a female [animal] for a burnt-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">All animals offered as burnt-offerings are male (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 1:8). Hence the animal could never be used for the purpose for which it was consecrated.</i> in which he [is obligated] and she gives birth to a male, [the offspring] should be left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish. [Then] it should be sold and the proceeds used for a burnt-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The offspring itself should not be offered for the reason mentioned in the notes to the previous halachah.</i>
If, however, an ordinary person<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to a king or a High Priest.</i> set aside a male [animal] for a sin-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">All the sin-offerings brought by an ordinary person are female (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 1:15).</i> a king<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This is the interpretation of the term <i>nasi</i> used by the Rambam (see <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 15:6).</i> set aside a she-goat as a sin-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of a he-goat as required.</i> or an anointed priest set aside a cow,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Instead of a bull as required.</i> these are not consecrated [at all], their physical person is not consecrated,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">So that they would be sacrificed for the stated purpose.</i> nor is their worth consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, they would have to be sold and the proceeds used to purchase a sacrifice.</i> Therefore they may be sold [even] when unblemished.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that, as stated in <i>Hilchot Temurah</i> 1:21, when an error was made in consecrating an animal as a sin-offering, it is not consecrated at all. The Ra'avad objects to this ruling based on <i>Temurah</i> 19b-20a, but states that there is a way to resolve the Rambam's perspective. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> recognizes the difficulty in the Rambam's ruling and also offers a possible resolution.</i>
[The following laws apply when a person] brought a conditional guilt-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Which must be brought when he is in doubt of whether he inadvertently committed a sin that would make him liable for a sin-offering,</i> and then discovered that he did not sin<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance he would not have to bring a sacrifice at all.</i> or that he definitely sinned.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In which instance, he would have to bring a sin-offering instead.</i> [Should he become aware of this] before the animal was slaughtered, it should be left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish. [Then it should be sold and] the proceeds used for a freewill offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since it was consecrated, it should be used for the purchase of a sacrifice. It cannot, however, be sacrificed as a guilt-offering, because the person is not obligated to bring such a sacrifice.</i> [The rationale<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since it was consecrated conditionally - i.e., because he might have sinned - when he discovers that he did not, there is room to say that the consecration is not binding. Indeed, <i>Keritot</i> 23b mentions an opinion to that effect. The Rambam does not, however, accept this view for the reasons stated.</i> is that] a person's heart feels contrite because of his sins. Since he designated [the animal as a sacrifice] because of a doubt, [we assume that] he resolved to consecrate it [regardless]. If he designated [an animal as a conditional guilt-offering] even because of witnesses<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he had no suspicions that he sinned, but witnesses told him that he performed an action that could have involved a transgression, e.g., he ate a piece of meat that could possible have contained an olive-sized portion of forbidden fat.</i> and the witnesses were disqualified through <i>hazamah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hazamah</i> refers to a situation in which other witnesses disqualify the witnesses who testified previously by stating that it was impossible for their testimony to be true, for the witnesses were together with them in a different place at the time the transgression mentioned in their testimony was performed (<i>Hilchot Edut</i> 18:2).<br>Here, also, there is room to say that the person consecrated the animal in error. Nevertheless, the rationale given previously applies in this instance as well.</i> [the above ruling applies and] the proceeds used for a freewill offering.<br>If this was discovered after [the animal] was slaughtered, the blood should be poured out<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">In the drainage channel.</i> and the meat burnt as prescribed with regard to other sacrifices that were disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 19, Halachah 1.</i> If this was discovered after the blood was cast [on the altar], the meat should be eaten by the priests like that of other guilt-offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">For the sacrifice was offered as prescribed, and from the outset, it was offered conditionally.</i>
[This law] does not apply with regard to an unconditional guilt-offering. [In that instance,] if the person became aware that he did not sin before [the animal designated as a sacrifice was slaughtered], it should be allowed to go out and pasture among the flock like an ordinary animal.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was consecrated in error, the consecration is not binding at all.</i> There is no holiness associated with it at all. If [he became aware of his innocence] after [the animal] was slaughtered, it should be buried.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it is governed by the laws pertaining to an ordinary animal that was slaughtered in the Temple Courtyard.</i> If [he became aware] after the blood was cast [on the altar], the meat should be taken to the place where [invalid sacrifices] are burnt like other sacrifices that were disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 19, Halachah 1.</i>
When a person became liable to offer a conditional guilt-offering and he set aside two [animals] as surety,<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if one will be lost, the other should be sacrificed in place of it.</i> he should gain atonement through one of them and the second should be left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish.<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 5, with regard to a sin-offering.</i> [Then] it should be sold and the proceeds used for a freewill offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Since he set aside an extra animal because he wanted to be certain that he would be able to offer a sacrifice as atonement for his sins, we assume that he desired to consecrate it under all circumstances.</i> Needless to say,<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries have questioned why the Rambam feels that an unconditional guilt-offering is a more obvious matter than a conditional guilt-offering.</i> this law applies with regard to an unconditional guilt-offering.
All of the guilt-offerings mentioned in the Torah should be brought when they are in their second year of life<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">More precisely when they are at least thirteen months old.</i> and their price should be [two] silver <i>shekalim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">As stated explicitly in Leviticus 5:15 with regard to the guilt offering that atones for the misappropriation of consecrated property. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Keritot</i> 6:6), the Rambam explains the process of exegesis through which this requirement is extended to apply to the guilt-offerings that atone for robbery and for relations with a maid-servant that was designated for another man.</i> with the exception of the guilt-offering brought by a person afflicted by <i>tzara'at</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">A skin affliction similar, but not analogous to leprosy. The obligation to bring a guilt-offering when one emerges from this impurity is stated in Leviticus 14:10-12.</i> and the guilt offering brought by a nazirite<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">When the nazirite becomes impure and shaves his head before beginning his nazirite vow again, he brings several sacrifices including a guilt-offering as stated in Numbers 6:12.</i> which should be brought in their first year of life<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">As specifically stated in the Torah.</i> and their cost has no limit.<br>A conditional guilt-offering comes from both young lambs and elder ones.<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">This rendering of the text is found in the standard printed texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> and in many reliable manuscript copies of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. It is also cited by the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>. Others maintain that the proper version is found in the early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> which reads "It is explicit that an unconditional guilt-offering is brought only from elder ones." This version is supported by the Rambam's statements in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>). Also, Leviticus 5:18 specifically states that a ram should be brought for this sacrifice.</i> According to the Oral Tradition, we learned that it be brought only from [two] silver <i>shekalim</i>.
If the price of rams decrease and a ram cannot be found for two silver <i>selaim</i>, the person has no means of correcting [his circumstance].<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">He is obligated to bring a guilt-offering and, as the Rambam proceeds to state, he may not bring such an offering for less.</i> He must wait until their price inflates and then bring one for two <i>selaim</i>, for the Torah was precise about their price and gave it an explicit limit.
If a person set aside an animal as a guilt offering which was worth [only] one <i>sela</i> at the time it was set aside, but its worth appreciated to two at the time of atonement,<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when it was sacrificed.</i> it is acceptable. For the fact that it was initially unacceptable does not make it permanently disqualified, since it was not fit to be sacrificed until it was worth two [<i>selaim</i>]. Even though it increased in value on its own,<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the market price of rams rose; it was not fattened to the extent that its value increased (Rav Yosef Corcus).</i> a person can gain atonement through the increase in value of consecrated property.<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it had already been consecrated at the time its value increased.</i><br>If it was worth two <i>selaim</i> at the time it was set aside, but its worth depreciated to one at the time of atonement, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">For at the time it is to be sacrificed, it is not worth the required amount.</i> If its value later appreciated to two, it becomes acceptable again. For living animals are never permanently disqualified, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 3, Halachah 22; <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 15:4.</i> To what can the matter be likened? To a physical blemish that was contracted, but which disappeared.
[The following rule applies when a person] set aside two <i>selaim</i> for a guilt-offering and purchased two rams for a guilt-offering with them. If one of them was worth two <i>selaim</i>, he should offer it as his guilt-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is of the required worth.</i> and the other should be left to pasture until it contracts a blemish [and then sold]. The proceeds should be used for a freewill offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Keritot</i> 6:6), the Rambam explains that this follows the general principle that if any money that was set aside for the purchase of a guilt-offering remains after the purchase of that offering, it should be used for the purchase of freewill offerings.</i>
If a person was obligated to bring a guilt-offering that was a year old,<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he was purifying himself from <i>tzara'at</i> or atoning for becoming impure while a nazirite.</i> and instead, brought one that was two years old,<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">As is obligated for the other types of guilt-offerings.</i> brought one that was a year old when he was obligated to bring one that was two years old, or brought one when the time for him to bring it had not come,<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., a nazirite must wait seven days after becoming impure to offer his sacrifice and a person who was purified from <i>tzara'at</i> must wait eight days. If these individuals sought to offer these sacrifices before this time came, they are unaceeptable.</i> it is unacceptable. It should be [left] until the next day<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">We have translated the term used by the Rambam according to its halachic intent. The literal meaning is that it should be left long enough to decompose until it loses the appearance of meat. Our Sages (see <i>Pesachim</i> 34b, <i>et al</i>; Rashi, <i>Menachot</i> 46b) understood that as being a twenty-four hour period.</i> and then it should be taken to the place where sacrifices are burnt.<br>This is the general principle: Any factor that disqualifies a sin-offering disqualifies a guilt-offering except a guilt-offering that was slaughtered with the intent that it was another sacrifice, which is acceptable, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 15, Halachah 1. A sin-offering, by contrast, is unacceptable if slaughtered with the intent that it was another sacrifice.</i>
When a burnt-offering that must be brought by a nazirite,<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">When he completes his nazirite vow, as stated in Numbers 6:14; <i>Hilchot Nizirut</i> 8:1.</i> a woman who gives birth,<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The obligation for a woman to bring a burnt-offering after childbirth is mentioned in Leviticus 12:6; <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i> 1:3..</i> or a person who is being purified after <i>tza'arat</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus 14:10, 20; <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah, op. cit.</i></i> was slaughtered when it was more than twelve months old or the time for the owner to bring it had not come,<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">See note 88 with regard to the nazirite and the person purified after <i>tzara'at</i>. A woman who gave birth must wait 40 or 80 days before bringing a sacrifice as explained in the passage from Leviticus.</i> it is acceptable<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the sacrifice is acceptable. The person bringing it, however, has not satisfied his obligation and is required to bring another offering.</i> and its accompanying offerings are required to be brought.<br>This is the general principle: Any factor that does not disqualify a burnt offering brought willingly does not disqualify a burnt-offering that is obligatory regardless of whether the one bringing it is considered to have fulfilled his obligation or not.

Chapter 5

When a person sets aside money for a sin-offering and dies, the money should be [thrown] to the Mediterranean Sea.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The ruling is comparable to that governing an animal set aside as a sin-offering whose owner died mentioned in Chapter 4, Halachah 1. Since this money was set aside to be used for a sin-offering, it may not be used for any other purpose. Hence, it should be cast in a place where no one will benefit from it. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Temurah</i> 4:2).<br>Although the term <i>Yam HaMelech</i> is generally translated as "the Dead Sea," it literally means "the Salt Sea." In several places in his Commentary to the Mishnah, however, the Rambam interprets the term as referring to the Mediterranean.</i> Similarly, when a person sets aside money for a sin-offering, the money is lost, another sin-offering is offered in its place, and then the [initial] money is found after atonement was achieved, [the money should be thrown] to the Mediterranean Sea.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., this ruling is comparable to that governing an animal set aside as a sin-offering which was lost and the owner attained atonement through the sacrifice of another animal, as stated in Chapter 4, <i>op. cit.</i></i>
When a person sets aside money for a sin-offering, the money is lost, and he set aside other money in its stead, but did not have the opportunity to purchase a sin-offering with the later funds before the first funds were found, he should purchase a sin-offering with the combined funds.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Since both sets of money were set aside for the purchase of a sin-offering, they should be used primarily for that purpose. It is preferable to purchase a more expensive animal for a sin-offering than to have more money left over to purchase burnt-offerings.</i> The remainder should be used for freewill offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Since the money was not used as of yet and the person did not yet gain atonement, any funds that remain can be used for another purpose.</i>
When a person sets aside money for a sin-offering, the money is lost, and he set aside [an animal] as a sin-offering in its stead, but before he sacrificed it, the [original] funds were found and the [animal was discovered] to have a disqualifying physical blemish, it should be sold and he should purchase a sin-offering with the combined funds.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For the same reasoning as in the previous halachah.</i> The remainder should be used for freewill offerings.
When a person set aside [an animal] as a sin-offering, it was lost, he set aside money in its stead, but was not able to purchase [an animal] as a sin-offering with it before the [first] animal was found, but it [was discovered] to have a disqualifying physical blemish, it should be sold and he should purchase a sin-offering with the combined funds. The remainder should be used for freewill offerings.
If he set aside two piles of money for surety [that he will be able to purchase a sin-offering], he should gain atonement with one of them<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">He should not, combine the two to purchase a single offering.</i> and the other should be used for freewill offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 4, Halachah 5.</i>
If he set aside [an animal for] a sin-offering or the money for a sin-offering, because he thought that he was obligated [to bring one] and then he discovered that he was not so obligated, [the animal or the money] is of ordinary status; it is not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For the consecration was made in error and hence is not binding. See Chapter 4, Halachah 20.</i><br>If he set aside two [animals for] sin-offerings or the money for two [animals] because he thought he was obligated to bring both and then it was discovered that he was only liable to bring one, he should bring one as a sacrifice and the other should be used for freewill offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., with regard to money, the money should be used to purchase freewill offerings. With regard to an animal, the animal should be left to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish. It should then be sold and the proceeds used for freewill offerings (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). Since he did not specify which of the animals should be associated with the particular sin and he is liable for one sin-offering, the remaining animal is not considered to have been consecrated in error (Rav Yosef Corcus).</i>
When he picks up coins in his hand or was in the process of collecting them and said: "I will bring my sin-offering from these," the remainder are not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Since he said, "from these," the implication is that all of the coins were not consecrated, only those necessary to purchase the animal for the sin-offering.</i> Similarly, it appears to me<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">This phrase introduces a conclusion drawn by the Rambam through logic that is not based on any explicit prior Rabbinic source. The rationale is that when bringing a sin-offering it is likely that the person feels remorse and is willing to give more to attain atonement. Nevertheless, none of the extra money is consecrated. It follows logically that this principle should also apply with regard to a freewill offering in which instance the donor may not be as powerfully motivated.</i> that an inference can be drawn [from this ruling] to [similar situations involving] other sacrifices and the remainder are not considered as consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad accepts the Rambam's conclusion with regard to sacrifices that one is obligated to bring, but differs with regard to burnt-offerings and peace-offerings that one gives of his own volition. In those instances, he maintains that the extra money should be used for those sacrifices. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, however, substantiates the Rambam's approach.</i>
When a person sets aside money for a meal-offering of a sinner<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 12:4; <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 10:4.</i> and brings a meal-offering from those funds, or he set aside a meal-offering itself, and [in either of the above instances, money] was left over, the remainder should be used to bring a freewill meal-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Note the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Shekalim</i> 2:5) which states that since we are speaking about what remains after bringing a sin-offering, it should be used to bring freewill burnt-offerings.</i> Any extra [meal<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in this and the following situations, the person required to bring a meal-offering brought more than the required amount.</i> left after] the tenth of the <i>ephah</i> that is brought by the High Priest as his <i>chavitin</i> offering<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 13:2.</i> should be left to rot.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">For the concept that the remainder of what was set aside should be used for burnt-offerings was stated with regard to sin-offerings and not these types of sacrifices (<i>Menachot</i> 108a).</i> Similarly, the remainder [of meal left after preparing] the bread for a thanksgiving offering or the bread for a nazirite's offering<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 9:5 which states that the bread are integral parts of these two types of offerings.</i> should be left to rot.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that these breads are not considered as independent sacrifices, but as elements of the thanksgiving or nazirite offerings. Hence they were consecrated - and may only be used for - those sacrifices.</i><br>The remainder of money for wine libations should be used for freewill offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">For wine libations are sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity. Hence the laws governing them are the same as those governing sin-offerings and guilt-offerings.</i> We have already explained in <i>[Hilchot] Shekalim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Shekalim</i> 3-13.</i> that any money remaining from the half-<i>shekalim</i> are considered as ordinary funds.
[Any money] left over [from that set aside to purchase] pairs of doves for <i>zavim, zavot</i> or for women after childbirth,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The requirement for these individuals to bring doves as sacrifices is mentioned in <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i> 1:3.</i> sin-offerings, or guilt-offerings should be used for freewill offerings. They should be offered as burnt-offerings, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 2:2-3, and in several instances in this and the previous chapter.</i><br>[Any money] left over [from that set aside to purchase] burnt-offerings should be used for burnt-offerings, [to purchase] peace-offerings, for peace-offerings, [to purchase] meal-offerings, for meal-offerings, [to purchase] a Paschal sacrifice, for peace-offerings,<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">For they are both sacrifices of a lesser degree of holiness [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Shekalim</i> 2:5)].</i> [to purchase] nazirite-offerings, for nazirite-offerings,<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Nizirut</i> 9:1 which states: "[The following rules apply when a person] sets aside money for the sacrifices of [poor] nazirites, those sacrifices were offered, and there is money left over. He should bring sacrifices of other nazirites with those funds." In his Commentary to the Mishnah, <i>op. cit.</i>, the Rambam interprets this teaching as referring to nazirites who desire to pool their offerings.</i> [to purchase] the offerings for a particular nazirite, for [freewill] offerings to be brought by that nazirite.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Since the money was designated for the offerings of that particular person, it cannot be used for the sacrifices of another nazirite.</i><br>When does the ruling that [money] left over [from that set aside to purchase] a sin-offering should be used for freewill offerings apply? With regard to a fixed sin-offering. When, however, one was obligated to bring an adjustable guilt offering<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 1:3 and ch. 10, which explains that there are certain transgressions for which the atonement offering required varies according to the transgressor's financial capacity.</i> and set aside money for a sin-offering of an animal and became poor, he should bring a fowl instead.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">As obligated of a person who violated these sins but did not have the means to purchase an animal as a sacrifice.</i> He may transfer the holiness from those funds to the fowl and benefit from them.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Keritot</i> 27b derives this law through a process of Biblical exegesis.</i> Similarly, if he set aside money for a fowl and became poor, he should bring a [meal-offering] of a tenth of an <i>ephah</i> instead.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">As obligated of a person who violated these sins but did not have the means to purchase doves as a sacrifice.</i> He may transfer the holiness from those funds to [the meal] and benefit from them.
If a person set aside an animal [for an adjustable guilt-offering], it contracted a disqualifying physical blemish, [and he became poor],<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The bracketed additions are based on <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 10:11.</i> it may be sold and the proceeds used to purchase a fowl.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the animal had already been purchased for the sacrifice, since it was disqualified and his status changed, he is allowed to use the proceeds from its sale to bring a lesser offering.</i> If, however, he set aside a fowl [for such an offering], it was disqualified, [and he became poor], he may not sell it and bring a tenth of an <i>ephah</i> from the proceeds of the sale, for a fowl [that was consecrated] may not be redeemed, as stated in <i>[Hilchot] Issurei HaMizbeiach</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 6:4. This applies only to a fowl itself. Money set aside for a fowl may be redeemed, as stated in the previous halachah.</i>
[The following rules apply to] all those obligated by the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the individuals mentioned at the beginning of Halachah 9.</i> to bring pairs of doves<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, a pair of doves includes one to be offered as a burnt-offering and one as a sin-offering.</i> who set aside money for those doves. If they desired to use all the money for sin-offerings of fowl alone, they may.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The person must, however, bring another dove(s) for the other burnt- or sin-offerings, he or she is obligated to bring.</i> If they desire to use it for burnt-offerings of fowl, they may. Even if they had [originally] said: "This is the money for my sin-offering and this is the money for my burnt-offering," he may mix the money together and buy the two offerings together, or use the money solely for sin-offerings, or solely for burnt-offerings. [The rationale is that the identity of] pairs of doves is designated only when purchased by the owners or when offered by a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if the owners specify which doves are to be offered for which specific offering, the fowl is designated for that purpose. Alternatively, if such a distinction was not made, they become designated by the priest at the time he offers them (<i>Keritot</i> 28a; see Chapter 8, Halachah 8).</i>
Therefore if one set aside money for a pair of doves without making a determination and died, all of the money that was undetermined should be used for freewill offerings. [The rationale is that] it is all fit to used for a burnt-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Were, however, the fowl to have been designated for sin-offerings, they would be consigned to die, as evident from Halachah 1. See also the previous chapter.</i>
[The following laws apply when someone] was obligated to bring a sin-offering and he said: "I pledge a burnt-offering," and set aside money saying: "This is for my obligation." If he desires, he may use them to bring an animal as a sin-offering or he may use them to bring an animal as a burnt-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs and, based on <i>Nazir</i> 27a, maintains that the text should read: "He should not bring a sin-offering.... He should not bring a burnt-offering." He also explains that this version is preferable, for, otherwise, there would be no reason why the money mentioned in the following clause should be consigned to be destroyed. Seemingly, there is no difference between that clause and the situation mentioned in the previous halachah.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> maintains that the Rambam had a different version of that text. Because of the difficulties, the Ra'avad raised, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggests a third version: If he desires to bring a sin-offering, he should. If he desires to bring a burnt-offering, he should not.</i> If he died and left the money, it should be taken to the Mediterranean Sea.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Rambam, since he has an obligation to bring a sin-offering and did not clarify his intent, we must accept the possibility that the money is associated with a sin-offering and must be done away with, as stated in Halachah 1.</i>

Chapter 6

If one of [the animals designated as] a sin-offering that was consigned to death or an ox that was condemned to be stoned<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">For killing a human; see Exodus 21:29-32; <i>Hilchot Nizkei Mammon</i>, ch. 10. As stated in <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 3:6, such an animal is unfit for sacrifice on the altar. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 8:1), the Rambam states that, in this context, the term also applies to an ox that was sodomized by - or forced to participate in sexual relations with - a Jew, for it is also condemned to be executed.</i> becomes intermingled with any other sacrificial animals - even in a ratio of one to a myriad - they should all be consigned to death.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">For it is possible that every animal is that animal condemned to die.</i> [The rationale is that] living animals are important and are never considered insignificant [in a mixture].<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., according to Scriptural Law, a forbidden substance is considered as insignificant if mixed with a larger volume of permitted substances (<i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 15:1). Even according to Rabbinic Law, it is considered insignificant if mixed with more than sixty times its volume in most situations (<i>ibid.</i>:5). Nevertheless, this situation is an exception for the reason stated by the Rambam.</i><br>If [the animals] were sacrificed,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For their designated purpose.</i> [the sacrifices] are acceptable, because living animals are never permanently disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general principle applicable in many contexts, as stated in Chapter 4, Halachah 24; Chapter 3, Halachah 22; and <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 15:4. Thus the fact that as an initial preference the animal should not have been sacrificed is not significant, for according to Scriptural Law, the presence of the forbidden animal is nullified. Hence, after the fact, the sacrifice is acceptable.</i>
If [sacrificial animals] became intermingled with [animals that are] forbidden to be offered on the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., animals that were set aside for sacrifice to false deities, an animal given to a prostitute, or one exchanged for a dog. See a full listing of such animals in <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 3:11.</i> they should all be allowed to pasture until they contract disqualifying physical blemishes. They should then be sold and the proceeds from the sale of the choicest animal among them<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">For perhaps the sacrificial animal was the most choice.</i> should be used to bring sacrifices from the type in which [the forbidden animal] became intermingled.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">After selling the animals, the person obligated to bring the offerings must say: "The holiness of the sacrificial animal is transferred to these funds" and with those funds, he should purchase a new sacrificial animal.</i>
If sacrificial animals become intermingled with unblemished ordinary animals, the ordinary animals in the mixture should be sold as sacrifices of that type, and they should all be sacrificed.<br>What is implied? If four animals that were designated as peace-offerings became intermingled with four unblemished ordinary animals, the four ordinary animals<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the owner does not know which four animals they are, he may sell them (Rav Yosef Corcus). To avoid the difficulty mentioned in the following halachah, however, the owners must specify which animals are being given to the purchaser.</i> should be sold to someone who is obligated to bring peace-offerings and they should all be offered as peace-offerings. Similar laws apply with regard to burnt-offerings and guilt-offerings.<br>The proceeds of the sale are considered as ordinary money, for they are the proceeds of the sale of ordinary animals.
When an ox that was consecrated became intermingled with ordinary animals, the largest among them is considered as the consecrated one<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">And should be sacrificed for the purpose for which the animal was consecrated originally.</i> and the others should be sold for sacrifices of that type.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">For it is possible that any one of them is the consecrated animal.</i> If sacrifices of the most sacred order became intermingled with animals consecrated for the same purpose,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., burnt-offerings with burnt-offerings.</i> each one should be offered for the sake of its owner, even though none [of the owners] recognize their sacrificial animal.<br>When does the above apply? With regard to sacrifices to be brought by women in which there is no obligation for <i>semichah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 3:5</i> With regard to sacrifices to be brought by men, since each one of them is obligated to perform <i>semichah</i> on his sacrifice,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Although, after the fact, the sacrifice is acceptable if <i>semichah</i> is not performed, as an initial preference, one should not offer it unless that rite could be performed.</i> these animals should not be offered until each one gives his portion [in the sacrificial animal] to his colleague<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Rabbi Akiva Eiger questions the Rambam's statements here, noting that in <i>Hilchot Meilah</i> 4:8, the Rambam rules that a person cannot sell an animal set aside as a peace-offering or as a burnt-offering. By the same reasoning, it would seem that it would be forbidden to make the exchange mentioned here.</i> or until they all become blemished and are sold. [In that instance,] each one should then bring a sacrificial animal equal in value to the more select of that type.
If [sacrificial animals of] two [different] types become intermingled, e.g., a peace-offering with a burnt-offering, they should not be sacrificed, even as the more sacred.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in the example given above, to offer both of them as a burnt-offering.</i> [The rationale is that] we should not cause sacrificial animals to become disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See the explanation of this principle in Halachah 12. This rationale is given by <i>Zevachim</i> 8:3 with regard to a peace-offering and a guilt-offering that became mixed together, for it is possible that the meat of one of the animals will not be finished during the first night and will therefore be disqualified, lest it be that of the guilt-offering. This is undesirable, because perhaps it is from the peace-offering and thus it will be disqualified, before its appropriate time (for peace-offerings may be eaten on the following day as well).<br>This explanation of this concept is slightly different for a mixture of a peace-offering and a burnt-offering, since a burnt-offering is not eaten at all. Instead, in that instance, offering the peace-offering as a burnt-offering is forbidden, based on <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 5:4 which states that it is forbidden to offer the meat of sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity on the altar. As a result, the meat of the sacrifice is disqualified.</i>
Just as we may not reduce the time [in which sacrifices] may be eaten,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">As explained in the previous note. See also <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 10:12.</i> so too, we may not limit the people eligible to partake of them,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">For example, a firstborn offering which may be eaten only by priests, became mixed with a tithe offering that can be eaten be anyone.</i> nor the place where they can be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrifices of the most sacred order may be eaten only in the Temple Courtyard, while sacrifices of lesser sanctity may be eaten throughout the city of Jerusalem.</i> Instead,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., for this reason, we do not merely take one animal for one type of sacrifice and another for the other.</i> what should be done? All [of the sacrificial animals of mixed identity] should be allowed to pasture until they contract a disqualifying physical blemish. Then each one should be sold individually. He should purchase a sacrifice for each type of the value of the most choice animal. He must suffer the loss<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The difference between the price of the most choice animal and the other animal.</i> from his own resources.
Even though the person already sacrificed the burnt-offering or the peace-offering which he was obligated to bring,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Using another animal so that he will not delay the fulfillment of his obligation.</i> he should nevertheless bring a different burnt-offering and peace-offering from the proceeds [of the sale] of the mixture.
When a sin-offering becomes intermingled with a peace-offering, [the two] should be allowed to pasture until they contracted disqualifying physical blemishes and were redeemed. He should bring a peace-offering of the value of the most choice animal and a sin-offering of the value of the most choice animal.
