[In all the following instances,] a meal-offering [is unacceptable]:<br>it was not placed in a sacred utensil,<br>the handful of meal was not placed in a sacred utensil,<br>it was brought to the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 12:12 with regard to bringing the meal-offering to the altar.</i> while not in a sacred utensil,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 26a elaborates on the necessity of using a sacred utensil for each of these stages of service.</i><br>or it was mixed with oil outside the Temple Courtyard; [it is acceptable] only if it was mixed with oil in the Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Although it need not be mixed with oil by a priest, it must be mixed in the Temple Courtyard (<i>Menachot</i> 9b).</i>
All of the meal-offerings are acceptable even if oil was poured over them by someone who was unacceptable for Temple service, e.g., a non-priest or the like, or such a person mixed [the oil with the meal], broke [wafers] into pieces,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">As required for certain meal-offerings; see Leviticus 2:6.</i> or put salt upon them. If [such a person] approached the altar with them or waved them,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 7:12 which describes the waving process which is necessary for certain meal-offerings.</i> a priest should approach the altar with them and/or wave them again. If a priest did not approach the altar with them and/or wave them again, they are [nevertheless] acceptable. [This is derived from Leviticus 2:2:] "And he shall bring it to the sons of Aaron and [one] shall take a handful..." From taking the handful and onward, the mitzvah must be performed by a priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 12:23.</i> Pouring and mixing [the oil] may be performed by a non-priest.
When even the slightest amount of oil from another meal-offering or oil that was not consecrated falls into a meal-offering, it is disqualified. If [the full measure<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">One <i>log</i> for every <i>isaron</i> (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 12:7).</i> of] its oil is lacking, it is disqualified. If, [by contrast, the full measure<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A handful per offering (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> of] its frankincense is lacking, it is acceptable provided there are at least two particles of frankincense upon it. If there is only one particle, it is disqualified, as it is written:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">There is no Biblical phrase using the exact wording employed by the Rambam. <i>Menachot</i> 11b derives the concept stated by the Rambam from Leviticus 6:8. Leviticus 2:2 uses a phrase very close to that cited by the Rambam.</i> "on all its frankincense."<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The use of a plural term indicates that one particle is not sufficient.</i>
If he added to its [measure of] oil and frankincense, [including] up to two <i>lugim</i> for every <i>isaron</i> and [up to] two handfuls of frankincense for every meal offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Double the usual measure.</i> it is acceptable. If one uses two <i>lugim</i> or two handfuls or more, it is disqualified.
If one placed oil on the meal offering of a sinner<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Concerning which Leviticus 5:11 states: "You shall not place upon it oil, nor shall you place upon it frankincense." See also <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 12:7.</i> or on the handful of meal taken from it, it is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Because of the transgression involved.</i> If one placed frankincense on it, it should be gathered up.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Since the frankincense can be removed, the offering is not disqualified.</i> If [the frankincense] is ground, [the offering] is unacceptable because of the doubt, because it is impossible to gather [the frankincense].
If one placed oil on the remnants [of such a meal-offering] after the handful was removed, he is not liable for lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">For the violation of the above prohibition.</i> nor does he disqualify the offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording appears to imply that as an initial preference, one should not place oil on these remnants. Nevertheless, from other sources, it would seem that there is no difficulty in doing so.</i> for the handful is acceptable.
If he placed even the smallest amount of oil<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Either ordinary oil or oil from another meal-offering.</i> on an olive-sized portion<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For anything less than an olive-sized portion is not halachically significant.</i> of the meal-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Before the handful of meal is removed.</i> he disqualifies it because of the doubt involved. If, however, he placed oil on less than an olive-sized portion, he does not disqualify it. One does not disqualify [a meal-offering] with frankincense<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when ground, as stated in Halachah 10. Our translation is based on authentic manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The standard printed text has a slight error.</i> unless he places an olive-sized portion<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For here also anything less is not considered significant.</i> [on the offering].
Even if he placed frankincense on the smallest portion of the meal-offering, he disqualifies it until he gathers it.
If one mixed water with the meal and then took a handful, it is acceptable. [The Torah's requirement<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 7:10 speaks of a meal-offering "mixed with oil or that is dry."</i> that the offering be] "dry" [refers only] to oil.
A meal-offering from which a handful was taken twice - or many times - is acceptable, provided an olive-sized portion is offered on the altar's pyre at once. For no less than an olive-sized portion may be offered on the altar.
If one offered the handful [of meal<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The remainder of the offering, however, need not be salted.</i> on the altar] without salt, it is unacceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Although <i>Menachot</i> 18a states: "If salt was not placed on it, it is acceptable," it is explained (<i>ibid.</i> 20a): "If a priest did not salt it, but a non-priest did."</i> for the salt is an absolute requirement for a meal-offering, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 5:12. As stated there, this is a severity that applies to the meal-offerings and not to other sacrifices.</i> When a meal-offering was lacking<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it must contain at least an <i>isaron</i>, as stated in <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 12:5.</i> before the handful was taken, he should bring [more meal] from home and complete the measure. For taking the handful is what defines [the meal as] an offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Hence it must be complete at that time.</i> not placing it into a sacred vessel.
When a person donates a handful<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This is the minimum size of the offering, as stated in <i>ibid.</i> 16:13.</i> of frankincense independently, it is unacceptable if it is lacking at all. Similarly, if the two bowls of frankincense that accompany the [show]bread<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 5:2.</i> are lacking even the slightest amount, they are unacceptable. They must contain two handfuls from the beginning until the end.
[The following rules apply when a] person set aside two handfuls [of frankincense] for one offering<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Doubling the minimum requirement.</i> and one was lost. If this occurred before the handful [of meal] was taken, [the association between them and this offering] was not [yet] established.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the offering is acceptable.</i> If it occurred afterwards, [the association] has been established and [the offering] is unacceptable, because he increased its frankincense.<br>Similar [laws apply if one] sets aside four handfuls for the two bowls of the [show]bread<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Doubling the minimum requirement.</i> and two were lost. If this occurred before the bowls were removed [from the showbread],<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">On the afternoon of the Sabbath, before the showbreads are replaced by new breads, the bowls of frankincense are removed and the frankincense offered on the altar.</i> [the association between them] was not established and they are acceptable. If it occurred after the bowls were removed, [the association] was established and they are disqualified, because of the extra amount.
When the handful taken from a meal offering became impure and then it was offered on the altar's pyre,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is speaking after the fact. As an initial preference, once the handful of meal becomes impure, it should not be offered.</i> the High Priest's forehead plate causes it to be considered acceptable, as [Exodus 28:38] states: "And Aaron shall bear [the iniquity....]"<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 1, Halachot 34-35; <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 4:7; <i>Hilchot Me'ilah</i> 3:9, <i>et al</i>.</i><br>If the handful was taken outside the Temple Courtyard and then brought in and offered on the altar's pyre, the forehead plate does not cause it to be considered acceptable. For the forehead plate causes [sacrifices tainted by] impurity to be considered acceptable, but not those which are taken outside the Temple Courtyard.
If one took the handful from a meal-offering and then the entire remainder [of the offering] became impure,<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 26a derives this from a comparison to the laws regarding offering the blood on the altar when the meat of a sacrifice became impure or otherwise disqualified.</i> was burnt, was taken out of the Temple Courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Which causes the meal-offering to be disqualified.</i> or was lost, the handful should not be offered on the altar's pyre. [After the fact,] if it was offered, it is accepted [Above].<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">And the person who brought it is considered to have fulfilled his obligation.</i> If a small amount of the remnants [of the offering] remained acceptable, the handful should be offered. [Nevertheless,] the remnant that remains is forbidden to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 9b derives this concept from Leviticus 2:3: "The remainder of the meal-offering shall be for Aaron and his sons." Implied is that the priests should receive the remainder of the offering and not the remainder of the remnants.</i>
If there was a divider in the lower portion of a vessel containing an <i>isaron</i> [of flour] for a meal-offering, even though [the flour] is mixed together above, one should not take a handful.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">For the handful must be taken from an <i>isaron</i> of flour and since there is a division in the container, it is considered as if the <i>isaron</i> was brought in two containers which is unacceptable (<i>Menachot</i> 24a).</i> If one did, it is unacceptable.
If, [by contrast,] the container was separated by a divider above, but [the contents] were mixed together below, one may take a handful from it.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Since the flour is mixed together below, it is considered to be a single entity.</i>
If one divided the <i>isaron</i> in a single container and thus the portions were not touching each other, but there was no divider between them, there is an unresolved doubt whether the container causes [the two portions to be considered as] combined or not. Therefore, [at the outset,] one should not take the handful [in such an instance].<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Since the question was not resolved, one should not attempt to bring the sacrifice in this manner.</i> If one did take the handful, it should not be offered on the altar's pyre. If it was offered, it is accepted [Above], but the remainder [of the offering] should not be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries have not found an explicit source for this ruling. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that it is derived from the Halachah 20 above.</i>
If one took a handful [of flour from a meal-offering] and placed the handful on the top of the [Golden] Table [elevated to] the height of the arrangement of the showbread,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">15 handbreadths, for there are six showbreads in each arrangement and each one is two and one half handbreadths high.</i> the Table causes it to be sanctified in that it can be disqualified,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., if it remains overnight, is taken out of the Temple Courtyard, or the like. Beforehand, it could not be disqualified for those reasons. From the Rambam's wording, one can infer that placing the handful of meal on the table does not disqualify the handful entirely and if it is gathered and placed in a sacred vessel, it may be placed on the altar's pyre (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> but it does not sanctify it so that it can be offered. For [the handful of flour] should not be sacrificed until it was sanctified in a sacred vessel fit for the handful [of flour].
If he attached the handful to the wall of the vessel and took it or overturned the vessel above his hand and took the handful while the opening of the vessel was turned downward, it should not be offered on the altar's pyre.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 11a questions whether these situations are acceptable and leaves the matter unresolved.</i> If it was offered, it is accepted [Above].
[The following rules apply when] an <i>isaron</i> was divided and one of its portions were lost, another portion was set aside in its stead, the lost portion was found and the three are all placed in one container, but are not touching each other. If the portion that was lost becomes impure, it is combined with the first portion and they are disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale for the ruling is that their presence in a common container causes the different elements of an offering to be considered as one, even if they are not touching (<i>Chagigah</i> 20b; <i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTuma'ah</i> 12:7). Hence, since these two portions were originally part of the same offering and they are now in the same container, the first part is also disqualified.</i> The portion that was set aside [as a replacement] is not combined with them<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Since these two portions were never planned to be offered together, they do not share a halachic connection.</i> and it should be supplemented [to produce a full <i>isaron</i>].<br>If the portion set aside [as a replacement] becomes impure, it and the first portion are combined and disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">For the portion set aside as a replacement and the original portion were intended to serve as a single offering.</i> The portion that was [lost and] discovered is not combined with them.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">For as mentioned, it and the replacement have no intrinsic connection.</i> If the initial portion becomes impure, both the portion that was lost and the portion set aside because of it are combined with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">For they both share a connection with it.</i>
Similar concepts apply with regard to taking the handful. If one took the handful from the portion that was [lost and then] discovered, the remainder of it and the first portion<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">In its entirety.</i> may be eaten and the portion that was set aside [afterwards] may not be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Instead, another portion should be combined with it and a second meal-offering brought (<i>Zevach Todah</i>).</i> If one took the handful from the portion that was set aside [afterwards] the remainder of it and the first portion may be eaten and the portion that was [lost and then] discovered may not be eaten. If one took the handful from the first portion, neither [of the other] two may be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The remainder of the first portion may, however, be eaten, because the handful is acceptable.</i> [The rationale is that] they are both extra portions.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">As the Rambam continues to explain, taking the handful from a meal-offering enables the remainder of the <i>isaron</i> from which it is taken to be eaten. It, however, only allows an <i>isaron</i> to be eaten, not more. Thus the two portions could not be eaten because when brought together, the three would comprise more than an <i>isaron</i>.</i> For together they are an entire <i>isaron</i> and thus resemble an entire <i>isaron</i> from which a handful was not taken and which is hence, forbidden.<br>[One might ask:] How can the handful [that was taken] be offered, since there is an <i>isaron</i> and a half [in the vessel]? Because taking the handful is dependent on the intent of the priest and when he takes the handful, he has his mind on an <i>isaron</i> alone and the portions [of the <i>isaron</i>] are not touching each other.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Since each portion is distinct from the other, it is possible for the priest to be focused on two, but not three.</i>
When a handful [taken from] a meal-offering became mixed with a handful [taken from] another meal-offering, they should both be offered on the altar's pyre together and they are acceptable. Similarly, it is acceptable if a handful [taken from a meal-offering] became mixed with a meal-offering of a priest,<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 12:9.</i> the meal-offering from an accompanying offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>ibid.</i> 2:1.</i> or the <i>chavitin</i> offering of the High Priest.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 12:9.</i> They should be offered on the altar's pyre together. [The rationale is that all of these substances] are offered on [the altar's] fire in their entirety.
[The following rules apply when] two meal-offerings from which a handful had not been separated become mixed together. If [the priest] can remove a handful from each one separately, they are acceptable. If not, they are disqualified.
When a handful [taken from a meal-offering] becomes mixed together with a meal-offering from which a handful had not been taken, it should not be offered on the altar's pyre.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., one might desire to offer the entire mixture so that he will have fulfilled his obligation to offer the handful. This, however, is undesirable for one will have offered a meal-offering without separating the handful from it.</i> If one did offer the entire mixture, the owner of [the offering] from which the handful was taken is considered to have fulfilled his obligation and the owner of the one from which the handful was not taken is not considered to have fulfilled his obligation.
If the handful [taken from a meal-offering] becomes mixed together with the remaining portions of the offering or the remaining portions become mixed together with the remaining portions of another meal-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation reflects the version in the standard published texts of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> even though many commentaries have questioned it and have suggested that the text should read: "or [the handful] became mixed with the remaining portion of another meal-offering." This version appears preferable, for seemingly, even if two offerings become mixed together, if their handfuls have already been separated, why shouldn't the handfuls be offered? Halachah 29 apparently leads to such a conclusion. Nevertheless, we did not correct the text in this fashion, for the authoritative manuscripts and early printings employ the same version as the standard printed text. Moreover, the Rambam's text of the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 3:3) also contains such statements.</i> it<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">In the first instance, this refers to the mixture of the handful and the remainder. In the second instance, according to the standard version of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, it refers to the handful for the remainder that became intermingled with another remainder.</i> should not be offered.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, when a forbidden substance becomes mixed together with a permitted substance of the same type, the forbidden entity becomes <i>betal</i> - it is considered nullified because it is a tiny proportion of the mixture. Nevertheless, in this instance, <i>Menachot</i> 23b quotes a textual association to prove that the handful does not become <i>betal</i> to the remainder of the offering.</i> If it was offered, the owner is considered to have fulfilled his obligation.

Chapter 12

If one adds to or subtracts from the measure for the two loaves [offered on Shavuot],<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 8:1.</i> the showbread,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 5:1.</i> and the <i>omer</i> that is waved,<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">And offered on the day following Pesach; <i>ibid.</i> 7:12; <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 12:5.</i> they are disqualified.
With regard to the loaves for the thanksgiving-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>ibid.</i> 9:17-22.</i> and the cakes of a nazirite<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i>:23.</i> that lacked [the required measure], before the blood from the sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The animal offered with the loaves.</i> was cast on the altar, they are unacceptable. If the blood was already cast on the altar, they are acceptable.
Similarly, with regard to the two loaves, if they lacked [the required measure], before the blood from the sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">The communal peace-offerings brought on Shavuot.</i> was cast on the altar, they are unacceptable. If the blood was already cast on the altar, they are acceptable.
And with regard to the showbread, if they lacked [the required measure], before the the bowls of frankincense were placed on the altar's pyre,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Offering the frankincense is thus equivalent to offering the blood on the altar. See also Chapter 11, Halachah 17.</i> it is unacceptable. If the bowls of frankincense were already placed on the altar's pyre, it is acceptable.
With regard to the accompanying offerings that lacked [the required measure]: whether the sacrifice was offered or not, they are acceptable, but other accompanying offerings must be brought to complete [the required measure].
[The following rules apply when] accompanying offerings were sanctified in a sacred vessel and then the sacrifice was disqualified. If it was disqualified through ritual slaughter, the accompanying offerings have not been sanctified so that they must be offered.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In all instances, however, they are considered sanctified to the extent that they must be kept overnight and then destroyed by fire.</i> If it was disqualified from the reception of the blood and onward,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, the intent is that even the reception of the blood was not performed in an acceptable manner. See the following note.</i> the accompanying offerings have become sanctified, because what sanctifies the accompanying offerings so that they should be offered is solely the slaughter of the sacrifice [in an acceptable manner].<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Hence since the sacrifices were slaughtered in an acceptable manner, the accompanying offerings should be offered on the altar. The Ra'avad notes that this ruling is the subject of a difference opinion between our Sages in <i>Menachot</i> 79a. Rabbi Elazer ben Shimon maintains that for an accompany offering to be offered, the blood of the sacrifice must be received in an acceptable manner. Rabbi Yehudah <i>HaNasi</i> differs and maintains that as long as the slaughter is acceptable, even if the blood was not received in an acceptable manner, the accompanying offering should be offered.<br>The Ra'avad maintains that the Rambam follows Rabbi Elazer ben Shimon's ruling. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and R. Yosef Corcus, by contrast, elaborate to show that he accepts the position of Rabbi Yehudah <i>HaNasi</i>. Moreover, they cite the Rambam's ruling in Chapter 17, Halachah 18, as proof that this is the Rambam's understanding here. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> does, however, explain a way to interpret the passage according to the Ra'avad's view.</i><br>What should be done with [these accompanying offerings]?<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., by definition an accompanying offering may not be sacrificed alone, only with a sacrifice, and in this instance, the sacrifice has been disqualified.</i> If there was another sacrifice that had already been slaughtered at that time,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">And does not have an accompanying offering to be brought with it.</i> they should be offered together with it. If there was not another sacrifice that had already been slaughtered at that time, they are considered as if they were disqualified because they were left overnight and they should be destroyed by fire.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The priests must wait until the next morning to burn them. For until a sacrifice is actually disqualified, it is forbidden to destroy it. See the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 7:4).</i><br>When does the above apply? With regard to communal sacrifices, because the heart of the court makes stipulations concerning them.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the court takes into consideration all the possible eventualities that might crop up and has the accompanying offering brought with those possibilities in mind. Hence if the sacrifice is disqualified, the basis on which the accompanying offering was brought is not nullified.</i> [Different rules apply with regard to] individual sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For the court does not make such stipulations about them.</i> Such [accompanying offerings] should not be offered together with another sacrifice even if it was sacrificed at that time. Instead, they should be left until they become disqualified because they remained overnight and then they should be destroyed by fire.
Whenever a sacrifice was offered for a purpose other than that for which it was consecrated,<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., when one sacrificed it with the intent that it was another type of offering, e.g., one slaughtered an animal consecrated as a burnt-offering with the intent that it was a peace-offering.</i> the accompanying offerings should be offered with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">For with the exception of a sin-offering, sacrifices are acceptable if slaughtered with such a mistaken intent. And there are no accompanying offerings for a sin-offering.</i>
[The following laws apply with regard to] the offspring of a thanksgiving-offering, an animal onto which the holiness of a thanksgiving-offering was transferred, and [a situation in which] one separated his thanksgiving-offering, it was lost, and he separated another one instead of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See the parallels to similar questions involving a sin-offering in Chapter 4, Halachah 4.</i> If [any of these animals] were to be offered after the owner's obligation was not satisfied with the original thanksgiving-offering, bread<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the 40 breads offered together with a thanksgiving-offering.</i> need not be brought with it. If the owner's obligation was satisfied with the original offering and it and the one separated in place of it, it and its offspring, or it and the animal onto which its holiness was transferred are both present before us, bread is required to be brought with both of them.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The apparent meaning of the Rambam's words here and in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot, loc. cit.</i>) is that bread should be brought when offering both of these sacrifices. <i>Shoham VeYashpah</i>, however, cites <i>Menachot</i> 79b which states that when both a thanksgiving-offering and an animal separated as a replacement for it are both present before us, the breads should be offered with either one and the other, offered without bread. Even such an interpretation, however, is not appropriate with regard to an animal onto which the holiness of a thanksgiving-offering was transferred. The Rambam's ruling here is also slightly problematic when compared to the following halachah.</i><br>When does the above<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">That bread is or is not required for both of the offerings in the above situations.</i> apply? When one vowed to bring a thanksgiving-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., he did not designate a specific animal as a thanksgiving-offering, but instead, undertook the responsibility to bring such a sacrifice.</i> When, however, one designated an animal as a thanksgiving-offering, an animal set aside instead of it or one onto which its holiness was transferred require that bread [be offered with them].<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rambam LeAm</i> explains that when an animal is designated as a thanksgiving sacrifice and is lost, there is no need to bring another instead of it. Hence the second thanksgiving-offering is considered as an independent sacrifice and bread is required for it independently.<br>With regard to an animal upon which the holiness of the thanksgiving offering was transferred, <i>Rambam LeAm</i> questions the Rambam's ruling, because seemingly, bread should not be required for such a sacrifice after the first animal was offered. Based on Halachah 13, Rav Yosef Corcus maintains that there is a printing error here and that in no instance is bread required when offering an animal on which the holiness of a thanksgiving-offering was transferred.</i> Its offspring does not require bread.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., in any situation; see <i>Hilchot Temurah</i> 4:1.</i> [This applies] whether or not the owner's obligation was already satisfied with the original offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Rambam LeAm</i> maintains that this line refers only to the offspring of a thanksgiving-offering.</i>
[The following laws apply if one] set aside an animal as a thanksgiving-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">This law applies when the person made a vow to bring a thanksgiving-offering, accepting responsibility for the sacrifice.</i> and it was lost, he set aside a second one in its stead and it was also lost, he then set aside a third animal in its place and then the first two were found. Thus the three animals are standing before us. If he fulfills his obligation with the first one, the second one does not require that bread be brought with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">The second animal was set aside in place of the first. Since the owner fulfilled his obligation with the first, there is no obligation to bring bread with the second.</i> The third one, however, requires bread.<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">For it does not have a connection to the first. Therefore it is considered as a new thanksgiving-offering which requires bread.</i><br>If he fulfills his obligation with the third one, the second one does not require that bread be brought with it.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">For the third animal takes the place of the second.</i> The first one, however, requires bread.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For it is not associated with the third animal.</i> If he fulfills his obligation with the middle one, both the others do not require bread.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Because the middle one is associated with both of the others. It was set aside instead of the first and the third was set aside instead of it.</i>
[The following laws apply when one] sets aside money for a thanksgiving-offering and it was lost, other money was set aside in its place, but [the owner] did not have the opportunity to buy a thanksgiving-offering until the first money was found. He should bring a thanksgiving-offering and its bread from a combination of the two. From the remainder, he should bring a thanksgiving-offering, but it does not require bread.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">For if there are funds left over from the purchase of a sacrifice, the money should be used to purchase an offering of the same type, as stated in Chapter 5, Halachah 9. Nevertheless, the additional thanksgiving-offering does not require bread as reflected by Halachah 8.</i> It does, however, require accompanying offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">For it must be offered according to the requirements appropriate for thanksgiving-offerings.</i><br>Similarly, when one sets aside [an animal for] a thanksgiving-offering and it was lost, money was set aside in its place, and afterwards [the original animal] was found, he should bring a thanksgiving-offering without bread with that money.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the animal originally set aside as a thanksgiving-offering should be offered for that purpose together with the bread and the money should be used to purchase an additional thanksgiving-offering.</i> Similarly, if one sets aside money for a thanksgiving-offering and it was lost, a thanksgiving-offering was set aside in its place, and then the money was found, the money should be used to purchase a thanksgiving-offering and its bread.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Since the money was originally set aside for this purpose, it should be used for the primary offering.</i> The second thanksgiving-offering should be offered without bread.
[The following laws apply if a person] says: "This is a thanksgiving-offering and this is its bread."<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">Setting aside a specific animal and bread.</i> If the bread is lost, he should bring other bread, If the thanksgiving-offering is lost, he should not bring another thanksgiving-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">Since he did not accept an obligation to bring a sacrifice upon himself, but rather designated an animal as a sacrifice, if that animal is lost, he is under no obligation. The fact that there is bread remaining does not obligate him as the Rambam explains.</i> [The rationale is that] the bread is brought because of the thanksgiving-offering, but the thanksgiving-offering is not brought because of the bread.
When a person set aside money for his thanksgiving-offering and some remained, he should use it to bring bread. If he set aside money to bring bread and some remained, he should not use it to bring a thanksgiving-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Because the bread is referred to as a thanksgiving-offering, but the offering is not referred to as bread (<i>Menachot</i> 80a).</i>
[The following rules apply when] one says: "This is a thanksgiving-offering,"<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">Designating an animal to be offered for that purpose.</i> it becomes intermingled with an animal upon which its holiness was transferred, one died, but he does not know which is which. There is no way to correct the situation of the one which remains. Were one to bring bread with it, [it is possible that he will have erred,] for perhaps this is the animal upon which the holiness was transferred.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">And bread should not be brought with such an offering.<br>As mentioned above, there appears to be a contradiction between this halachah and Halachah 8, for Halachah 8 appears to imply that bread is required for an animal to which the holiness of a thanksgiving offering was transferred if the original animal had been designated for the sacrifice. For this reason, Rav Yosef Corcus maintains that there is a printing error in Halachah 8.</i> [But] were he to bring it without bread, it is possible that it is the thanksgiving-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Which requires bread.</i> Therefore this animal should not be sacrificed at all. Instead, it should be allowed to pasture until it contracts a disqualifying blemish.<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">At which time, it should be sold and the proceeds used to purchase another thanksgiving-offering and its bread. The Ra'avad maintains that the person should bring another thanksgiving-offering and bread from his own resources and the proceeds from the sale of the blemished animal should be used to purchase a thanksgiving-offering without bread. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> justifies the Rambam's ruling, while the <i>Chacham Tzvi</i> (Responsum 24) reinforces the Ra'avad's objection.</i>
When one of the loaves of the bread brought with a thanksgiving-offering was broken in pieces, they are all disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">The breads accompanying the thanksgiving offering must be whole. The Rambam is speaking about an instance when one of these breads became broken between the slaughter of the animal and the presentation of its blood on the altar.</i> If a loaf was taken outside [the Temple Courtyard] or it became impure,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot</i> 12b states that the High Priest's forehead plate causes those impure to be considered acceptable and the acceptability of those taken out of the Temple Courtyard is derived through Talmudic logic.</i> the remainder of the breads are acceptable.<br>If the bread<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">This is speaking about an instance when all of the loaves were disqualified in this manner. If only some of the loaves were disqualified, they should be replaced.</i> was broken in two, contracted impurity, or was taken outside [the Temple Courtyard] before the thanksgiving-offering was slaughtered, he should bring another bread and then slaughter [the sacrificial animal]. If the above occurred after [the animal] was slaughtered, the blood should be cast [upon the altar], the meat [of the sacrifice] should be eaten, but all of the bread is disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yosef Corcus and others question the Rambam's ruling, noting that he is equating the loaves becoming impure or taken outside the Temple Courtyard with their being broken when at the beginning of the halachah, he himself mentioned the difference between these categories. Also, this ruling would apparently contradict the ruling in Chapter 17, Halachah 13. Rav Yosef Corcus suggests that the Rambam's statements are referring to a situation where all the loaves became impure or were taken out of the Courtyard.</i> The person [bringing the sacrifice] does not fulfill his vow.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">And instead must bring another thanksgiving-offering. The commentaries note that the Rambam's ruling is in direct contradiction to the standard printed text of <i>Menachot</i> 46b. They suggest that perhaps the Rambam had a different version of that Talmudic passage.</i><br>If the blood has been cast [upon the altar] and afterwards some of the breads were broken in two, became impure, or were taken outside, [the person bringing the sacrifice] should separate one of the whole loaves<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">A total of 40 loaves (10 of four different types) are offered with the thanksgiving offering. One loaf of each type is given to a priest (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 9:12, 17-18).</i> for [all the loaves of that type, including] the one which is broken, one of the pure for [all the others, including] the one which is impure, and one which is in [the Temple Courtyard] for [all the others, including] the one which was taken outside.
When a thanksgiving-offering was slaughtered in connection with 80 loaves, 40 of the 80 are not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Since only 40 are required, the additional 40 are not consecrated.</i> If [the person bringing the sacrifice] says: "May 40 of these 80 become consecrated," he should take 40 from the 80 and separate one from each [category brought as] an offering. The other 40 should be redeemed and then they are considered as ordinary bread.<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries question why the loaves must be redeemed. Since the person stated that only 40 are being consecrated, why is it necessary to redeem the other 40? Among the answers given is that originally, when setting aside the loaves, he mentioned that all the loaves would be consecrated.</i>
When a person slaughters a thanksgiving-offering, but its bread was located outside the walls of <i>Beit Pagi</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">This term refers to the wall that surrounds the Temple Mount. The term relates to the phrase (Daniel 1:5 : <i>patbag hamelech</i>, "the food of the king" [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 7:3)].</i> the bread is not consecrated. If, however, the bread was outside the Temple Courtyard, the bread becomes consecrated even though it is not inside the Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>ibid.</i>), the Rambam explains that although the Torah states that the thanksgiving offering should be brought "on the bread," the intent is not they must be physically adjacent to each other. It is sufficient that they be close.</i>
If he slaughtered [an animal designated as a thanksgiving-offering] before the surface of the bread in the oven becomes hard, even if all of [the breads] became hard except for one, the bread is not consecrated.<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">For in order to be associated with the sacrifice, the bread must be baked at the time that the animal is slaughtered.</i>
If he slaughtered [the animal designated as a thanksgiving-offering] and its slaughter was disqualified because of an improper intent concerning the time or the place [where the sacrifice will be offered or eaten]<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">See the following chapters which discuss these issues at length.</i> the bread is sanctified.<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">Because the disqualification came at the time of the slaughter of the animal and not beforehand. Since the bread becomes sanctified, it is considered as <i>piggul</i></i> If [the animal] is discovered to have a disqualifying physical blemish, it was <i>tereifah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">An animal that will die within a year. In these instances, since the animal was never acceptable for sacrifice - even if that was not discovered before its slaughter - the breads are not consecrated.</i> or it was slaughtered with an improper intent,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., it was slaughtered with the intent of it being offered as another type of sacrifice. In this instance, even though the disqualifying factor took place at the time the animal is slaughtered, the bread is disqualified. For based on Leviticus 7:12, the <i>Sifra</i> states that for the bread to be consecrated, the animal must be slaughtered for the sake of a thanksgiving-offering.</i> the bread is not consecrated. These laws also apply with regard to the ram brought by a nazirite.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., for this offering is also accompanied by bread. The same concepts also apply with regard to the two loaves brought on Shavuos and the two lambs brought at that time. See Chapter 17, Halachah 18.</i>

Chapter 13

There are three improper intents that disqualify sacrifices. They are: the intent [to offer a sacrifice]<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The particular activities which disqualifiy a sacrifice are mentioned in Halachot 4-6.</i> for a different purpose,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., for the sacrifice of another type or not for the sake of its owner, as the Rambam proceeds to explain.</i> the intent [to offer or partake of the sacrifice] in an [improper] place, and the intent [to offer or partake of the sacrifice] at an [improper] time.<br>What is meant by the term "the intent [to offer a sacrifice] for a different purpose"?<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 1:1 states: "All of the sacrifices that were sacrificed without the proper intent are acceptable, but their offering does not fulfill the owner's obligation with the exception of a sin-offering and the Paschal sacrifice." Thus although most sacrifices that are not offered with the proper intent are acceptable, since the owner does not fulfill his obligation while offering them, the Rambam mentions them in this halachah (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>). See also <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 4:10.</i> [The animal was designated as] a burnt-offering and [the priest] had the intent that it was a peace-offering, he slaughtered it for the sake of a burnt-offering and a peace-offering, or for the sake of a peace-offering and a burnt-offering, or he did not slaughter the sacrifice for the sake of its owners. These are all examples of "intents [to offer a sacrifice] for a different purpose."<br>What is meant by the term "the intent [to offer or partake of the sacrifice] in an [improper] place"? [The priest] slaughtered the sacrificial animal for the correct purpose<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., for the type of sacrifice for which it was designated and for the correct owner.</i> with the intent of casting its blood or offering a portion of it that was fit to be offered on the altar's pyre outside the Temple Courtyard or eating a portion of it that is fit to be eaten outside the place designated for it to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Sacrifices of the most sacred order must be eaten in the Temple Courtyard and sacrifices of lesser sanctity must be eaten in Jerusalem.</i> These are all examples of "intents [to offer a sacrifice] in an [improper] place." Sacrifices [that were slaughtered] with such an intent are called sacrifices that were slaughtered outside their proper place.<br>What is meant by the term "the intent [to offer or partake of the sacrifice] at an [improper] time"? [The priest] slaughtered the sacrificial animal for the correct purpose with the intent of casting its blood [on the altar] after sunset which is not the time at which its blood may be cast, with intent of offering a portion of it that was fit to be offered on the altar's pyre] on the next day, after dawn, which is not the time when it may be offered, or to partake of a portion of it that is fit to be eaten after the time when it is fit to be eaten.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Most sacrifices must be eaten on the day they were offered and on the following night. Certain others may also be eaten on the following day.</i> These are all examples of "intents [to offer a sacrifice] at an [improper] time." Sacrifices [that were slaughtered] with such an intent are called sacrifices that were slaughtered outside their proper time. They are also referred to be the term <i>piggul</i>. This is the meaning of the term <i>piggul</i> mentioned in the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Leviticus 7:18; 19:7. The term has the implication of "rejected" (<i>Targum Onkelos</i>) and "abhorrent" (Rav Saadia Gaon).</i>
According to the Oral Tradition,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra</i> to the verse quoted; <i>Zevachim</i> 29a; see the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 3:3).</i> we learned that the Torah's statements [Leviticus 7:18]: "If some of the meat of the peace-offering was eaten on the third day," [should not be interpreted literally]. Instead, it is speaking about one who has the intent while offering the sacrifice that it will be eaten on the third day.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the verse states: "if it was eaten on the third day, it is unacceptable." Peace-offerings may be eaten only for two days. The Oral Tradition explains that the intent is not that eating the sacrifice on the third day disqualifies it, but that having the intent that it be eaten on the third day while offering disqualifies it from the outset.<br>Although this interpretation is communicated by the Oral Tradition, there are allusions to it in the Torah's words. The above verse uses the term: "the one who offers it," implying that the disqualification involves the offering. And it uses the phrase <i>venechshav</i> ("and it will be considered"), implying that the disqualification has to do with thought.</i> The same applies with regard to every sacrifice that, while offering it, one had the intent to partake of it after the time that is appropriate to partake of that type of sacrifice.<br>Similarly, [the sacrifice is disqualified] if one had the intent to offer portions of it that are fit to be offered on the altar's pyre after the time appropriate for them to be offered. According to the Oral Tradition,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 28b explains that since the above verse uses a twofold construction for the term "eat," <i>haechol yaechol</i>, our Sages interpreted it as referring to two types of consumption: consumption by the altar and consumption by man.</i> the following concept was derived: With regard to both consumption by man and consumption by the altar, if one had the intent that [sacrifices] be consumed after the appropriate time, the sacrifice is considered as <i>piggul</i>.
When, however, a sacrifice was not disqualified because of an improper intent, but instead, its blood was cast on the altar in the proper manner, but it remained after the time allotted for it to be eaten, the portion that remains is considered <i>notar</i>. It is forbidden to eat it,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 18, Halachah 10, for more details regarding this prohibition.</i> but the sacrifice was already accepted and atonement was achieved. It is written with regard to the blood [of a sacrifice, Leviticus 17:11]: "And I gave it to you upon the altar to bring atonement." [Implied is that] since the blood reached the altar according to law, the owners achieved atonement and the sacrifice was acceptable. Therefore the concept of <i>piggul</i> applies only to entities that possess services that will enable [them to be consumed] either by men or by the altar, as will be explained.<br>The same laws apply if one had one of these three disqualifying intents when slaughtering a sacrifice, receiving its blood, taking its blood to the altar, or casting it on the altar.
We derive from the above that it is with regard to these four services that a sacrifice can be disqualified because of an [improper] intent: slaughter, receiving the blood, bringing it [to the altar], and casting it on the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that these four services are necessities for the offering of a sacrifice (<i>Zevachim</i> 1:4.)</i>
A fowl [can be disqualified because of an improper intent] in two services: <i>melikah</i> and squeezing out the blood [on the altar].<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 6:7), the Rambam writes that performing <i>melikah</i> is equivalent to slaughter and squeezing a fowl's blood on the altar equivalent to casting an animal's blood. In this instance, there are no parallels to receiving the blood or carrying it to the altar.</i>
The meal-offerings from which a handful is taken [can be disqualified because of an improper intent] in four services: taking the handful, placing the handful in a sacred utensil, bringing the utensil to the altar, and casting it on the pyre.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">For these four services are comparable to the four services mentioned in Halachah 4 (<i>Zevachim</i> 13b). As the Rambam writes in his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 1:3) separating the handful is equivalent to ritual slaughter and the handful of meal, equivalent to the blood of a sacrificial animal.</i>
If, however, one had an improper intent while performing services other than these: e.g., one had such an intent when skinning [sacrificial animal], when cutting it into pieces, when bringing its internal organs and fats to altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">All of these services are not essential to the offering of a sacrifice. Even if they are not performed, the sacrifice is acceptable.</i> when mixing [the oil and flour of] a meal-offering, when bringing it close to the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">These services are performed before taking the handful. Thus it is comparable to the services performed before slaughter which do not disqualify an animal.</i> or the like, that [improper] intent is of no consequence. [This applies] whether it is an intent [to offer a sacrifice] for a different purpose, an intent [to offer or partake of the sacrifice] in an [improper] place, or an intent [to offer or partake of the sacrifice] at an [improper] time.
Similarly, if when performing one of these four tasks or all of them, one has an [improper] intent other than these three intents, that undesirable intent does not disqualify [a sacrifice] at all.<br>What is implied? When slaughtering [a sacrificial animal], receiving [its blood], bringing [the blood to the altar], and casting [on the altar], a person had the intent to:<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">All of the acts mentioned by the Rambam would disqualify a sacrifice or its meat if performed. In this instance, however, we are not speaking about a situation where these acts were performed. Instead, it is merely that the priest performing the service intended that they be performed.</i><br>a) leave the blood of the sacrifice or the organs and fats to be burnt on the altar for the next day<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">While according to law, the blood must be cast on the altar on the day the sacrifice was offered and the limbs and organs must be burnt on either that day or the following night.</i> or to remove them from the Temple Courtyard,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Which would disqualify them.</i><br>b) or he had the intent to cast the blood on the [altar's] ramp, where it is not opposite the base<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">And the blood of certain sacrifices must be poured on the base of the altar.</i><br>c) or [take] the blood of sacrifices that must be presented on the upper portion of the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Burnt-offerings.</i> on the lower portion or those to be presented on the lower portion<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Sin-offerings.</i> on the upper portion,<br>d) or those to be presented on the outer altar<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the overwhelming majority of both the communal and individual offerings.</i> on the inner altar, or those to be presented on the inner altar<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:11.</i> on the outer altar, or to bring the blood of a sin-offering into the inner chamber,<br>e) he had the intent that impure people or others disqualified from partaking of a sacrifice should partake of it,<br>f) that the sacrifice be offered by impure people or others who are disqualified from performing sacrificial service,<br>g) to mix the blood of the sacrifice with unacceptable blood;<br>h) he intended to break the bones of a Paschal sacrifice or to eat from it while it is not thoroughly cooked;<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Both of these are forbidden (Exodus 12:46, 9).</i><br>i) or he intended to burn a sin-offering that must be burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>ibid.</i> 7:2-5 with regard to the burning of these sin-offerings. As related there, they are burnt in a special place outside of Jerusalem on the day they were offered or on the following night.</i> outside its proper time or outside its proper place;<br>With regard to any of the above - or similar - intents, the sacrifice is acceptable. Similarly, if when taking the handful of meal, placing it into a vessel, bringing it to the altar, or casting it on the [altar's] pyre, one had the intent to leave the handful or the frankincense until the following day or to take them out of [the Temple Courtyard], the offering is acceptable.
We already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 1, Halachah 23.</i> that bringing [blood or limbs to the altar] in a way other than walking is not considered as bringing them. Therefore an undesirable intent<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">Even one of the three undesirable intents mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.</i> does not disqualify [a sacrifice in such an instance]. Carrying [blood or a limb] to a place to which one need not is considered as carrying and [if one has] an undesirable intent while doing this, [the sacrifice] is disqualified.<br>What is implied? One received the blood and while standing in his place extended his arm to cast it on the altar and while he extended his arm, he had an undesirable intent, that intent does not disqualify it. If, however, he received the blood inside [the Temple Courtyard] and did not carry it toward the altar, but instead, carried it and took it [toward the area] outside [the Courtyard],<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">He did not actually take the blood outside - that would disqualify it - but he walked in that direction, away from the altar (see Rashi, <i>Zevachim</i> 16b).</i> having a disqualifying intent, [like one] involving the time [the sacrifice would be eaten] or the like, he causes it to be disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam's ruling, explaining that the matter is the subject of a difference of opinion in <i>Zevachim, loc. cit.</i>, and the halachah appears to follow the view of Rabbi Elazar who maintains that a priest's intent can disqualify the sacrifice only when he is carrying the blood to the altar. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> offers a resolution of the passage according to the Rambam's understanding.</i>

Chapter 14

[With regard to the intent that could disqualify a sacrifice:] The only intent that is significant is that of the person performing the Temple service. The intent of the person bringing the sacrifice is of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Note the parallel in <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 2:22.</i> Even when we heard that the owner had an intent which would cause the sacrifice to be considered as <i>piggul</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Vayikra Rabbah</i> 22:7 states that even if the owner "sits and thinks [unacceptable intents] the entire day," the sacrifice is not disqualified.</i> if the person performing the Temple service had the proper intent, the sacrifice is acceptable.
The intent of [the person performing sacrificial service] is significant only when he is fit to perform this service, he is performing service with an entity fit for service, and is doing so in a place fit for service.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam proceeds to define each of these concepts.</i><br>What is implied? If a person who is unfit for Temple service receives the blood, brings it to the altar, or casts it on the altar and at the time he is performing this service has an unacceptable intent with regard to place or time, his intent does not disqualify the sacrifice, because he is not fit to perform Temple service.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">For these undesirable intents disqualify a sacrifice only when they alone are the factors that disqualify it and not when it is disqualified for other reasons [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 3:1)].</i> The blood that he received or a portion that remained after he cast some [on the altar] should be poured into the drainage canal.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">For it is disqualified and must be disposed.</i> If "the blood of the soul" remains [within the animal], a person who is fit to perform Temple service should receive it with a proper intent.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">He should then bring it to the altar and cast it upon it. The sacrifice is then acceptable. As stated in Chapter 1, Halachot 27-28, the rationale is that "individuals who are unacceptable for Temple service do not cause the remainder of the blood to be considered as remnants." Hence it is as if the blood of the sacrifice had never been taken.</i><br>If, however, a person who is unfit for Temple service has a disqualifying intent at the time he slaughters [the animal], he disqualifies it with his intent, because ritual slaughter is acceptable if performed by an unfit person, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 1, Halachah 1.</i><br>They are sacrifices that are acceptable if they are offered for an intent other than specified originally,<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">E.g., a burnt-offering is offered with the intent that it is a peace-offering.</i> as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 15, Halachah 1.</i> Therefore, [in those instances,] if a priest who is not fit for Temple service receives the blood, carries it, or casts it on the altar, he disqualifies the sacrifice as if he offered it for the desired intent in which instance, it would be disqualified. Even if "the blood of the soul" remains and it was received by an acceptable [priest] and cast on the altar, the sacrifice was already disqualified. It was not disqualified because it was offered for a different purpose, but because [service] was performed by someone unfit for service, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam is implying that since the sacrifice is acceptable in these instances, even though in other instances, such a change in intent would disqualify it, a person who is not fit for Temple service is considered just like one who is. His intent is significant and could disqualify the sacrifice. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, however, questions on what the Rambam bases this principle.</i>
What is meant [by the concept that these principles apply only with regard] to "performing service with an entity fit for service"? [For example,] if a handful was taken from the meal-offering of the <i>omer</i> without the proper intent,<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., with the intent that it be offered as another type of meal-offering.</i> it is considered as if it was taken with the proper intent and the remnants are eaten. [The rationale is that] it is offered from barley and barley is not a substance fit for other offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., all of the other meal-offerings were brought from wheat and thus the barley used for the <i>omer</i> offering is unfit to be used for other meal-offerings. Hence even if one had the intent to offer it as another type of offering, that intent is of no consequence. (The meal-offering of a <i>sotah</i> is also brought from barley, but there are fundamental differences between it and the <i>omer</i> offering.)<br>The Rambam's understanding is based on his understanding of <i>Menachot</i> 5b which states that the <i>omer</i> offering is a <i>chidush</i>, something new and different than other meal-offerings, for it is from barley, as explained. The Ra'avad follows a different version of the text which states that the <i>omer</i> offering is different, because it is brought from <i>chadash</i>, wheat from the new harvest.<br>There is an advantage to the Ra'avad's understanding, because - as he explains - according to the Rambam, the same rationale could seemingly be used with regard to the <i>sotah</i> offering mentioned in the second clause of the halachah. There would be no necessity to mention frankincense. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> favors the Rambam's version and explains that by mentioning frankincense, our Sages (and the Rambam) chose one of two possible answers. They could also have stated that it is unfit to be used for other offerings.</i><br>Similarly, if one had a [disqualifying] intent with regard to the meal-offering of envy<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">The term used by Numbers 5:15,18 to describe the meal-offering brought by a woman suspected of adultery.</i> while frankincense was on it, before the frankincense was removed,<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to place frankincense on this offering (Numbers 5:15 . Thus before the offering is brought, the frankincense must be removed and until it is removed, the offering is not fit. Hence, whatever intent the person has concerning the offering at that time is of no consequence.</i> there is no consequence to that intent, because [it does not involve] an entity fit for service. Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.
What is meant [by the concept that these principles apply only with regard to "performing service] in a place fit for service"? When the altar has become damaged and [the priest offering the sacrifice] had a [disqualifying] intent with regard to the time or place [the sacrifice was to be offered or eaten], he did not disqualify the sacrifice with this intent,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">And it may be offered when the altar is repaired.</i> because the place was not fit for Temple service at that time.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if he had this intent while performing service in the Temple Courtyard. Since the altar is not fit for sacrifices to be offered upon it, the place is not considered as fit for service.</i><br>If one took a handful of flour from a meal-offering outside the Temple Courtyard and had a [disqualifying] intent with regard to the time or place [the meal-offering was to be offered or eaten] while taking the handful, the intent is of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Because the act was performed outside the Temple Courtyard, a place where sacrificial service may not be performed.</i>
These are elements of sacrificial [animals] that are not fit to be eaten, but are fit to be offered on the altar's pyre:<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">The definitions given in this and the following two halachot are necessary to understand the laws stated in Halachot 8-10 (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> its blood,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries have noted that the Rambam's wording is not exact, for although the blood is presented on the altar, it is not "offered on the altar's pyre."</i> its <i>eimorim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The fats and organs offered on the altar (see <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 1:18).</i> the meat of a burnt-offering, and the handful of meal and the frankincense from the meal-offerings from which such a handful is removed.
These are the elements that are fit to be eaten and are not fit to be offered on the altar: the meat that is eaten from any of the sacrifices, whether eaten by the priests or by all other people, the remainder of the meal-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., what remains after the handful is removed.</i> the two breads [offered on Shavuot], and the showbread.
These are the elements that are neither fit to be eaten, nor fit to be offered on the altar's pyre: the meat of the sin-offerings that are burnt [outside the Temple Courtyard],<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 7:2-5 which describes the burning of these sacrifices.</i> the entire hide of an animal with the exception of the hide of the fat-tail which is fit to be eaten, [and] the <i>murah</i>, the thin membrane that clings to the hide and separates between it and the meat; it is not fit to be eaten. [This category] also [includes] the bones, the <i>giddim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 3:4), the Rambam explains that this is a general term referring to blood vessels, nerves, and sinews.</i> the horns, and the hoofs, the feathers<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Taharot</i> 1:2), the Rambam explains that this term refers to the growth that remains after the large feathers are removed. [The Mishnah there uses the term <i>mourah</i>. The spelling of that term is important, for some spell it in the same way as a term the Rambam translates as referring to one of a fowl's stomachs.]</i> of a fowl, its nails, its beak, the tips of its wings, and the end of its tail.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In the above source, the Rambam explains that when the feathers are removed from these places, they remain dry projections that are unfit for consumption unless the fowl is very fat.</i> Even with regard to the soft places of the above which cleave to the flesh and would cause bleeding if cut off from a living animal, since they are not important, they are considered as an entity that is not fit to be eaten with regard to the sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Similarly, they are not considered as meat with regard to the prohibitions against partaking of forbidden foods and the laws of ritual purity. See <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 4:18, 9:7; <i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTumah</i> 1:7.</i> This also applies to the sauce [in which a sacrifice is cooked], the spices [with which it is cooked], a fetus, a placenta, the egg of a fowl, and meat that slipped by the knife at the time the animal was skinned and remains cleaving to the hide; it is called the <i>allal</i>. All of the above are not significant with regard to an intent [that could disqualify] sacrifices. They are considered as a matter that is not fit to be eaten.
A [disqualifying] intent is significant [even though] it concerns an entity that ultimately will be destroyed or that will ultimately be burnt.<br>If, while performing any or all of the four [significant] services,<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 2 above.</i> one has a [disqualifying] intent - whether concerning the place or the time - to partake of an entity that is not usually eaten or to offer on the altar's pyre an entity that is not usually offered there, the sacrifice is acceptable.<br>What is implied? One thought to drink the blood of a sacrifice or partake of its <i>eimorim</i> or of the handful of meal or the frankincense [taken from a meal offering] outside [the Temple Courtyard]<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a disqualifying intent because of the place.</i> or on the following day,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., a disqualifying intent because of the time.</i> the sacrifice is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">Because the substances mentioned are not usually eaten.</i> [This ruling also applies] if one had the intent of offering the meat of the sacrifice or what remains of the meal-offering outside [the Temple Courtyard] or on the following day.<br>Similarly, if one had a [disqualifying] intent - whether concerning the place or the time - to partake of or to offer on the altar's pyre the hide [of a sacrificial animal], its bones, <i>giddim</i>, sauce, <i>allal</i>, or the like, the sacrifice is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For these entities are neither fit to be eaten, nor fit to be offered on the altar's pyre.</i> Similarly, if one had the intent to partake of the bulls or the goats that are burnt,<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 7:2-5 which describes the burning of these sacrifices.</i> outside [Temple Courtyard] or on the following day, the sacrifice is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Because these sacrifices are not fit to be eaten. With regard to a disqualifying intent while burning these sacrifices, see Chapter 13, Halachah 8.</i> Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.
If<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">While performing one of the four services mentioned previously.</i> one had the intent that [other] persons who are impure or who are disqualified shall partake of an entity that is fit to be eaten or that these persons should offer an entity that is fit to be offered on the altar's pyre outside of the set times for it to be eaten or offered, the sacrifice is <i>piggul</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that, as stated in the following halachah, our Sages considered an animal's consumption as "eating." Hence consumption by such individuals will certainly fall into that category.</i> as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 13, Halachah 1. The fact that the person who was intended to partake of the sacrifice or offer it was impure or disqualified is not significant.</i> If he [had the intent that they partake of it or offer it] outside the places designated for eating and offering, the sacrifice is unacceptable, but not <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 13 which explains that the concept of <i>piggul</i> applies only when the disqualifying intent applies to time alone.</i>
The concept of eating does not apply to a substance smaller than an olive, nor does the concept of offering an entity on the altar's pyre apply to a substance smaller than an olive.<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">An olive-sized portion is 27 cc according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>. Both the mitzvot and the prohibitions involving eating center on partaking of an olive-sized portion of food. See <i>Hilchot Sh'vuot</i> 4:1; <i>Hilchot Terumot</i> 10:2, <i>et al.</i> This measure is also of consequence with regard to offering substances on the altar as stated in Chapter 11, Halachah 15.</i> Therefore if a person had a [disqualifying] intent - whether concerning the place or the time - to partake of less than an olive size portion of an entity fit to be eaten or to offer less than an olive-sized portion of an entity fit to be offered, the sacrifice is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Because his forbidden intent is of no consequence.</i><br>If he had the intent to eat half of an olive-sized portion outside [the appropriate place] and offer half an olive-sized portion outside [the appropriate place] or he had the intent to eat half of an olive-sized portion after [the appropriate] time for eating and offer half an olive-sized portion after [the appropriate] time for offering, the sacrifice is acceptable. [The rationale is that] eating and offering are not combined [to be considered as a single activity]. If, however, he used the wording <i>achilah</i>, saying: "Half an olive-sized portion should be eaten and half an olive-sized portion consumed by [the altar's] fire," the [two halves] are combined, for the wording of <i>achilah</i> is one.<br>If one had a [disqualifying] intent to eat or offer half an olive-sized portion and then in the same thought had an intent concerning another half of an olive-sized portion, the two can be combined. If one had a [disqualifying] intent - whether concerning the place or the time - to eat half of an olive-sized portion and that an animal or beast should eat half of an olive-sized portion,<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 31b refers to II Kings 9:10 as support for the concept that consumption by animals can be termed <i>achilah</i>.</i> they can be combined because both are called eating.<br>If one had a [disqualifying] intent that two people partake of the sacrifice, the two are combined.
