Even though one intended to partake of an olive-sized portion in longer than the time it takes to eat a half a loaf of bread,<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., an equivalent of three egg-sized portions. Generally, if a person stretches out his consumption of an olive-sized portion beyond this time span, it is not considered as "eating," for he will not have ingested a significant amount at once. The Rabbis mention different opinions with regard to this time span, referred to as <i>k'dai achilat pras</i>, some as brief as 2 minutes and some as long as 9 minutes. Based on Shiurei Torah, the suggested practice is to consider <i>k'dai achilat pras</i> as 4 minutes with regard to eating matzah on Pesach, but 9 minutes with regard to eating on Yom Kippur.</i> the eating is combined.<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">For here the emphasis is not on the person's activity of eating, but on the sacrifice being eaten (<i>Kin'at Eliyahu</i>).</i> If at the time of slaughter, he had a [disqualifying] intent to eat half an olive-sized portion and at the time of casting [the blood on the altar], he had a [disqualifying] intent to eat half an olive-sized portion, the two intents - whether concerning the place or the time - are combined. Similarly, if one had a [disqualifying] intent concerning an olive-sized portion<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> suggests amending the text to read "half an olive-sized portion" and in that way fit the context of the entire halachah. The notes to the Frankel edition of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>, however, indicate that all of the authoritative manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> speak of an olive-sized portion.</i> at the time he received [the blood] and [such an intent] concerning an olive-sized<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">An olive-sized portion is 27 cc according to <i>Shiurei Torah</i>. Both the mitzvot and the prohibitions involving eating center on partaking of an olive-sized portion of food. See <i>Hilchot Sh'vuot</i> 4:1; <i>Hilchot Terumot</i> 10:2, <i>et al.</i> This measure is also of consequence with regard to offering substances on the altar as stated in Chapter 11, Halachah 15.</i> portion while bringing [it to the altar, they are combined]. For all the four services [mentioned above]<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 2 above.</i> are combined and can be considered as a single service.<br>If one had a [disqualifying] intent regarding offering half an olive-sized portion of the handful [of meal] and [a similar intent] regarding half an olive-sized portion of the frankincense,<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishneh LiMelech</i> notes that in Chapter 11, Halachah 8, the Rambam writes that two grains of frankincense are sufficient for a meal-offering to be considered acceptable. Seemingly, then, that amount should also be enough to disqualify such an offering.</i> they are combined. For with regard to the meal-offering, the frankincense and the handful [of meal] are considered like the <i>eimorim</i> for an animal sacrifice. Therefore, if one had the intent to offer an olive-sized portion of frankincense at an improper time, [the meal-offering] is considered <i>piggul</i>, as we explained.<br>Whether one had the intent to cast all [of an animal's] blood outside [the Temple Courtyard] or on the following day or had the intent to cast [only] a portion of its blood outside [the Temple Courtyard] or on the following day, since he had a [disqualifying] intent concerning the amount of blood sufficient to present on the altar,<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">I.e.,. a very small amount.</i> [the sacrifice] is disqualified.

Chapter 15

Any of the sacrifices - whether individual sacrifices or communal sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The mention of communal sacrifices represents a change of mind for the Rambam. In his original version of his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 1:1, which is preserved in the standard printing of that text), he writes that a communal sacrifice slaughtered for a different intent fulfills the community's obligation. Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura also follows this view. In his later years, however, the Rambam emended his text to agree with this ruling (see Rav Kapach's text). Note also Rabbi Akiva Eiger's gloss who questions the initial version of the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah.</i> - that were sacrificed for a different purpose than that for which they were originally designated are acceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, even if one slaughtered an animal designated as a burnt-offering for the sake of a sin-offering, one should continue performing all the subsequent tasks for the sake of a burnt-offering [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>op. cit.</i>)]. See Halachah 3.</i> but they did not satisfy the obligation incumbent on their owner with the exception of a sin-offering and the Paschal sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 3 which explains why these offerings are singled out in contrast to all others.</i> If they are offered for a different purpose, they are unacceptable. [This applies whether] one changes the purpose of the sacrifice at the time of slaughter, at the time he receives its blood, at the time he brings it to the altar, or when he casts it upon it, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 13, Halachah 4.</i><br>What is meant by saying that [the owner] does not fulfill his obligation through such [a sacrifice]? For example, one slaughtered [an animal designated as] a burnt-offering as a peace-offering. It does not fulfill the obligation of the owner, neither for the burnt-offering for which he is obligated or for a sin-offering. Instead, he is obligated to bring another sacrifice. Similarly, if one slaughtered a burnt-offering brought by Reuven for the sake of Shimon, it does not fulfill the obligation either of Reuven or of Shimon.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 3 which explains the derivation of this concept. The <i>Mishneh LiMelech</i> questions the Rambam's equation of sacrifices offered for a different purpose than they were originally designated and those offered for the sake of a different person. There is, he explains, a fundamental difference between them. If one slaughters an animal for the sake of another person, the owner is still considered to have fulfilled his obligation. It is only when the blood is cast on the altar for the sake of another person that he is not considered to have fulfilled his obligation. Nevertheless, the Rambam's statements can be interpreted as referring to an instance when one slaughtered the animal with the intent to cast its blood on the altar for the sake of another person.</i><br>When does the above apply? When one changed the purpose of the sacrifice intentionally. If, however, one erred and had the impression that the [animal designated as] a burnt-offering was [designated as] a peace-offering and carried out all of its services for the sake of a peace-offering, the owner is considered to have fulfilled his obligation. Similarly, when one offered a sin-offering or a Paschal sacrifice for a different purpose in error, they are acceptable. For supplanting [a purpose] mistakenly is of no consequence.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">This also represents a change of mind for the Rambam. In his original version of his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 1:1), which is preserved in the standard printing of that text), he writes that if an intent is supplanted in error, it is considered to be supplanted. In his later years, however, the Rambam emended his text to agree with this ruling (see Rav Kapach's text).</i><br>Similarly, if one performs <i>melikah</i> on a fowl [designated as] a burnt-offering or squeezed out its blood for a different purpose, it is acceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">This applies even if its blood was presented on the lower portion of the altar as is the blood of a sin-offering.</i> but does not fulfill the obligation of the owner. And a sin-offering of fowl [brought for a different purpose] is unacceptable.
Similarly, all of the meal-offerings that were offered for a different intent than that originally conceived are acceptable, but the owners do not fulfill their obligation with the exception of a meal-offering of a sinner<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the meal-offering brought by a transgressor obligated to bring an adjustable guilt-offering who is very poor. See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i>, ch. 10.</i> and a meal offering of a <i>sotah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">A woman suspected of adultery. This meal-offering is comparable to that of a sinner.</i> If while performing one of the four services<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Mentioned in Chapter 13, Halachah 6.</i> one had an intent for a different purpose, [the meal-offering] is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 3 which explains why these offerings are singled out in contrast to all others.</i><br>What is implied? One separated a handful from a freewill meal-offering for the sake of a meal-offering of a sinner, from an offering intended to be prepared in a deep frying-pan for the sake of one to be prepared in a flat frying-pan, or from an offering intended to be prepared in a flat frying-pan for the sake of one to be prepared in a deep frying-pan.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i>, ch. 13, for a description of the differences between these offerings.</i> Similar laws apply in all analogous situations.
It is forbidden to have an incorrect intent [when performing sacrificial service with] consecrated animals, as will be explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 18, Halachah 1.</i> Therefore if one slaughtered a sacrificial animal for a different purpose or took a handful from a meal-offering for a different purpose, whether intentionally or unintentionally, he is obligated to complete the remainder of the services for the proper purpose. Even if one slaughtered [the animal], received its blood, and brought it to the altar for an improper purpose, one is obligated to cast it on the altar for the proper purpose.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">The rationale is that since the sacrifice is acceptable, its functions must be performed for the proper intent.</i><br>Why are the laws governing a sin-offering and the Paschal sacrifice different from those governing all other sacrifices and the laws governing a meal-offering of a sinner and that of a <i>sotah</i> different from those governing all other meal-offerings? Because the Torah singled them out. With regard to a sin-offering, [Leviticus 4:33] states: "And he shall slaughter it as a sin-offering," i.e., that it must be slaughtered for the sake of a sin-offering. Similarly, all of its other services [must be performed] for the proper intent, as [implied by <i>ibid.</i>:28]: "for his sin," i.e., that its service must be performed for the sake of [atoning for] that sin. And [<i>ibid.</i>:26] states: "And he will atone for him," i.e., [the service must be performed] for the sake of its owner.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 7a interprets the phrase cited as implying: for him and not for his colleague. See Halachah 8.</i><br>And with regard to the Paschal sacrifice, [Deuteronomy 16:1] states: "And you shall offer a Paschal sacrifice to God, your Lord," implying that all of the acts must be performed for the sake of the Paschal sacrifice. [Exodus 12:27] states: "And you shall say, 'It is a Paschal sacrifice unto God,' implying that it must be slaughtered for the sake of the Paschal sacrifice. Thus if one altered the purpose for which it was sacrificed or [offered for] a different owner,<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 1:1), the Rambam states that the first verse teaches that the offering must be brought as a Paschal sacrifice and the second, that it must be brought for the sake of its owner. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Pesachim</i> 5:2), the Rambam quotes the Jerusalem Talmud which mentions only the second verse and states that a Paschal sacrifice that is not offered for the proper purpose is unacceptable. See also Halachah 11.</i> it is not acceptable.<br>And with regard to the meal-offering of a sinner, [Leviticus 5:12] states: "It is a sin-offering."<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Our text reflects an amended version. The standard printed text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> contains a different prooftext.</i> And with regard to the meal-offering of a <i>sotah</i>, [Numbers 5:15] states: "It is a meal-offering [resulting from] envy." [The implication is that] all of the actions associated with them must be performed for that purpose.
When a sin-offering is offered for the sake of another sacrifice, e.g., it was offered for the sake of a burnt-offering, a guilt-offering, or a peace-offering, it is unacceptable, as we explained. If, however, it was slaughtered as an ordinary animal, it is acceptable, but the owner does not fulfill his obligation.
According to the Oral Tradition,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zevachim</i> 46b derives this principle through Biblical exegesis.</i> it was derived that [an intent for] sacrificial purposes can disqualify sacrificial animals, but an intent for ordinary purposes does not.
If one slaughtered [an animal designated as a sin-offering to atone] for another sin, e.g., it was brought [to atone] for partaking of fat and one slaughtered it [to atone] for partaking of blood, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even though it was slaughtered as a sin-offering, since it was not slaughtered for the sake of the sin for which the animal was originally designated, it is unacceptable.</i>
If one slaughtered [an animal designated as a sin-offering to atone] for the sake of another person who was obligated to bring a sin-offering, even an adjustable guilt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is a different type of sacrifice, one might think that the situation is comparable to that stated in the following halachah, slaughtering a sin-offering for the sake of a person obligated to bring a burnt-offering. Nevertheless, since both offerings atone for sins punishable by <i>karet</i>, they are considered as comparable. Hence the sacrifice is disqualified (Rashi, <i>Zevachim</i> 9b).</i> it is unacceptable.
If, however, one slaughtered it for the sake of another person who was obligated to bring a burnt-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">To atone for the failure to observe a positive commandment.</i> it is acceptable, but the owner has not fulfilled his obligation.<br>[The concept mentioned previously<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the law stated in Halachah 7. From this concept, it is also possible to derive the law stated in this halachah, for the implication is that when one offers a sin-offering for the sake of another person who is obligated to bring a sin-offering, it is disqualified, but not when one offers it for the sake of another person who is not so obligated (<i>Zevachim</i> 7a).</i> derived from Leviticus 4:26:] "And he will atone for him," [i.e., "for him,"] and not for his colleague who is obligated to bring a sin-offering like he is.
If one slaughtered [an animal designated as a sin-offering] for the sake of a deceased person, it is acceptable, but it does not satisfy the obligation of the owner, because there is no atonement for the dead [through sacrifices].<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Hence the deceased person is not considered as obligated to bring a sacrifice. Therefore, this is not considered as an instance where one offered a sacrifice for the sake of another person obligated to bring a sacrifice.</i><br>If one slaughtered it for the sake of a person who is not obligated to bring a sacrifice at all, not a sin-offering, nor a burnt-offering, nor any other sacrifice, it is unacceptable. [The rationale is that] perhaps he is obligated [to bring a sacrifice],<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">A burnt-offering to atone for the failure to observe a positive commandment.</i> but does not know.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Hence he is considered to be obligated to bring a sacrifice.<br><i>Zevachim</i> 71-7b explains that there is a difference between such a situation and a person who knows he is obligated to bring a burnt-offering (in which instance, the sin-offering is not disqualified, as stated in the previous halachah). When the person knows he must bring a burnt-offering, his atonement is associated with that offering only. When, however, he does not know that he must bring a burnt-offering, he will not seek atonement. Hence, the sin-offering he brings will bring him a certain measure of atonement for the positive commandments he did not perform. For as stated in Halachah 14, everyone has certain positive commandments that he has failed to fulfill.</i>
If one slaughtered [a sacrificial animal] with the desired intent, but at the time of slaughter had the intent to cast its blood on the altar for a different purpose, it is disqualified, for the intent one has for one service during the performance of another service [is significant].<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Mishneh LiMelech</i> restricts the scope of the Rambam's statements, maintaining that if at the time of slaughter or the performance of one of the other three services mentioned in Halachah 2, one has a disqualifying intent concerning receiving the blood or bringing it to the altar, the sacrifice is still acceptable.</i> Thus the intent one had during the time of slaughter is considered as if it was in [the priest's] mind at the time he cast [the blood on the altar]. Therefore [the sacrifice] is disqualified.
A Paschal sacrifice that was slaughtered for a different intent - whether for the sake of another sacrifice or whether as an ordinary animal<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries question the Rambam's ruling, because even a sin-offering is acceptable when slaughtered as an ordinary animal. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that the phrase "unto God" in the prooftext excludes slaughtering the animal for ordinary purposes. In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Pesachim</i> 5:2), the Rambam cites the Jerusalem Talmud which states that the phrase "And you shall say, 'It is a Paschal sacrifice' excludes all other intents.</i> - it is unacceptable, as it is written: "And you shall say, 'It is a Paschal sacrifice unto God,'<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachot 1 and 3.</i><br>When does the above apply? When it was slaughtered on its appropriate time, the day of the fourteenth of Nisan. Moreover, even if it was slaughtered in the morning of that day<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The Paschal sacrifice may not be sacrificed until the afternoon of the fourteenth of Nisan. Nevertheless, since it is offered on that day, the morning is considered "the time of its sacrifice" with regard to the disqualification of an offering.</i> for a different intent, it is unacceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference of opinion concerning this matter in <i>Zevachim</i> 1:3. The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam maintaining that the more lenient opinion should be accepted. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam accepted the more stringent view because it is debated in the Talmud.</i> If, however, one slaughtered it with a different intent at a time not appropriate for its [sacrifice], it is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For if it was slaughtered at such a time, it is considered as a peace-offering and a peace-offering that was slaughtered with a different intent is acceptable.<br>In the clause which follows, the sacrifice is unacceptable, because there is no one to partake of it and a Paschal sacrifice is brought only to be eaten (<i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i>, ch. 2).</i><br>[If it was slaughtered] for the sake of others and not for its owner, it is considered as if it did not have an owner on the day [when it should be sacrificed] and it is unacceptable.
When a Paschal sacrifice was slaughtered with the proper intent on the fourteenth of Nisan before noon, it is unacceptable, because this is not the time of the sacrifice. If it was older than one year<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">At this age, it is no longer fit to be offered as a Paschal sacrifice.</i> and it was slaughtered at the appropriate time for the sake of a Paschal sacrifice and similarly, if one of the other sacrifices were slaughtered for the sake of a Paschal sacrifice, even if it was slaughtered after noon, they are acceptable, but the owners do not fulfill their obligation.
When a thanksgiving-offering is slaughtered for the sake of a peace-offering, the owner's obligation is fulfilled. When a peace-offering is slaughtered for the sake of a thanksgiving-offering, the owner's obligation is not fulfilled. [The rationale is that] a thanksgiving-offering is called a peace-offering,<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">See Leviticus 7:15 which speaks of "the thanksgiving-peace sacrifice."</i> but a peace-offering is not called a thanksgiving-offering.
When a burnt-offering was slaughtered for the sake of another person who was not obligated to bring a sacrifice at all, the owner does not fulfill his obligation,<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">For it is considered to have atoned for the person for whose sake it was sacrificed, as the Rambam proceeds to explain.</i> for it was not slaughtered for his sake. Although the person for whom it was sacrificed is not liable for any sacrifice in his own mind, it is impossible that he is not obligated [to seek] atonement from heaven, for there is no Jewish person who has never violated a positive commandment.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">And atonement for these unknown faults will be secured by this sacrifice.</i>
When a burnt-offering that was brought after the death [of the person who set it aside] was offered for the sake [of that person who set it aside and] not for the sake of its owner,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">The heir.</i> the owner<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">For it is considered to have atoned for the person for whose sake it was sacrificed, as the Rambam proceeds to explain.</i> is considered to have fulfilled his obligation, for there is no conception of ownership after death.
When the two sheep to be brought on Shavuot were slaughtered with the intent that they were rams,<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 8:1, together with the two loaves brought on Shavuot are offered several burnt offerings, among them two rams, and two sheep as communal peace-offerings.</i> the community is not considered to have fulfilled its obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">And two other sheep must be brought.</i> If [the priests] thought they were rams and slaughtered them with the intent that they were rams, they are considered to have fulfilled their obligation, for the intent was uprooted in error.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Halachah 1.</i>
When a guilt-offering of a person [to be purified from] <i>tzara'at</i><sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">A skin malady similar to, but not identical to leprosy that renders one ritually impure.</i> was slaughtered for the sake of another type of sacrifice or its blood was not placed on the thumb and large toe of the person seeking atonement,<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i> 4:2.</i> accompanying offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i>, ch. 2.</i> are required.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the person has not fulfilled his obligation and must bring another sacrifice, he is required to bring the accompanying offerings, for the reason mentioned by the Rambam.</i> For if it was offered without accompanying offerings, it would be as if one offered a freewill offering. And a guilt-offering is never brought as a freewill offering.
When the sheep that is brought together with the <i>omer</i> offering<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 7:3.</i> was slaughtered for a different intent, one should not bring two <i>esronim</i> for its accompanying offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">As would be required were it offered for the specified intent (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 2:5).</i> Instead, he should bring one <i>isaron</i>, as is brought for other freewill offerings. [The rationale is that] it did not satisfy the obligation.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In contrast to the guilt-offering mentioned in the previous halachah, it is customary to bring burnt-offerings as freewill offerings. Hence, the accompanying offerings should be brought accordingly.</i><br>Similarly, when [a lamb intended as] a continuous offering was slaughtered for a different intent, the two logs of wood<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 2:2-3.</i> should not be brought up with it, as is done for the other continuous offerings. [The rationale is that] it does not fulfill the obligation of the continuous offering, but instead, is like other freewill offerings.
When the sheep offered on Shavout<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 8:1, together with the two loaves brought on Shavuot are offered several burnt offerings, among them two rams, and two sheep as communal peace-offerings.</i> were slaughtered for a different intent or were slaughtered before their appropriate time or after their appropriate time, the blood should be cast upon the altar and the meat eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries note that it is forbidden to offer the <i>eimorim</i> on the festival and question whether the intent is that the meat may be eaten on the festival or whether it is necessary to wait until the evening.</i> even though the obligation of the community was not fulfilled. If it was the Sabbath, the blood should not be cast [on the altar].<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">For only obligatory sacrifices are offered on the Sabbath.</i> If it was cast [upon the altar], it is considered acceptable insomuch as the <i>eimorim</i> should be offered in the evening.
Similarly when the peace-offerings of a nazirite were offered for a different intent, even though the owner does not fulfill his obligation, they are eaten for a day and a night<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Like the peace-offerings of a nazirite, rather than for a two days and a night like other peace-offerings.</i> and do not require bread.<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">Bread must be brought with the peace-offerings of a nazirite (<i>Hilchot Nizirut</i> 8:1).</i> Similarly, when the guilt-offering of a nazirite or the guilt-offering of one [to be purified from] <i>tzara'at</i><sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">A skin malady similar to, but not identical to leprosy that renders one ritually impure.</i> were offered for a different intent, they are eaten, even though the owner does not fulfill his obligation.

Chapter 16

We have already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 13, Halachah 1.</i> that when a person has a disqualifying intent with regard to the place [a sacrifice will be offered or eaten] while performing one of the four [specified] services,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Slaughter, receiving the blood, bringing it to the altar, and casting it on the altar, as stated in Chapter 13, Halachah 4, and in the following halachah.</i> the sacrifice is disqualified, but it is not <i>piggul</i>. If he had a disqualifying intent with regard to the time [the sacrifice would be offered or eaten], it is <i>piggul</i>.<br>When does the above apply? When no other intent is combined together with the intent concerning time. If, however, an intent concerning the place - or with regard to the Paschal sacrifice or a sin-offering, an intent concerning the type of sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Implied is that with regard to other sacrifices which are not disqualified when slaughtered for another purpose, if there was a disqualifying intent concerning time, the fact that they were slaughtered for a different purpose does not prevent them from being considered as <i>piggul</i>.</i> - was combined with the intent concerning time, the sacrifice is disqualified, but it is not <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Chapter 18, Halachot 3 and 6, when a sacrifice is merely disqualified, a person who partakes of its meat is liable for lashes. If, however, it is deemed <i>piggul</i>, he is liable for <i>karet</i>, a much more serious punishment.</i><br>What is implied? If one slaughtered, received [the blood], brought it [to the altar], and cast it [on the altar] with a [disqualifying] intent concerning time or [even if] he had a [disqualifying] intent concerning time while [perfoming] one of these services and his intent was proper or he had no intent while performing the other services, [the sacrifice] was <i>piggul</i>. If, however, one slaughtered with a [disqualifying] intent concerning time, but received [the blood], brought it [to the altar], and cast it [on the altar] with a [disqualifying] intent concerning place or slaughtered with a [disqualifying] intent concerning place, but received [the blood], brought it [to the altar], and cast [on the altar] with a [disqualifying] intent concerning time, [the sacrifice] is not <i>piggul</i>, merely disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., there is no difference which disqualifying intent a person has first, as long as another intent is mixed together with the intent involving time, the sacrifice is disqualified, but not <i>piggul</i>. In <i>Zevachim</i> 29b, there is a differing opinion which maintains that if the intent involving time is first, the sacrifice is deemed <i>piggul</i> even if there is another disqualifying intent afterwards.</i><br>Similarly, if a Paschal sacrifice or sin-offering were slaughtered for a different purpose, but one received [the blood], brought it [to the altar], and cast it [on the altar] with a [disqualifying] intent concerning time, or one slaughtered with a [disqualifying] intent concerning time, but received [the blood], brought it [to the altar], and cast [on the altar] for a different purpose, [the sacrifice] is not <i>piggul</i>, merely disqualified.<br>The same principles apply with regard to offerings of fowl and meal-offerings. The term <i>piggul</i> applies only when a sacrifice is disqualified because of an intent concerning time, without a [disqualifying] intent concerning place combined with it, neither at the outset or at the end, nor is an intent concerning the type of sacrifice combined with it with regard to those sacrifices that are disqualified when offered for a different purpose.
When, while performing one or all of the four services, a person has the intent to eat<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">More precisely, that the meat be eaten whether by himself or by someone else.</i> an olive-sized portion of a substance that is fit to be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 14, Halachah 8.</i> outside [the limits of where it may be eaten] and an olive-sized portion on the following day;<br>an olive-sized portion on the following day and an olive-sized portion outside [the limits of where it may be eaten],<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">Both of these situations are examples where a disqualifying intent involving place is combined with a disqualifying intent concerning time.</i><br>half an olive-sized portion outside [the limits of where it may be eaten] and half an olive-sized portion on the following day, or<br>half an olive-sized portion on the following day and half an olive-sized portion outside [the limits of where it may be eaten],<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">As mentioned in Chapter 14, Halachah 10, to disqualify a sacrifice one must have an intent concerning an olive-sized portion. Here the Rambam is emphasizing that even though two different intents are involved, they may be combined.</i> the sacrifice is disqualified, but not <i>piggul</i>. Similarly, if one combined another disqualifying intent regarding offering [a sacrifice with one regarding time, the sacrifice] is disqualified, but not <i>piggul</i>.
If one had the intent to eat or to offer half an olive-sized portion with a [disqualifying] intent concerning place and to eat or to offer an olive-sized portion with a [disqualifying] intent concerning time, [the sacrifice] is <i>piggul</i>. [This applies] whether the [disqualifying] intent concerning the olive-sized portion was first or last. [The rationale is that] half an olive-sized portion is not significant in relation to an olive-sized portion.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">In the previous halachah, the half portion was considered significant, because there was no olive-sized portion present and it can be combined with another half portion. In this halachah, there is an olive-sized portion present. Hence, nothing concerning the smaller portion is significant.<br>The commentaries note that the Rambam apparently had a slightly different version of the Talmudic passage that serves as the source for this law than the standard printed text.</i>
If he had a [disqualifying] intent concerning time with regard to half an olive-sized portion and a [disqualifying] intent concerning place with regard to [another] half an olive-sized portion, and then a [disqualifying] intent concerning time with regard to [another] half an olive-sized portion, [the sacrifice] is <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the same law mentioned in the previous halachah applies in this instance as well. The fact that - had the person not had the second disqualifying intent concerning time, the first half portion would have been combined with the second half portion is not of consequence Ravva (<i>Zevachim</i> 31a) states lyrically: "The <i>piggul</i> arises, like one rising from sleep.".</i><br>Similarly, if one had a [disqualifying] intent concerning time with regard to half an olive-sized portion and then a [disqualifying] intent with regard to an olive-sized portion: with regard to half, a [disqualifying] intent concerning time, and with regard to the other half, a [disqualifying] intent concerning place, [the sacrifice] is <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Although the second disqualifying intent combines an intent concerning place and one concerning time and thus there is room to think that they cannot be separated from each other, the two intents concerning time are considered as one unit and the intent concerning place is disregarded.</i>
If one had an intent to present blood that should be presented on the lower portion [of the altar] on the upper portion [of the altar] or he had an intent to present [blood] that should be presented on the upper portion [of the altar] on the lower portion [of the altar] or the like, [these] intents that do not disqualify [a sacrifice], as explained;<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 2, Halachah 10.</i> if one combined a [disqualifying] intent concerning time, [the sacrifice] is <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Since these intents are not significant, they do not prevent the sacrifice from being considered as <i>piggul</i>.</i> If he combined a [disqualifying] intent concerning place alone,<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes that this word is problematic, because even if a disqualifying intent concerning time is also combined, the sacrifice is not <i>piggul</i>.</i> [the sacrifice] is unacceptable, but it is not <i>piggul</i>.
If one had an intent to present blood that should be presented on the upper portion [of the altar] on the lower portion on the following day,<br>[an intent] to present blood that should be presented on the upper portion [of the altar] on the lower portion on the following day,<br>he had an intent to present blood that should be presented in the Sanctuary on the outer altar on the following day, or<br>he had an intent to present blood that should be presented on the outer altar in the Sanctuary on the following day, [the sacrifice] is not <i>piggul</i>. Even though he had an intent concerning the time, since he changed the place where the blood was presented in his mind, [the sacrifice] is disqualified, but is not <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Ra'avad's objection follows the interpretation of <i>Zevachim</i> 27a advanced by Rashi. The Rambam, he maintains, has a different understanding of that Talmudic passage.<br>It is possible to distinguish between the situations mentioned in this halachah and those mentioned in the previous halachah as follows: In the situations mentioned here, the very same thought which concerned the place where the blood of the sacrifice would be offered concerned also its time.</i><br>Since we have explained in these halachot<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 2, Halachah 10.</i> that when blood is presented in a place other than the desired place, it is considered as if it was presented in the desired place, why is [the sacrifice] not considered as <i>piggul</i> because of this intent to present the blood outside of its desired place on the following day? [The rationale is that] even though the sacrifice is acceptable, since the blood was not presented in its proper place, it does not cause the meat to be permitted to be eaten, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 2, Halachah 10.</i> [There is a general principle:] In any situation where blood is cast upon [the altar, but it] does cause the meat to be permitted to be eaten, if one had the intent to present it at a time [after the prescribed time], it is not <i>piggul</i>. Therefore, [in the circumstances mentioned above,] if one had a [disqualifying] intent concerning time with regard to this sacrifice, it is unacceptable, but it is not <i>piggul</i>.
If a person had a disqualifying intent concerning time at the time he took [the handful of flour from a meal-offering], but did not have such an intent at the time he collected the frankincense or he had a disqualifying intent concerning time at the time he collected the frankincense, but did not have such an intent at the time he took [the handful of flour, the meal-offering] is disqualified, but is not <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">Although the priest had a disqualifying intent while performing one of these acts, we do not say that he had the same intent concerning the other unless he explicitly had such a thought.</i> [An offering becomes <i>piggul</i>] only when one has a disqualifying intent with regard to all [the substances which] cause it to be permitted [to be eaten], i.e., the handful of meal and the frankincense, at the time the handful is taken and the frankincense is collected, or when they are both placed into a sacred utensil, brought [to the altar], or cast [upon its pyre].
If at the time he cast the handful [of meal] on the altar's pyre, he had the intent to offer the frankincense on the following day, it is not <i>piggul</i>, because an intent to offer something on the altar's pyres while offering something else on the pyre does not cause the offering to be <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">For, as stated in Chapter 13, Halachah 6, a disqualifying intent concerning time causes a meal-offering to be considered <i>piggul</i> only when one thinks of it while performing one of the four services mentioned in the previous halachah.</i> Similarly, if one offered only the frankincense or only the handful [of meal] and had the intent to eat the remainder on the following day, [the meal-offering] is disqualified, but is not <i>piggul</i>. [The rationale is that a disqualifying intent involving only] half [the substances which] cause an offering to be permitted [to be eaten], does not cause it to be considered as <i>piggul</i>.<br>If, [by contrast,] one offered only the handful [of meal] and had the intent to offer the frankincense on the following day and afterwards offered the frankincense and had the intent to partake of the remainder [of the offering] on the following day, [the offering] is <i>piggul</i>, for the [disqualifying] intent concerning time has spread throughout the entire meal-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Even though neither of the intents in their own right would cause the sacrifice to be considered as <i>piggul</i>, when combined, they have this effect.<br>To explain: Until the frankincense is offered, it is forbidden to partake of the remaining portions of the meal-offering. Thus having the intent to offer the frankincense on the following day is equivalent to having the intent to partake of the remainder of the offering on the following day.</i>
If one offered a portion of the handful [of meal] the size of a sesame seed together with the frankincense with the intent that he eat a sesame seed-sized portion of the remainder [of the offering] on the following day, [even if] he continues offering the entire handful [of meal] with the frankincense with [the same disqualifying] intent concerning time, [the meal-offering] is disqualified, but is not <i>piggul</i>. [The rationale is that] even though eating little by little is an ordinary manner of eating,<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">As indicated by Chapter 14, Halachah 10, which states that if one had the intent to eat an olive-sized portion in an extended interval, he can be held liable.</i> this is not the ordinary manner in which entities are offered on the altar. Instead, it is like a meal-offering whose handful was not offered on the altar's pyre.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference of opinion concerning this instance in <i>Menachot</i> 16b. One sage maintains that the offering is acceptable. One maintains that it is <i>piggul</i>, and one rules as the Rambam rules here. The Rambam accepts that view, because there are a majority of opinions, negating either of the extremes (see <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i>
When there was frankincense placed on the meal-offering of a sinner or that of a <i>sotah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">Even though there are explicit negative commandments not to place frankincense on these offerings. See Chapter 14, Halachah 3; Chapter 11, Halachah 10.</i> and one had a [disqualifying] intent involving time before the frankincense was removed, [the offerings] are disqualified, but are not <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Because the offering is not fit to be brought on the altar until the frankincense is removed.</i> If after he collected the frankincense he had a [disqualifying] intent concerning time with regard to the offering, it is <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">Because the offering is fit to be brought on the altar.</i>
If the remaining portion [of a meal-offering] was diminished between the time the handful was taken and it was offered on the altar and then the handful was offered with a [disqualifying] intent concerning time, there is a doubt whether it was established [as fit to become] <i>piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">For the handful is fit to be offered on the altar. Hence if one has a disqualifying intent while placing the handful in a sacred vessel, bringing it to the altar, or offering it on the altar's pyre, there is room to say that it is <i>piggul</i>. (See Rashi, <i>Menachot</i> 12a.)</i> and thus it is <i>piggul</i> or it was not established<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">For it is unfit to partake of such a meal-offering.</i> and it is not <i>piggul</i>.

Chapter 17

Whenever blood must be presented on the outer altar and the first presentation was made with a proper intent and a second or further presentation was made for the sake of a different sacrifice or he had a [disqualifying] intent with regard to place or time, atonement is achieved and the sacrifice is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Hence the person(s) bringing the sacrifice are not required to bring another one.</i><br>If the first presentation [of blood] was made with a [disqualifying] intent with regard to time and [the priest] completed the presentation of the blood with a [disqualifying] intent with regard to place, [the sacrifice] is <i>piggul</i>. [The rationale is that] the first presentation of the blood is of fundamental importance.<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 2, Halachah 1.<br>Chapter 16, Halachah 1, states that when a disqualifying intent concerning place is combined with a disqualifying intent concerning time, the sacrifice is disqualified, but is not <i>piggul</i>. In the present instance, it is placed in the more severe category, because once the fundamental presentation was made in a manner that rendered the sacrifice <i>piggul</i>, the subsequent intentions the priest had are of no consequence.</i><br>In contrast, with regard to all of the presentations of blood on the inner altar - since they are all absolute requirements [for the offering of the sacrifice], as we explained<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 2, Halachah 3.</i> - if one of presentations was not made as required, but instead, one had a disqualifying intent, the sacrifice is unacceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">However, it is not <i>piggul</i>. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that it is not considered <i>piggul</i> because one must have the disqualifying intent concerning time when performing all of the presentations.</i> even if all the other presentations were made as required.
If one had a [disqualifying] intent with regard to time when making the first [of the presentations of the blood on the inner altar]<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:7.</i> and had no specific intent<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">Literally, the Rambam's words mean: "Remained silent with regard to the others."</i> regarding the remainder or he presented all of them as required with the exception of the final one, which he presented with a [disqualifying] intent with regard to time, [the sacrifice] is disqualified, but it is not <i>piggul</i>. [It is not given that distinction] unless one makes [all] the presentations with a [disqualifying] intent with regard to time, for they are all considered as one presentation.
[Having a disqualifying intent while] immersing one's finger in the blood of a sin-offering<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>ibid.</i>:8.</i> [whose blood is offered] on the inner altar can cause a sacrifice to become <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries note that this ruling appears to run contrary to the statements of <i>Zevachim</i> 44a: "If one had a disqualifying intent that would render an offering <i>piggul</i> inside the Sanctuary, the offering is not <i>piggul</i>." <i>Rambam LeAm</i> suggests that Rabbi Elazar the author of the statement cited does not accept the concept that one's intent when immersing one's finger in the blood can cause an offering to be considered as <i>piggul</i>. If, however, he would have accepted that concept, he would also have accepted the Rambam's ruling here.</i><br>What is implied? If at the time [the priest] immersed his finger in the blood, he had a disqualifying intent concerning time, it is as if he had such an intent when presenting [the blood on the altar].
If a priest was standing in the Temple Courtyard and he had a disqualifying intent concerning time with regard to one of the sin-offerings [whose blood is offered] on the inner altar with regard to an aspect of the sacrifice that is performed in the Sanctuary, [the offering] is not <i>piggul</i>. If he had such an intent with regard to an aspect that is performed in the Temple Courtyard, it is <i>piggul</i>.
What is implied? If a priest was standing in the Temple Courtyard and said: "I am slaughtering [this animal] with the intent of presenting its blood tomorrow,"<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">When they should be presented on the day the sacrificial animal is slaughtered.</i> [the offering] is not <i>piggul</i>, because presenting the blood is performed inside, in the Sanctuary.
If [a priest] was standing in the Sanctuary and he said: "I am presenting [the blood] with the intent to pour the remaining [blood]<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">On the outer altar (see <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:10).</i> on the following day, [the offering] is not <i>piggul</i>, because he had a disqualifying intent inside [the Temple Sanctuary] regarding a service performed outside. If, however, he was standing in the Temple Courtyard and slaughtered [the animal] with the intent to pour out the remainder [of the blood] on the following day or to offer the fats and the organs on the following day, [the offering] is <i>piggul</i>, for he had a [disqualifying] intent while outside concerning a service that is performed outside.
A [disqualifying intent] concerning a thanksgiving-offering causes the bread [that accompanies it] to become <i>piggul</i>, but a [disqualifying intent] concerning the bread does not cause the thanksgiving-offering to become <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">For the bread is secondary to and dependent on the sacrifice, but the sacrifice is not dependent on the bread [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 2:3)]. See also Chapter 15, Halachah 13.</i><br>What is implied? When one slaughtered a thanksgiving-offering and had the intent to partake of its meat, cast its blood on the altar, or offer its fats and organs on the following day, the offering and the bread are <i>piggul</i>. If he had the intent to partake of the bread on the following day, the bread alone is <i>piggul</i>; the thanksgiving-offering is not <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Rashi's commentary to <i>Menachot</i> 15a implies that it is forbidden to eat the meat. From the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah, it would appear that the meat is permitted entirely.</i>
Similar concepts apply with regard to the two sheep offered on Shavuot with the two breads offered with them. If one had a [disqualifying] intent concerning time with regard to the sheep, the two breads are considered as <i>piggul</i>. If he had the intent to partake of the two breads on the following day, the two breads are <i>piggul</i> and the sheep are not <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Here, also, the bread is considered as secondary to the sacrifice, but the sacrifice is not secondary to the meat [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>)].</i> If while performing one of the four [significant] services, [the priest] had the intent to partake of an olive-sized portion of the meat of the sacrifice together with the bread<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., half an olive-sized portion of meat and half an olive-sized portion of bread (Rashi, <i>Menachot, loc. cit.</i>).</i> tomorrow, the bread alone is <i>piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Menachot, loc. cit.</i>, mentions both of the situations spoken about in this halachah. One opinion maintains that the ruling was given both with regard to the bread on Shavuos and the bread of the thanksgiving-offering. A second view maintains that it was given with regard to the breads and the offering of Shavuos, for they are interrelated as evidenced by the fact that they are waved together (Leviticus 23:20 . It is possible, however, that it does not apply to the thanksgiving-offering. The Rambam accepts the more stringent view, because of the doubt involved (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).</i> and the thanksgiving-offering or the sheep are not <i>piggul.</i>
When [a priest] offers the two bowls of frankincense that accompany the showbread and, while offering them, had the intent to partake of the showbread on the following day, the bread is <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For it is the offering of the bowls of frankincense that enable the breads to be eaten.</i>
When one slaughters the two sheep for Shavuot and has the intent to eat one of the loaves on the following day, they are both <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">For the two loaves are considered as a single offering.</i>
If one offered the two bowls [of frankincense] and he had the intent to partake of one of the two arrangements of bread on the following day, both arrangements are <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Here too both arrangments are considered as a single offering.</i>
Similarly, if one had a [disqualifying] thought concerning time with regard to one of the breads of the thanksgiving-offering or with regard to one of the breads of meal-offering baked in an oven, all of the breads are <i>piggul</i>.
If, by contrast, one of the two breads [of Shavuot], one of the two arrangements [of the showbread], or one of the breads of the thanksgiving offering<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries have noted the apparent contradiction to Chapter 12, Halachah 14. See the notes to that halachah.</i>- whether before the casting on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">Of the blood of the sacrifices or the frankincense for the showbreads [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Menachot</i> 2:2)].</i> or afterwards - becomes impure, only that bread or that arrangement are forbidden to be eaten. What is pure may be eaten in its state of purity.
If, while performing the sacrificial service associated with one of the two sheep, [the priest] had the intent to eat an olive-sized portion of the two breads on the following day - and similarly, if while offering one of the two bowls [of frankincense], he had the intent to partake of an olive-sized portion of the showbread on the following day, the bread is disqualified, but it is not <i>piggul</i>. [It is given that distinction] only when he has a [disqualifying] intent while performing all the services that permit the bread to be eaten: [i.e.,] bringing both sheep and offering both bowls [of frankincense] on the altar's pyre.
If one slaughtered one [of the sheep] and had the intent to eat half an olive-sized portion from one loaf on the following day and slaughtered the second lamb and had the intent of eating half an olive-sized portion on the following day, [the two intents] are combined to render the loaves <i>piggul</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">For he had a disqualifying intent concerning time with regard to the entire offering that would enable the bread to be eaten.</i> Similar concepts apply with regard to the two bowls [of frankincense] and the two arrangements [of showbread].
If one had a [disqualifying] intent concerning time with regard to one of the two sheep and offered the second with a proper intent, the one that was offered with a [disqualifying] intent concerning time is <i>piggul</i> and the other is acceptable.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In this context, each of the sheep is considered as an independent entity.</i>
If one slaughtered one of [these two sheep] and had the intent while slaughtering it to partake of the meat of the other one on the following day, they are both acceptable. For the intent one has with regard to one is of no consequence regarding the second.
The two lambs [offered on] Shavuot do not cause the bread to be sanctified unless they are slaughtered.<br>What is implied? If one slaughtered them and cast their blood [on the altar] for the sake of another sacrifice, he did not sanctify the bread. If he slaughtered them with the proper intent and cast their blood [on the altar] for the sake of another sacrifice, the bread is sanctified, but is not sanctified.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">It appears that the Rambam follows the view of Ravva (<i>Menachot</i> 13b) that bread is considered as consecrated, but it is forbidden to be eaten .</i><br>If they slaughtered it for the sake of another sacrifice even though he cast [the blood] for the proper intent, the bread was not sanctified.
When the two loaves were taken out [of the Temple Courtyard] between the slaughter [of the two sheep] and the casting [of their blood] and the blood of the sheep was cast on the altar with a [disqualifying] intent concerning time, the bread becomes <i>piggul</i> even though it is outside [the Temple Courtyard]. For casting [the blood] has an effect on [bread] that was taken out even though it is still outside [the Temple Courtyard].<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The place where the bread is located is not of consequence.</i>
When the two sheep offered on Shavuos were slaughtered with the proper intent and the breads were lost, they are disqualified if their blood was cast [on the altar] with the desired intent.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">For as stated in <i>Hilchot Temidim UMusafim</i> 8:15, the offering of the bread is a fundamental requirement for the offering of the sheep and if the bread is lost, the sheep should be destroyed by fire.</i> If their blood was cast [on the altar] with a [disqualifying] intent concerning time<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> maintains that this is a printing error and the text should read "with an intent for another sacrifice." This view, however, is not borne out by the manuscripts and early printings of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>.</i> after the bread was lost, there is an unresolved doubt if [the meat of the sheep] is permitted to be eaten or not.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">According to the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, the Rambam's ruling can be explained as follows: One might think that the meat would be permitted to be eaten, because they are peace-offerings and when a peace-offering is offered for the sake of another offering, it is permitted to be eaten, as stated in Chapter 15, Halachah 1. On the other hand, since the sheep are associated with the bread and the bread is lost, there is room to say that they have been disqualified. A question concerning this issue was raised by <i>Menachot</i> 47b. Rav Yosef Corcus maintains that according to the Rambam, the question was left unresolved. Rashi maintains that the question is rhetorical and that the meat is disqualified.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> notes, however, that Rabbenu Yehoshua, one of the Rambam's descendants, was asked about the matter and explained the question according to the existing text. According to his view, the issue is that since the blood was cast on the altar after the bread was lost, the Sages had a question whether to consider their meat as ordinary meat or whether the meat should still be considered as sacrificial meat, because the sheep were slaughtered before the bread was lost.</i>

Chapter 18

Anyone who has an incorrect intent [while performing] sacrificial service violates a negative commandment,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The wording used by the Rambam is often employed when referring to one of the 613 mitzvot. Nevertheless, neither in the listing at the beginning of these halachot, nor in <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i>, does he count this charge in that reckoning. The Ramban (in his <i>Hosafot</i> to <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i>, negative commandment 4) does give this charge that distinction. <i>Megilat Esther</i> explains that this charge is part of the directive to offer sacrifices in the proper manner and hence need not be considered as a separate mitzvah. See also <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 144) where the issue is discussed.</i> for [Leviticus 7:18] states: "He may not intent this."
According to the Oral Tradition, it was taught that included in this prohibition is not to cause sacrificial offerings to be disqualified through thought, for this is comparable to causing a blemish in sacrificial animals. Nevertheless, [a transgressor] is not punished by lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">As is one who causes a blemish to sacrificial animals (<i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 1:7).</i> for thought is not considered as deed.<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">And lashes are given only when one violates a transgression while performing a deed (<i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 18:2).</i>
Whenever a sacrifice is defined as unacceptable - whether it was disqualified because of an intent, an action, or something which caused it to be disqualified - anyone who partakes of an olive-sized portion of it intentionally is liable for lashes, as [a result of the prohibition, Deuteronomy 14:3]: "Do not partake of anything abhorrent."<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 140) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 469) consider this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i>
According to the Oral Tradition, we learned<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">See the <i>Sifri</i> to the verse cited.</i> that the verse was warning solely against [partaking of] sacrificial animals that were disqualified.<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is emphasizing this point lest one think that the charge also refers to other prohibited substances. This stress is necessary, for otherwise the prohibition could be considered a prohibition of a general nature (<i>lav shebiklalut</i>). Lashes are not given for violating a prohibition of this nature (<i>Hilchot Sanhedrin, loc. cit.</i>).</i>
Similarly, when sacrificial animals which were [intentionally] blemished, a person who eats an olive-sized portion of them is liable for lashes, for they are included in the category of "anything abhorrent." [It is forbidden to partake of them] until they contract another blemish, [at which time,]<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">After they have been redeemed. As the <i>Rambam LeAm</i> elaborates, in addition to contracting a blemish, an animal dedicated as a sacrifice must be redeemed before the prohibition against partaking of its meat is lifted. (This constitutes a difference between the laws pertaining to such an animal and a firstborn animal.) Even after the Scriptural prohibition is lifted, there is a Rabbinic prohibition to partake of its meat until it contracts another blemish on its own accord. (This prohibition was instituted as a penalty lest one intentionally inflict such a blemish. See <i>Bechorot</i> 34b.)</i> they may be eaten because of the blemish, as we explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam's wording has aroused the attention of the commentaries, for this law is stated in <i>Hilchot Bechorot</i> 2:7, where the entire law stated here is mentioned. As such, it would have been more correct for the Rambam to have stated "as will be explained." Some have suggested that the intent here is to the concept that a sacrificial animal that has contracted a blemish may be eaten after being redeemed, as stated in <i>Hilchot Issurei Mizbeiach</i> 1:10.</i> Whenever there is a unresolved doubt whether [a sacrificial animal] has been disqualified, lashes are not given.
Whenever a sacrifice has been deemed <i>piggul</i> because of a disqualifying intent concerning time, as we explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">In various halachot from Chapter 13, Halachah 1, onward.</i> anyone who partakes of an olive-sized portion of it intentionally is liable for <i>karet</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Literally, that the soul is cut off. This involves premature death in this world (before the age of 50, <i>Mo'ed Kattan</i> 28a) and the soul not meriting a portion in the world to come (<i>Hilchot Teshuvah</i> 8:1).</i> as [implied by Leviticus 7:18]: "The soul which partakes of it will bear its sin."<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Since this phrase is also used with regard to <i>notar</i> (sacrificial meat left beyond its limit) in Leviticus 19:8 and the punishment of <i>karet</i> is explicitly stated with regard to that prohibition in that verse, the <i>Sifra</i> makes an equation with regard to the punishment for the two transgressions.</i> If one partakes of [the meat of such a sacrifice] inadvertently, he should bring a fixed sin-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">This term is used to differentiate between this offering and an adjustable guilt-offering in which instance, the sacrifice the person required to bring is dependent on the person's means. See <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 1:3-4.</i>
One is not liable for <i>karet</i> unless one partakes of entities that were permitted for consumption, either by a person or by the altar.<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">Entities eaten by a person or consumed by the altar's pyre.<br>In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 4:3), the Rambam explains this concept as follows: The prohibition of <i>piggul</i> is derived from Leviticus 7:18 which pertains to the peace-offerings. Our Sages explain that the peace-offerings are unique in that they involve both consumption by the altar and consumption by man and that there is an act that permits such consumption (the offering of the blood permits the fats and organs to be offered and offering them permits the meat to be eaten). Hence this is established as a general rule with regard to all sacrifices.</i> If, however, one eats of the entity that permits [the sacrifice to be eaten] itself, one is not liable for <i>karet.</i> Instead, he is liable for lashes like one who partakes of disqualified sacrificial animals for which the transgression of <i>karet</i> is not involved.<br>What is implied? When a meal-offering becomes <i>piggul</i>, one who partakes of an olive-sized portion of the remaining [meal]<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the portion to be eaten by man.</i> intentionally, is liable for <i>karet</i>. If, however, he partakes of the handful [that is separated to be offered on the altar] or from the frankincense, he is not liable for <i>karet</i>, for these are the substances that enable [the meal to be eaten] by men. Similarly, when a sacrifice is deemed <i>piggul</i>, one who partakes of an olive-sized portion of its meat or of the fats and organs offered on the altar or from the meat of a burnt-offering is liable for <i>karet</i>. If, however, he partakes of an olive-sized portion of the blood, he is not liable for <i>karet</i>, because [the casting of] the blood permits the fats and the organs to be offered on the altar and [the offering of] the fats and the organs permit the meat [to be eaten by] a person. [Similarly,] the blood of a burnt-offering permits its meat [to be offered] on the altar. [The presentation of] the blood of a sin-offering of fowl permits its meat to be eaten by the priests. [The presentation of] the blood of a burnt-offering of fowl permits its meat to be offered on the altar.<br>[The presentation of] the blood of a sin-offering that is burnt permits its fats and the organs to be offered on the altar. Therefore one is liable for [partaking of] the fats and the organs as <i>piggul</i>. [Offering] the handful [of meal] and the frankincense permit a meal-offering to [be eaten by] the priests. [Offering] the two sheep<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The sheep themselves, however, can also become <i>piggul</i>, as stated in Chapter 17, Halachah 16.</i> on Shavuot permit the two loaves to [be eaten by] the priests. [Offering] the two bowls of frankincense permit the showbread to [be eaten by] the priests. Sacrificial entities that do not have entities that permit them [to be consumed either by the altar or by man], e.g., the meat of the sin-offerings that are burnt or the meal-offerings that are burnt, are never deemed as <i>piggul</i>.
These are the entities that are never deemed as <i>piggul</i>:<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">For there is no other act performed that enables these to be offered.</i> the handful [of meal] and the frankincense; the incense-offering; the blood [of any sacrifice]; wine - whether wine that comes as part of the accompanying offerings<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i>, ch. 2.</i> or wine that is offered independently;<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ibid.</i> 17:12.</i> and the meal-offerings that are burnt in their entirety; for there is not a handful that permits them, e.g., the meal-offering of a priest or the meal-offering of the accompanying offerings; the meat of the sin-offerings that are burnt; and the <i>log</i> of oil brought by a nazirite.<br>If one would ask [with regard to the latter instance]: Does not the blood of the guilt-offering [brought by the nazirite] permit the oil to be eaten? [In resolution, it can be said that] one is not dependent on the other, for a person may bring his guilt-offering one day and the <i>log</i> of oil after several days, as will be explained in the appropriate place.<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i> 4:2.<br>It must be noted that the latter point is the subject of a difference of opinion in the Mishnah. Rabbi Shimon maintains that the <i>log</i> cannot become <i>piggul</i>, while Rabbi Meir maintains that it can for the reasons stated here. Although the standard published text of the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah states that the halachah follows Rabbi Shimon's view (as the Rambam rules here), Rav Kappach notes that all the manuscript copies of the Commentary to the Mishnah state that the halachah does not follow Rabbi Shimon.</i>
It is forbidden to leave sacrificial meat beyond the time in which it may be eaten,<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 120) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 142) consider this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> as [Leviticus 22:30] states with regard to the thanksgiving-offering: "Do not leave it over until the morning." This same applies to all other sacrifices.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">Nevertheless, <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandments 117-119) does count the prohibitions against leaving over the meat of the Paschal sacrifice, the <i>chagigah</i> offering, and the second Paschal sacrifice as separate commandments.</i><br>One who leaves over sacrificial meat is not liable for lashes, for Scripture enables [the transgression] to be corrected<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> questions why the Rambam mentions this point. True, it is mentioned by <i>Pesachim</i> 84a, but that passage follows the opinion that lashes can be given for the violation of a prohibition even if a deed is not involved. The Rambam (<i>Hilchot Sanhedrin</i> 18:2) maintains that lashes are not given unless the transgression involves a deed. Hence, seemingly, he does not have to add the explanation given here.</i> by [the fulfillment of] a positive commandment,<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 91) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 143) consider this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> as [Exodus 12:10] states: "That which remains from it until the morning should be burnt with fire."
One who partakes of an olive-sized portion of the meat of sacrifices that were left beyond their required time intentionally is liable for <i>karet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 131) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 215) consider this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> If he did so unintentionally, he must bring a fixed sin-offering, as [Leviticus 19:8]: "He who partakes of it shall bear his sin, for he has desecrated what is holy unto God; [that soul] shall be cut off."<br>From when is a person held liable for partaking of <i>notar</i> [this left-over meat]? If it is from sacrifices of the most sacred order, he is liable from dawn.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">The first shining of the light on the eastern horizon, between 72 and 120 minutes before sunrise according to the various authorities.</i> If it is from sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">With the exception of the thanksgiving-offering and the nazirite's ram for which one is liable from dawn of the day following their sacrifice.</i> he is liable from sunset on the second day which is the beginning of the third day.<br>Where does the Torah warn against <i>piggul</i> and <i>notar</i>? With regard to [the sacrifices of] the dedication [of the Sanctuary], [Exodus 29:34] states: "[They shall not be eaten, for they are holy."<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The commentaries note that the Rambam's citation of the verse is not entirely exact. See also <i>Sefer HaMitzvot, loc. cit.</i></i> This warns against [partaking of] any [sacrificial food] disqualified [in the Sanctuary], [stating] that there is a negative commandment against partaking of it.
<i>Piggul</i> and <i>notar</i> can be combined to reach the minimum measure of an olive-sized portion<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">From the wording of the Mishnah (<i>Meilah</i> 4:3), one might think that these two prohibitions are not combined. Nevertheless, the Talmud (<i>Meilah</i> 17b) states that the Mishnah is speaking about the impurity of one's hands, but that with regard to the prohibition against partaking of the food, they may be combined.</i> [for which one is held liable]. All sacrificial foods that became <i>piggul</i> or <i>notar</i> can be combined [for this purpose].
It is forbidden to cause sacrificial foods to contract impurity or to create a circumstance that makes them impure,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">Although the Rambam's wording implies that a Scriptural prohibition is involved, he does not include it as one of the 613 mitzvot. See a parallel in <i>Hilchot Terumah</i> 12:1.</i> for he disqualifies them.<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>Or Sameach</i> comments that the Rambam's wording implies that if the sacrificial foods were disqualified for other reasons, it is permitted to cause them to contract impurity. See Chapter 19, Halachot 5-6.</i> One who makes sacrificial foods impure is not liable for lashes, but a person who is pure who partakes of an olive-sized portion of sacred foods that have become impure is liable for lashes,<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">Partaking of sacrificial foods that have become impure is considered by <i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 130) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 145) as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> as [Leviticus 7:19] states: "Meat that will touch anything impure should not be eaten."<br>The same also applies with regard to other sacrifices. [For example,] if one partakes of an olive-sized portion of the frankincense of a meal-offering that became impure after it was sanctified in a utensil is liable for lashes. [This refers] both to sacrificial food that became impure before atonement was attained<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The attainment of atonement refers to the casting of the blood on the altar.<br>Although the sacrifice became impure before the blood was cast on the altar, after the fact, it is acceptable, because the forehead plate of the High Priest causes such sacrifices to be considered acceptable (<i>Menachot</i> 25b). And since, after the fact, it is acceptable, one is liable for partaking of it if it became <i>piggul</i>.</i> or afterwards, whether it became impure because of contact with a primary source of impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">In the original, an <i>av tumah</i>, literally, "a father of impurity," an object deemed inherently impure by Scripture decree which has the potential to make other objects impure. See the Rambam's introduction to the Order of <i>Taharot</i> in his Commentary to the Mishnah and also, <i>Hilchot Tumat Meit</i> 5:7 for more details regarding this and the term mentioned in the following note.</i> or a derivative of impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In the original, a <i>v'lad tumah</i>, literally, "the offspring of impurity," an object that contracts ritual impurity through contract with a primary source of impurity, which in certain instances can impart impurity to other substances.</i> of Scriptural origin.
