If, however, sacrificial foods contracted impurity that is Rabbinic in origin, one is not liable for lashes for partaking of them; he does, however, receives stripes for rebellious conduct.<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The punishment given anyone who violates a Rabbinic ordinance.</i><br>[Even one who partakes of sacrificial food that contracts impurity of Scriptural origin] is liable for lashes only when he partakes of it after its blood is cast [on the altar]. If, by contrast, he partakes of it before the casting of its blood, he is not liable for lashes because he partook of impure sacrificial food.<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">Because such a sacrifice is disqualified and, as an initial preference, its blood should not be offered on the altar. The <i>Mishneh LiMelech</i> states that he is, however, liable for lashes for partaking of sacrificial food before its blood was cast on the altar, as stated in <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 11:1,4.</i> He does, however, receive stripes for rebellious conduct.
Any person who contracted a form of impurity that would make him liable for <i>karet</i> for entering the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 3:13-14. As mentioned in the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i>, there are certain states of ritual impurity for which one is not liable for <i>karet</i> for entering the Temple. This prohibition, however, focuses only on those concerning which this penalty can be incurred, because of an association between the words <i>mikdash</i>, "sanctuary," and <i>kodesh</i>, "sacrificial food."</i> who ate an olive-sized portion of sacrificial food - whether the food is pure or impure<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">There is a difference of opinion concerning this matter in the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 13:2) because there are two prohibitions involved: the prohibition against partaking of impure sacrificial meat and the prohibition against a person who is impure partaking of sacrificial meat. Rabbi Yossi maintains that since the meat is impure and unfit to be eaten, we are not concerned whether the person is impure or not. The Sages, by contrast, maintain that since the impure person is forbidden to partake of pure sacrificial food, the prohibition also applies when partakes of impure sacrificial food. The Rambam accepts the Sages' opinion. See also Halachah 16.</i> - intentionally is liable for <i>karet</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (negative commandment 129) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 167) consider this prohibition as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> as [Leviticus 7:20] states: "A soul that will partake... of the slaughter of the peace-offerings that are for God while his impurity is upon him and [that soul] shall be cut off." If he partakes of it inadvertently, he must bring an adjustable guilt-offering.<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">An obligation for which the offering changes dependent on the person's financial capacity (see Leviticus, ch. 5; <i>Hilchot Shegagot</i> 10:1).</i><br>What is the source that teaches that the verse is speaking about a situation where the person's body is ritually impure?<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">For one could interpret the above verse as referring to sacrificial food that contracted ritual impurity.</i> [Leviticus 7:21] states: "When a soul will touch any impurity, whether impurity of a human, an impure animal, or an impure creature and he partook of the meat of the slaughter of the peace-offerings that are for God while his impurity is upon him and [that soul] shall be cut off."<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">This verse clearly indicates that the passage is speaking about a person who has contracted ritual impurity.</i> The same applies to all other sacrifices of the altar.<br>Where did [the Torah] warn concerning this prohibition? With regard to a woman who gave birth, [Leviticus 12:4] states: "She shall not touch anything that is sanctified."<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">The interpretation of the verse is explained in the following halachah.</i>
According to the Oral Tradition, it was taught that [the verse] is a warning to a person who is impure, that he or she should not partake of sacrificial food before he immerses himself [in a <i>mikveh</i>]. If one partakes of sacrificial food after immersion, before sunset of that day and before bringing the sacrifice that brings atonement,<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Mechusrei Kapparah</i>, ch. 1, to be permitted to partake of sacrificial food or to enter the Temple, a <i>zav</i> (a male who has secretions similar to those produced by gonorrhea), a <i>zavah</i> (a woman who experiences vaginal bleeding outside her menstrual cycle), a woman who gives birth or miscarries, and a person afflicted by the skin condition of <i>tzara'at</i> must do the following after they are fit to emerge from their ritual impurity: a) immerse in the <i>mikveh</i>, b) wait until nightfall after immersion, and c) bring the appropriate sacrifice.</i> he or she is liable for lashes, but not for <i>karet</i>, for [Leviticus 7:20] states "while his impurity is upon him." [Implied is that] the full measure of impurity must be upon him.<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since the person has already immersed in the <i>mikveh</i>, a certain dimension of his or her ritual impurity has been removed. Hence, although he or she is liable for lashes for this transgression, there is no liability for <i>karet</i>.<br>The Ra'avad differs with the Rambam and maintains that even in such a situation, one is liable for <i>karet</i>. As mentioned in the notes to <i>Hilchot Bi'at HaMikdash</i> 3:9, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> cites Talmudic passages which could be used as support for both positions.</i>
If a person was impure because of impurity resulting from a Rabbinic ordinance, he is not liable for lashes [if he partakes of sacrificial food].<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">For according to Scriptural Law, he is not liable. Compare to Halachah 12.</i> Needless to say, he is not liable for <i>karet</i>. He does, however, receive stripes for rebellious conduct.
One is not liable for <i>karet</i> for partaking of sacrificial food that is made permitted by a particular act unless one partakes of it after that act is performed. If, however, he partakes of the meat [of a sacrifice] before its blood is cast on the altar, he is not liable for lashes for partaking of sacrificial meat while impure.<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">In this instance as well, according to the <i>Mishneh LiMelech</i>, the person would be liable for lashes for partaking of sacrificial food before its blood was cast on the altar, as stated in <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 11:1,4.</i><br>This is the general principle: Whenever sacrificial food is permitted because [of the performance of] a particular act, one is not liable for the violation of any of the prohibitions against partaking of <i>piggul, notar</i>, or impure sacrificial food unless the act which permits partaking of the food was performed according to law. Whenever there is not a given act that makes sacrificial food permitted, once it is sanctified in a consecrated vessel, one is liable for partaking of it if it becomes impure. Even if [sacrificial] meat becomes impure before the person partaking of it becomes impure, if the act [that would have] permitted the meat to be eaten was performed and the person partook of the sacrificial food, he is liable for <i>karet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">See Halachah 13 and notes.</i> Similarly, if a person who is impure partakes of the meat of the sin-offerings that are burnt, after their blood is cast [on the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">For afterwards, they are ready to be burnt.</i> he is liable for <i>karet</i>.
It was already explained for you,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., this conclusion can be reached by comparing Halachah 7 with the previous halachah. An equation is made between sacrificial meat that is <i>notar</i> and that which contracted ritual impurity.</i> that even entities for which one is not liable for <i>piggul</i>, one may be liable for <i>notar</i> or because the object contracted ritual impurity?<br>What is implied? There is no liability for <i>piggul</i> for sacrificial entities that do not have an activity that permits them [to be eaten], but one may be held liable [for partaking of them if they] became <i>notar</i> or impure in such circumstances.<br>Similarly, even though there cannot be liability for <i>piggul</i> for the very entities that cause the sacrificial meat to be permitted, as we explained,<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">In Halachah 7.</i> one can be liable [for partaking of such entities if the sacrificial meat] became <i>notar</i> or impure with the exception of the blood. For one is liable for only one transgression for partaking of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., the prohibition against partaking of blood, which appears uniformly, both to the blood of sacrificial animals and to that of ordinary animals. Since it is already prohibited, none of the other prohibitions apply to it. See <i>Zevachim</i> 4:5.</i>
When a person who is impure partakes of the fats and organs to be offered on the altar, he is liable for <i>karet</i>.
[If an impure person] partook<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">This is the version accepted by the R. Shabsi Frankel edition of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i>. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> offers a different version of the text.</i> of a Paschal sacrifice that was not roasted, or breads of the thanksgiving-offering of which the breads [to be given to the priest] were not taken, he is liable for <i>karet</i> because of the impurity of [his] body even though they are not fit for their [purpose at this stage].<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., although these activities are necessary for these sacrifices to be acceptable, a person can still be held liable for partaking of the sacrifice in a state of ritual impurity.</i><br>It is impossible for a person to be liable for the transgressions of <i>piggul</i> and <i>notar</i> with regard to consumption of the same sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">This is a general rule. There are several particular aspects to it, as explained in <i>Keritot</i> 14a.</i> [The rationale is that] <i>piggul</i> is a sacrifice that was disqualified because of an unacceptable thought concerning time. It does not fulfill the obligations of a sacrifice and is not acceptable at all. <i>Notar</i>, by contrast, refers to the remnants of a sacrifice that was offered as required which remained after the time [prescribed] for its consumption.
When one combined [different types of sacrificial food that were] <i>piggul, notar</i>, and impure and partook of them,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">This is speaking about a situation where there is an olive-sized portion of all the prohibited substances. Nevertheless, one might think that the presence of one might nullify the other. The person receives a set of lashes for each prohibition he violates.</i> he is liable. Even though there was more of one type of prohibited substance than another, it does not nullify it, because [these] prohibited substances do not nullify each other.<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Instead, as stated in Halachah 11, they are combined together.<br>The commentaries have noted that this ruling appears to contradict the Rambam's own ruling in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 16:18, that <i>orlah</i> nullifies the presence of <i>terumah</i>. See also the <i>Beit Yosef, Yoreh De'ah</i> 98.<br>It can be explained that in <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i>, the Rambam is speaking about an instance where there is sufficient amount of kosher food to nullify the prohibited substance according to Scriptural Law (for only a majority is required). Hence, for the additional amount required by Rabbinic Law, a forbidden substance is also sufficient. In this instance, however, the substances are not nullified according to Scriptural Law. Hence, one forbidden substance cannot nullify another.</i>
When [sacrificial meat] that was <i>piggul, notar,</i> or impure was brought up to the altar, once the fire takes hold of the majority of it, their prohibitions take flight.<br>The fat and the organs can be combined with the meat, both with regard to a burnt offering or to other sacrifices with regard to the prohibitions of <i>piggul, notar,</i> or [sacrificial meat] that has become impure.
When a sacrifice becomes <i>piggul</i> or <i>notar</i> after the time for its consumption passes and a person partakes of it, from its skin, from the sauce or spices [in which it is cooked], the <i>allal</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">In Chapter 14, Halachah 7, the Rambam defines this as: "the meat that slipped by the knife at the time the animal was skinned and remains cleaving to the hide."</i> the <i>murah</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">The thin membrane that clings to the hide and separates between it and the meat; it is not fit to be eaten (<i>ibid.</i>).</i> from the <i>giddim</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 3:4), the Rambam explains that this is a general term referring to blood vessels, nerves, and sinews.</i> the horns, and the hoofs, the nails, the beak [of a fowl], its feathers,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Taharot</i> 1:2), the Rambam explains that this term refers to the growth that remains after the large feathers are removed.</i> or its eggs, he is not liable for <i>karet</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">For these entities are not considered as fit to be eaten.</i> Similarly, if an impure person partakes of these substances from an acceptable sacrifice, he is not liable for <i>karet</i>. He is, however, given stripes for rebellious conduct.
If one partook of a fetus or a placenta, he is liable for [violating the prohibitions of] <i>piggul, notar,</i> or [sacrificial meat] that has become impure like one who partake of any other [portion of] the meat of a sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Based on a comparison to Chapter 14, Halachah 7, the Ra'avad explains this should be understood as meaning that if one intended to eat the meat of a sacrifice after the time when it was supposed to be eaten, the entire sacrifice, even the fetus and the placenta, become <i>piggul</i>. If, however, one's intent is to partake of the fetus or the placenta, the sacrifice does not become <i>piggul</i>.</i>
[The prohibitions of] <i>piggul, notar,</i> or [sacrificial meat] that has become impure do not apply with regard to sacrifices brought by gentiles.<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 3:2, burnt-offerings brought by a non-Jew may be offered on the altar. According to the Rambam, even though such sacrifices are acceptable, the prohibitions mentioned, although they apply to sacrifices brought by Jews, do not apply to these offerings.<br>The commentaries note that the Rambam's ruling appears to reflect the understanding of Rabbi Shimon in <i>Zevachim</i> 4:3. However, most authorities - including the Rambam in his Commentary to the Mishnah - follow the view of Rabbi Yossi who differs. It can, nevertheless, be explained that Rabbi Shimon's opinion concerns only sacrifices offered outside the Temple. The preceding clause of the mishnah concerning <i>piggul</i> and the like is accepted by all opinions (<i>Kessef Mishneh</i>).<br>In truth, the Rambam's opinion concerning this issue is somewhat problematic. He accepts Rabbi Yossi’s view in his ruling in <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 19:16. Nevertheless, his ruling in <i>Hilchot Me'ilah</i> 5:15, like the one here, appears to follow Rabbi Shimon's view.</i> Nor do they apply to sacrificial blood, as explained.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">Halachah 17 above.</i> Similarly, one is not liable for <i>karet</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad objects to the Rambam's ruling, explaining that it reflects the understanding of Rabbi Shimon in the above mishnah. Rav Kapach notes that in the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah, his original writing was rubbed out and his final statement is: "The halachah does not follow Rabbi Shimon." The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> offers a resolution to the difficulty explaining that the Rambam is postulating that the transgressor is liable for lashes and not for <i>karet</i>. The exemption for <i>karet</i> is accepted by all opinions. Rabbi Shimon exempts the transgressor from lashes as well, but the initial opinion of the mishnah - which is accepted by the Rambam - holds him liable on that account.</i> for [the prohibitions of] <i>piggul, notar,</i> or partaking of [sacrificial entities] while impure<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>), the Rambam equates a person partaking of them while he is impure with one partaking of them while they are impure. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> debates the Rambam's intent here.</i> for partaking of frankincense, the incense offering, or the wood [of the altar].<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Although wood does not usually contract impurity, sacrificial wood may [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>)].</i>

Chapter 19

It is a positive commandment to burn all the sacrifices that have become impure,<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 90) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 146) include this as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah.</i> as [Leviticus 7:19] states: "And the meat that will touch anything that [imparts] impurity may not be eaten. It must be burnt with fire."<br>Similarly, it is a mitzvah to burn <i>notar</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sefer HaMitzvot</i> (positive commandment 91) and <i>Sefer HaChinuch</i> (mitzvah 143) include this as one of the 613 mitzvot of the Torah. There the Rambam states that the commandment was instituted to correct the transgression of leaving the meat past its required time. See Chapter 18, Halachah 9.</i> as [<i>ibid.</i>:17] states: "What remains from the meat of the sacrifice on the third day<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">This is speaking about a peace-offering which may be eaten on the day it was offered and on the following day. If it was left for a third day, it must be burnt.</i> shall be burnt with fire." Included in [the category of] <i>notar</i> is <i>piggul</i> and all other sacrifices that were disqualified. They must all be burnt.
When a sacrifice becomes <i>piggul</i> or is [otherwise] disqualified, it should be burnt in the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">In the Temple Courtyard, but not on the altar. See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 7:3-4 which states that there are three places where sacrifices are burnt.</i> immediately. Whenever there is a doubt whether a sacrifice has been disqualified, it should be left until the next day<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">We have translated the term used by the Rambam according to its halachic intent. The literal meaning is that it should be left long enough to decompose until it loses the appearance of meat. Our Sages understood that as being a twenty-four hour period.<br>Leaving the sacrifice until the next day disqualifies it and requires it to be burnt. Since initially there was a doubt involved, this is the desired course of action.</i> and then burnt in the Temple.
There is an unresolved doubt with regard to the bulls and the goats which are to be burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they are burnt in the ash heap outside of Jerusalem after their fats and organs were offered on the altar's pyre.</i> whether leaving them overnight or taking them outside [the Temple Courtyard] before the time to take them out<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., before their blood is cast upon the altar.</i> disqualifies their meat as it would their fats and organs or not.<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">The fats and the organs would definitely be disqualified in such circumstances. <i>Zevachim</i> 104b questions whether this would also apply with regard to the meat of a sacrifice and leaves that question unresolved.</i> Therefore, as a stringency, it is considered as if they were disqualified and they should be burnt in the Temple Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Rav Yosef Corcus and the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> question the Rambam's decision, because the Talmud's query seems to follow the opinion of Reish Lakish (<i>Zevachim</i> 89b) who maintains that when the meat of sacrifices of a lesser degree of holiness was removed from the Temple Courtyard before their blood was cast on the altar, the sacrifice is disqualified. According to Rabbi Yochanan who maintains that in such an instance, the sacrifice is not disqualified, seemingly, these sacrifices are also not disqualified.<br>Rav Yosef Corcus resolves the issue, explaining that even Rabbi Yochanan maintains that the meat of those sacrifices is disqualified. Hence, there is reason to question what his opinion would be in this instance.</i><br>Similarly, there is an unresolved doubt if half [such] an animal was taken out [including] the majority of one limb.<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">If the majority of an animal is not taken out of the Temple Courtyard, it is not disqualified. <i>Zevachim</i> 105a speaks about a situation where only half an animal was taken outside the Temple Courtyard, but included in that half was the majority of one limb. If the remainder of that limb was considered as outside the Temple Courtyard, the majority of the animal would be considered to be outside.</i> Therefore, as a stringency, it is considered as if it was disqualified and it should be burnt in the Temple Courtyard.<br>Similarly, if five people carried [such] an animal to take it outside the Temple Courtyard and three departed from [the Courtyard] and two remained, but the three removed half of the animal, [such animals] are disqualified because of the doubt and they should be burnt in the Temple Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad takes issue with the Rambam on both of these instances, maintaining that the Talmudic passage which is the Rambam's source (<i>Zevachim</i> 104b-105a) can be interpreted differently. The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> explains that the Rambam's interpretation can be substantiated.</i> It appears to me that in such instances,<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">All three instances mentioned above.</i> it is not necessary to wait until the following day. [The rationale is that] regardless [such animals] will be burnt,<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., they will definitely be burnt. The question is only where they will be burnt, whether in the Temple Courtyard, like sacrifices that are disqualified or outside Jerusalem, as is required for these bulls and goats. When, by contrast, a doubt arises with regard to other sacrificial animals, there is no obligation to burn them unless they are disqualified. On the contrary, burning them would be considered as degrading for sacred articles (Rav Yosef Corcus). Hence they are required to be left until the next day, so that they will definitely be disqualified.</i> even if they are not disqualified.
[The following laws apply when] meat is found in the Temple Courtyard: [Whole] limbs are [considered as parts of] burnt-offerings. Pieces [of meat] are considered as parts of sin-offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Even though it is permitted to cut the meat of burnt-offerings into portions (<i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 6:19), the priests were not accustomed to doing so. Rather a burnt-offering was cut up into several large portions and then brought to the altar. Hence if the meat of an animal was cut up into smaller pieces, one could assume that it was a sin-offering (the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Shekalim</i> 7:3)]. The meat of such offerings must be eaten in the Temple Courtyard.<br>It is also possible that the meat was from a guilt-offering, but sin-offerings are more common and hence, they were mentioned. The meat could also have come from a peace-offering - for such offerings are also cut up into smaller pieces - but out of respect to the stringencies associated with sin-offerings, it is considered in that category.</i> Pieces which are found in Jerusalem are considered as parts of peace-offerings.<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">Since peace-offerings may be eaten throughout Jerusalem, we can assume that meat found there was left over from such an offering.</i> Everything should be left until the following day and then taken out to the place where sacrifices are burnt lest it be <i>notar</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">Which is forbidden to be eaten or offered on the altar. Since it is possible that the meat was left beyond its appointed time, it must be burnt as required for such meat. Nevertheless, since it is also possible that it had been sacrificed on this day, it cannot be burnt immediately. Instead, we wait until the following day when it is certainly required to be burnt and burn it at that time. For peace-offerings, it is necessary to wait two days.</i><br>[One might ask: If so,] of what benefit will it be that it be considered as [part of] a burnt-offering, a sin-offering, or a peace-offering? [To define the law for one] who transgressed and partook of it.<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., since it is possible that the sacrificial meat had not been left for an extra day, if a person who is permitted to eat such a sacrifice partakes of it, he is not obligated to bring a guilt-offering to atone for misusing sacrificial meat.</i><br><i>Notar</i> is burnt only during the day, as stated: "On the third day,<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">Since the verse mentions the day, it must be burnt during those hours.</i> [it] shall be burnt with fire."
Although peace-offerings are forbidden to be eaten from the beginning of the night of the third day,<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 10:6.</i> [the remainder] is only burnt during the day, whether [it is burnt] at the appropriate time or not at the appropriate time.<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">I.e., even if it is discovered at night, several days after the meat should have been consumed, it should be burnt on the following day and not immediately at night.</i> Similarly, <i>piggul</i> is burnt only during the day.<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">The verse regarding <i>notar</i> serves as the basis for the ruling regarding all sacrifices that must be burnt.</i><br>Burning [sacrificial meat] that is impure, <i>notar</i>, or <i>piggul</i> does not supersede [the prohibitions against forbidden labor on] festivals.<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Pesachim</i> 7:10), the Rambam states the rationale: The prohibition of work on festivals is mandated by both a positive and negative commandment, while the charge to burn <i>notar</i> is merely a positive commandment and a positive commandment never overrides the observance of both a positive and negative commandment. See also <i>Hilchot Sh'vitat Yom Tov</i> 3:8.</i> Needless to say, it does not supersede [the prohibition against work on] the Sabbath.<br>It is permitted to burn [sacrificial meat] that is impure, <i>notar</i>, and <i>piggul</i> together.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Although it is forbidden to cause sacrificial meat to contract ritual impurity - and by mixing <i>notar</i> or <i>piggul</i> with impure meat, one would be doing so - since <i>notar</i> or <i>piggul</i> are already considered impure, this provision is granted (<i>Pesachim</i> 15b).</i>
When the meat of a sacrifice of the most sacred order became impure in [the Temple Courtyard], it should be burnt in [the Temple Courtyard]. When it became impure outside [the Temple Courtyard], it should be burnt outside [the Temple Courtyard].<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 7:3-4.</i> [This applies] whether it became impure because of a primary source of ritual impurity or a derivative of ritual impurity.<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">In the original, an <i>av tumah</i>, literally, "a father of impurity," and a <i>v'lad tumah</i>, literally, "the offspring of impurity." See Chapter 18, Halachah 12, for more details regarding these terms.</i><br>The priests never refrained from burning meat that contracted impurity from a primary source of impurity - and thus it is defined as impure to the first degree - with meat that contracted impurity from a derivative of impurity,<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The Rambam is borrowing the wording of the Mishnah (<i>Pesachim</i> 1:6), even though - as he states in his Commentary to the Mishnah - the intent is "a derivative of a derivative," i.e., an entity of third degree impurity as mentioned here. Thus we are speaking about meat that touched an entity that had touched an entity that had touched a primary source of impurity. Indeed, the <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> and others suggest that text of the <i>Mishneh Torah</i> should be emended to reflect that understanding.</i> even though this would increase the level of its impurity.<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">The meat becomes impure only according to Rabbinical decree. According to Scriptural Law, food does not cause other food to contract ritual impurity [<i>Hilchot Sha'ar Avot HaTuma'ah</i> 7:1; the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>op. cit.</i>)].</i> For [an entity that is] of third degree impurity that touches an entity of first degree impurity is considered as of secondary impurity, as explained in [the appropriate] place.<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Tuma'at Ochalin</i> 4:15.</i> Moreover, even oil that became impure because it touched a person who immersed on that day,<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">To emerge from most types of ritual impurity, a person must immerse in a <i>mikveh</i> and then wait until nightfall. Even if a person has already immersed in a <i>mikveh</i>, he does not regain impurity until night. Until that time, he can impart ritual impurity to certain entities (<i>Tivul Yom</i> 2:1).</i> which is of third degree impurity is permitted to be burnt in a metal lamp<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">But not an earthenware lamp touched by a person who became impure because of contact with a corpse, for an earthenware utensil never becomes a primary source of impurity [the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>op. cit.</i>)].</i> that was touched by a person who is impure because of contact with a human corpse, in which instance, the lamp is a primary source of impurity.<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">For the impurity resulting from contact with a corpse is so severe that even an entity that touches it becomes a primary source of impurity.</i> Although the oil becomes impure to the first degree when it touches the lamp, since it was already deemed impure, we are not concerned with the increase of the impurity. We are only careful that an entity that is pure will not become disqualified.
<i>Notar</i> left over from sacrifices of a lesser degree of holiness<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Which may be eaten anywhere in Jerusalem.</i> should be burnt by the persons bringing the sacrifice in their homes.<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">There is no necessity to bring it to the Temple and have it burnt there.</i>
[The following rules apply when a person] left Jerusalem and remembered that he had sacrificial meat<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Meat from sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity must be eaten in Jerusalem.</i> in his possession. If he already passed Mt. Scopus,<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">The last place from the surroundings of Jerusalem from which the Temple can be seen. See <i>Hilchot Beit HaBechirah</i> 7:8.</i> he should burn it where he is. If not<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">And thus the difficulty in returning is not so great.</i> and it is the size of an olive-sized portion, he should return and burn it in Jerusalem.<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">If, however, it is smaller, it is not significant and can be burnt wherever he is.</i> If he is a guest who does not have a home, he should burn it before the Temple<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">The Hebrew term <i>birah</i> is used to refer to the entire Temple complex.</i> with wood designated for the arrangement of wood [of the altar].<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">This provision was made lest guests refrain from burning the sacrificial meat because of a lack of wood. See also <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i> 4:3 which touches on related matters.</i>
All of the bones of the sacrifices that do not have marrow need not be burnt<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">It is sufficient to merely discard them.</i> with the exception of the bones of the Paschal sacrifice.<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">See <i>Hilchot Pesachim</i> 10:1-2 which explain that the bones of the Paschal sacrifice are burnt together with its meat, because according to the Rambam, the prohibition against breaking a bone from the Paschal sacrifice applies even after the mitzvah to partake of the sacrifice is concluded, it is therefore desirable to burn the bones so that they do not become a cause of transgression. The Ra'avad mentions, based on <i>Pesachim</i> 83a, it can be concluded that only bones that had marrow and which were cracked open and the marrow removed must be burnt. If they have no marrow at all, there is no need to burn even the bones of the Paschal sacrifice.<br>According to this view, the difference between the law governing the bones of the Paschal sacrifice and those of other sacrifices can be explained as follows. It is forbidden to break open the bones of the Paschal sacrifice. Therefore if the bones of a Paschal sacrifice were broken open, we can assume that this was done after the Paschal sacrifice became <i>notar</i>, for, according to many authorities, there is no prohibition against breaking the bones of a Paschal sacrifice once it has been disqualified. In such a situation, the bones are forbidden, because they served <i>notar</i> (i.e., the marrow). (See the gloss of the <i>Mishneh LiMelech</i> who notes that in <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i> 10:6, the Rambam writes that even in such a situation, it is forbidden to break the bones of a Paschal sacrifice, and offers a possible resolution.)<br>With regard to other sacrifices, by contrast, there is no prohibition against breaking their bones even during the time the sacrifice is acceptable. Hence we can assume that they were broken during that time and the marrow removed. Thus there is little likelihood that they served <i>notar</i> and thus became forbidden. According to this understanding, if a sacrifice was <i>notar</i>, any bone that contains marrow should be burnt. See the gloss of the Meiri to <i>Pesachim, loc. cit.</i>, who implies that the Rambam should have been more explicit in his statements.</i> We already explained<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:20.</i> that when a sacrifice was disqualified after it was skinned, its hide should be given to the priests<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">In the instance of sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity.</i> or to the owners, in the instance of sacrifices of a lesser degree of holiness. If, however, [a sacrifice] was disqualified before it was skinned, the hide is considered as the meat and it should be burnt in its entirety.<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">As the Mishnah (<i>Zevachim</i> 12:2) states: "Whenever the altar did not acquire the flesh [of a sacrifice], the owners do not acquire the hide."</i><br>Similarly, if a sacrifice was skinned and then it was discovered to be <i>tereifah</i> or it was disqualified because of an improper thought concerning time or place, since the sacrifice was not accepted, the hide should be burnt. [This applies] both to sacrifices of the highest degree of sanctity and to sacrifices of a lesser degree of sanctity. If, however, a sacrifice was offered for the sake of a different intent, even though the obligation of the owners was not fulfilled, since it is acceptable,<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 15, Halachah 1.</i> the hide is given to the priests or the owners, as explained [above]. When a sacrifice was skinned before the blood was cast [on the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote">This is a violation of the norms of sacrificial practice (see <i>Hilchot Ma'aseh HaKorbanot</i> 5:18). Nevertheless, it does not disqualify a sacrifice.</i> and the sacrifice was disqualified afterwards, the hide] is not disqualified.
These are the entities that should be burnt:<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to benefit from these entities. They should be burnt so that they are destroyed entirely.</i> sacrificial meat that became impure, <i>notar</i>, or was disqualified, and also a meal-offering that became impure, <i>notar,</i> or was disqualified, a conditional guilt-offering in an instance when it became known to the transgressor that he definitely did not sin before its blood was cast [on the altar],<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">A conditional guilt-offering is brought when a person suspects he has violated a negative commandment, but has no definite knowledge that he did so. If he receives knowledge that he is guiltless after the animal has been slaughtered, but before its blood is cast on the altar, the sacrifice is disqualified. Once its blood has been cast on the altar, the sacrifice is acceptable even if the person receives definite knowledge that he is guiltless. See Chapter 4, Halachah 19.</i> a sin-offering of fowl that is brought because of a doubt,<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">See Chapter 7, Halachah 10.</i> the hair of a nazirite who is ritually pure,<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">A nazirite's hair is considered "holy" and it is forbidden to be benefit from it. Therefore at the conclusion of his nazirite vow, he shaves his head and burns his hair in the Chamber of the Nazirites that was in the southeastern corner of the Women's Courtyard (<i>Hilchot Nizirut</i> 8:1-3).</i> and [produce that is] <i>orlah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">Produce that grows in the first three years after the planting of a tree. See <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i>, ch. 10, and <i>Hilchot Ma'aser Sheni</i>, ch. 10.</i> or <i>kilei hakerem</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">Species of grain or vegetables sown in a vineyard. See <i>Hilchot Kilayim</i>, ch. 5.</i> Entities that are not fit to be burnt - e.g., liquids that are <i>orlah</i> or <i>kilei hakerem</i> - should be buried.
These are the entities that should be buried: sacred animals that died, whether they were consecrated to [be offered on] the altar or for the sake of the Temple treasury - when sacred animals miscarry and discharge a fetus or a placenta, it should be buried - an ox that is stoned to death,<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">An ox - or any other animal - that killed a person. The ox is stoned to death and it is forbidden to benefit from its flesh at all (Exodus 21:29-32; <i>Hilchot Nizkei Mammon</i>, ch. 10).</i> a calf whose neck is broken,<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">When a wayfarer is found murdered and it is not known who killed him, a calf is brought as atonement. See Deuteronomy, ch. 21; <i>Hilchot Rotzeach</i>, ch. 9.</i> the fowl [used for the purification of] a person afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i>,<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in Leviticus, ch. 14, <i>Hilchot Tuma'at Tzara'at</i>, ch. 11, when a person whose body had been afflicted with <i>tzara'at</i> becomes pure, he must bring two birds as part of the purification ritual.</i> the hair of a nazirite who became impure,<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">As stated in <i>Hilchot Nizirut</i> 6:11, when a nazirite becomes impure because of contact with a human corpse, he must have [the ashes of the Red Heifer] sprinkled upon him on the third and seventh days. He then has his hair shaved on the seventh day. This shaving need not be performed in the Temple Courtyard.</i> a firstborn donkey [which was not redeemed],<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">The firstborn male offspring of a donkey must be redeemed for a sheep. If it is not redeemed, it is executed and it is forbidden to benefit from its flesh (Exodus 13:13, <i>Hilchot Bikkurim</i>, ch. 12).</i> a mixture of milk and meat,<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Which is forbidden not only to be eaten but also to derive benefit from (Exodus 23:19; <i>Hilchot Ma'achalot Assurot</i> 9:1).</i> and ordinary animals that were slaughtered in the Temple Courtyard.<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to benefit from the meat of such animals, as stated in <i>Hilchot Shechitah</i> 2:2.</i>
When a person weaves the full length of a <i>sit</i><sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Orlah</i> 3:2), the Rambam translates the term <i>sit</i> into Arabic. Most commentaries interpret his statements as meaning "the distance between the top of the thumb and the next finger [when the fingers are spread out]. This is one-sixth of the distance between the thumb and the middle finger." Rav Kappach notes that in fact such a calculation will not be accurate. He interprets the Rambam's words as defining a <i>sit</i> as half the distance between the index finger and the middle finger when spread out. This he maintains is two thumbreadths.<br>In his Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>Shabbat</i> 13:4), the Rambam differentiates between "the width of a <i>sit</i>" and "the full length of a <i>sit</i>." As indicated by <i>Hilchot Shabbat</i> 9:20, "the full length of a <i>sit</i>" is two thumbreadths. In contrast, as stated (<i>ibid.</i>:7), "the width of a <i>sit</i>" is two thirds of a <i>zeret</i>, i.e., three thumbbreadths.</i> from the hair of a nazirite or a firstborn donkey with a weave of goatshair,<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Our translation is based on the Rambam's Commentary to the Mishnah (<i>loc. cit.</i>:3).</i> it should be consigned to flames.<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">Generally, the hair of an unredeemed firstborn donkey need only be buried, as stated in the previous halachah. Nevertheless, in this instance, the cloth must be burnt lest the forbidden substance not be recognized and the cloth considered as permitted (<i>Temurah</i> 34a).</i>
[If] any of the entities that must be buried [are burnt], it is forbidden to benefit from their ashes. It is permitted to benefit from the ashes of all of the entities that must be burnt, [even if] they are sacred, with the exception of the ashes of the outer and inner altars and the ashes of the <i>Menorah</i>.<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Temurah, op. cit.</i>, derives this concept from the exegesis of the statements of Leviticus 25:3 with regard to the ash of the inner altar. From those statements, a parallel is established with regard to the other ashes mentioned here.<br>The Ra'avad takes issue with the Rambam and maintains that it is only forbidden to benefit from the ashes which the priest removes when taking out the ash in the morning. He maintains that the ash on the altar is permitted. The Rambam, by contrast, maintains that all of the ash of the altar is forbidden.</i>
None of the entities to be burnt should be buried<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">Because in all these instances, the mitzvah is that the article be burnt.</i> and none of the entities to be buried should be burnt. [The rationale for the latter point is that] even though he is stringent by burning it, he is being lenient with regard to its ash, for the ashes of the entities that are buried are forbidden.<sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">While the ash of entities that are to be burnt is permitted.</i>
If a person was offering sacrifices together with [a priest] and he told him: "[The sacrifices became] <i>piggul</i>," or if he was involved with entities that are ritually pure with a person and he told him, "They became impure," his word is accepted.<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">The Ra'avad states that, on the basis of <i>Gittin</i> 54b, both of these statements should be understood as applying while the article in question is in that person's hands. The rationale is that since he could now make the article <i>piggul</i> or impure, his word is accepted when he says that it was previously brought to that state.<br>The <i>Kessef Mishneh</i> states that although that is the opinion of Abbaye in <i>Gittin, loc. cit.</i>, Ravva differs, maintaining that the law applies even when the articles in question are not in his hand. Generally, the halachah follows Ravva's opinion, but in this instance, Abbaye's view is favored.</i> A Jew is not suspected of lying in such an instance.<sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">For this would cause acceptable sacrifices to be burnt unnecessary, and articles and pure entities to be destroyed.</i> If, by contrast, he told him: "The sacrifices which I offered for you on this and this day became <i>piggul</i>" or "those pure objects became impure," [different rules apply]. If [the person is one] whom he trusts, he should rely on his word. If not, according to the letter of the law, his word [need] not be relied upon. One who wishes to be stringent<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">And accept the other person's statements.</i> is praiseworthy.<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">The wording of the Talmud that the Rambam quotes, "according to the letter of the law, his word [need] not be relied upon," implies that it is desirable to go beyond the letter of the law. See the parallels in <i>Hilchot Korban Pesach</i> 4:1; <i>Hilchot Mitamei Mishkav UMoshav</i> 13:8.</i>
